# The Wrong Mouth

# Chapter 1

The farmer had been dead two days by the time they sent for Maren, and the house smelled like it.

Not the body. Orla Dunnock had packed her husband in salt and dried lavender the way her mother had taught her, and Haff Dunnock lay on the kitchen table looking more like cured meat than a man who'd collapsed in his own barley field. The smell was the other thing. The sweet-ozone reek that crept in through the walls after someone died near dungeon territory, the contamination smell that meant residue was pooling somewhere close, settling into the dirt and the well water and the grain stores. Maren had smelled it in a dozen farmhouses across the Fenhold flats. It was his cue to arrive, look grave, and collect his fee.

He set the ceramic bowl on the table beside Haff's left hand. Close to the body but not touching, near enough that the family could see it mattered. The bowl was eleven years old, purchased from a market stall in Fenhold for four coppers when Maren was seventeen and had just decided that pretending to eat sins was easier than actually working. It had no System properties. It had no properties at all, besides being watertight and small enough to carry in a satchel without clinking against the candle holder. He'd chipped the rim twice and glued it back both times with pine resin, not because the bowl mattered but because the family needed to see a sin-eater who cared for his tools. The glue had darkened with age and looked, if you squinted, almost like a deliberate marking.

"Orla," he said. "I'm going to need quiet for a bit."

She nodded. She was standing in the doorway with her arms crossed over her chest, not from coldness but from the way people held themselves when they didn't know what to do with their hands. Her son Tam — fourteen, maybe, with his father's heavy jaw and his mother's set mouth — stood behind her, trying to look like he wasn't watching. He was watching.

Maren lit the candle. Beeswax, not tallow — he'd learned early that the wax mattered to people. Tallow smelled like the rendering they did every autumn, and nobody wanted rendering-smell mixed with their father's last rites. The beeswax cost him two coppers out of his twelve-copper fee, but the investment paid for itself in trust. The flame held steady in the still air, throwing the room into gold and harder shadow, making Haff's salt-packed face look almost dignified.

The hum was there, as it always was. Low, constant, like something large and electrical sitting just past the edge of hearing. Maren had heard it his whole life. He'd asked Derry once what the hum sounded like to someone who could see the System, and Derry had said, "What hum?" That told Maren everything he needed to know about being the only person in the world who noticed the background noise.

He placed his hands on the rim of the bowl. Pressed down until his knuckles whitened. This was the first posture — supplication, the sin-eater opening himself to receive. He'd copied it from a woodcut in a book he'd stolen from a traveling merchant when he was sixteen, and he still wasn't sure whether the woodcut was accurate or artistic license. It didn't matter. Orla and Tam had never seen a real sin-eater work. Neither had anyone else in Fenhold.

Maren closed his eyes and began the murmuring. Low, rhythmic, a not-quite-language that sounded devotional if you didn't listen too closely. He'd invented it. The first few years he'd been terrified someone would recognize that the words were nonsense — fragments of a lullaby his mother had sung, mixed with numbers and the names of rivers he'd crossed as a boy. But nobody had ever stopped him. Nobody had ever said, *that's not how the ritual goes.* Because nobody in Fenhold knew how the ritual went. Sin-eating was like plumbing: everyone agreed it needed doing, nobody wanted to watch it happen, and the only qualification the job really required was showing up.

He murmured. He pressed his hands against the bowl. He let his face do the work — the slight downward pull of the mouth, the furrowed brow, the quality of concentration that told Orla and her son something difficult and holy was happening in the space between the bowl and the body.

He watched them through his lashes. This was the part that mattered.

Orla's shoulders dropped an inch. Her breathing slowed. Tam shifted his weight from his left foot to his right, which meant he was relaxing into the performance rather than studying it. Maren catalogued the changes the way a carpenter reads grain — automatically, with professional interest, noting where the pressure sat and where it didn't. In another year, Tam would be the man of this house. He'd be the one to send for the sin-eater when a neighbor dropped. If Tam believed today, he'd believe then, and that was another twelve coppers every time death came to the Dunnock family's corner of the flats.

The contamination smell was stronger near the body. Sweet and sharp, like overripe fruit left in the sun with a metallic undertone that clung to the tongue. Maren had been tasting it for eleven years, in every farmhouse and field shed and dirt-floor chapel where he'd knelt beside the dead and pretended to eat what killed them. The taste never changed. Nothing ever happened when he performed the ritual. He'd stopped expecting it to, the way a man who knocks on the same locked door every morning eventually stops expecting anyone to open it but keeps knocking because the knocking itself has become the job.

He shifted to the second posture — reception. Hands lifted from the bowl, palms up, fingers spread. The stolen book had shown the sin-eater's hands in this position with lines of light flowing into them, which Maren had always assumed was embellishment. Quest-light pooled outside dungeon mouths like pale standing water, and sometimes a faint shimmer hung in the air after someone died — a residue, like heat-haze with color — but nothing had ever flowed into his hands. His hands were just hands.

He held the posture. Counted to forty in his head. Forty was the number he'd settled on years ago — long enough to look effortful, short enough that the family didn't start fidgeting. At thirty-two, he usually began the closing murmur, the rise in pitch that signaled the ritual was ending, that the sins had been eaten and the contamination cleared and the dead could rest.

He got to fourteen.

Something answered.

Not from the body. Not from outside the farmhouse. From a place Maren didn't have a word for — behind his sternum, or underneath his thoughts, or in the space where the hum had always lived. A pressure, sudden and enormous, like a fist closing around something he hadn't known was there. His hands locked. His fingers went rigid, still spread in the reception posture but no longer under his control. The air in his lungs stopped moving.

Then the paladin hit him.

Not from outside — there was no impact, no blow. It was an implosion. Everything Aldric had been — fourteen years of combat drills, the specific way to shift weight before a sword stroke, the prayers he'd said at dawn for a decade, the face of a woman he'd loved and never told, the smell of stone corridors in the Order's chapter house, the grief and the grief and the grief of a man who had died serving something he believed in — all of it crashed into Maren's skull like someone had kicked open a door to a room that had always been sealed and now everything was falling on him at once.

His spine arched. His hands snapped closed into fists he hadn't made. Something in his shoulders knew how to throw a punch and was trying to, right there at the kitchen table, Haff Dunnock's body three inches from his elbow. His right arm cocked back. His weight dropped to the balls of his feet in a fighting stance that belonged to a man he'd never met.

Orla said something. The words didn't reach him.

His legs moved wrong — not his legs, someone else's muscle memory trying to inhabit the same limbs, a body that knew how to pivot and advance and drop its center of gravity for a shield-brace Maren had never learned and could not stop performing. His knee hit the table leg. The candle toppled. Beeswax spattered across Haff's salt-crusted shirt, and the thought that surfaced was absurd and entirely his: *that cost me two coppers.*

Then the voice.

It came from inside him — not through his ears, not arriving from any direction. It occupied the same space as his own thoughts but was clearly, violently not his. A voice like a man reading from a scroll. Formal, clipped, absolutely certain of its own authority.

*Kneel.*

Maren's knees tried to buckle. Not from weakness — from obedience. The paladin's body had known how to kneel in prayer the way Maren's body knew how to smile at a mark, and the kneeling was trying to happen through his legs without his consent.

*Kneel and pray. Now. We are in the presence of the dead and you will show respect.*

He braced against the table. His fingernails scraped salt from the wood. Orla was backing toward the doorway, pulling Tam with her, and even through the chaos of his own muscles betraying him, Maren saw her face — not fear exactly, but the alarm of something that was supposed to be safe becoming unpredictable. She'd trusted him. Twelve coppers of trust, beeswax-scented and professional, and the sin-eater she'd hired was thrashing against her kitchen table with his eyes wet and his arms doing things arms shouldn't do.

*Kneel. I will not ask again.*

"Get — off —" The words came out choked and wrong because his throat was doing something new, trying to shape words in a register too low for his vocal cords, the paladin's baritone fighting his tenor for the same mouth.

The memories kept coming. A sword pommel in cold rain. The weight of plate armor on shoulders that had carried it so long the body didn't know how to stand without it. A young man, maybe twenty, taking his first oath in a chapel where quest-light pooled in stone fonts like colored water — and the oaths meant something, every word, and the man saying them believed with the specific ferocity of someone who had never yet been disappointed by the thing he served.

Maren's eyes were wet and he didn't know whose grief it was.

He stumbled backward. His hip caught the chair. He went down, one hand on the floor, the bowl still on the table — he'd placed it with a con man's instinct for keeping props out of the violence, pushed back against the wall, away from the thrashing — and his stomach finally revolted.

He made it outside. Barely. The farmhouse door was four steps from the table and he covered them in three, hand on the frame, the late-summer sun hitting his face like an accusation. The ditch beside the path was dry, weedy, humming with something small and persistent in the grass. He fell to his knees — not prayer, just gravity — and threw up.

It was not a dignified thing. It was loud and convulsive and it tasted like the contamination, ozone and rot, as though his body was trying to expel whatever had entered through the only method it understood. His hands pressed into the dirt. Small stones bit into his palms. His stomach clenched again and he spat bile into the dead grass, strings of it hanging from his lip while his back heaved and the muscles along his ribs spasmed in patterns that weren't his — the aftershock of combat training forcing itself into a body that had never been drilled.

The hum was louder now, much louder, and beneath it the paladin's voice, still insisting, still certain:

*This is a desecration. You have defiled the ritual. You must — you must account for this.*

"I'm throwing up," Maren said into the weeds. "In a ditch. Give me a minute."

*You were not performing the ritual correctly. You were not performing it at all. You were — what were you doing?*

"My job." He spat. The taste didn't go away. "What are you?"

*I am Aldric of the Order of the Veil. I am a knight-paladin of the Fourth Seal. And I appear to be dead. And I appear to be inside — what is your classification?*

"My what?"

*Your System classification. Your class. Your level. Your — you are not a sin-eater. The ritual was wrong. Every element of it was wrong. What are you?*

Maren pressed his forehead into the dirt. It was warm from the sun and gritty against his skin. Behind him, through the farmhouse door, Orla was talking fast and low to Tam, the way people talked when they were deciding whether to run or whether to bar the door.

"I'm a fraud," he said, because it was easier than lying right now and because the truth had a kind of weight to it when you were on your knees in a ditch with bile on your chin. "I've been faking this for eleven years. Nothing ever answered. You — you shouldn't be here."

The silence that followed was not the silence of someone thinking. It was the silence of someone reassessing every assumption he'd made in the last thirty seconds and finding none of them intact.

*You are not a sin-eater.*

"No."

*You have been performing false rituals over the dead for eleven years.*

"Yes."

*In villages. Over people who trusted you.*

"Yes."

*And the contamination — every ritual you have performed — it was never cleared. It is still there. In every village, every field, every —*

"I know."

Another silence. Then, in a voice that had lost its formal clipping and become something rawer, something closer to a man than a title:

*What kind of person does this?*

Maren spat again. His stomach was done but his hands were shaking, a tremor that started in his wrists and ran up to his elbows, and he couldn't tell whether it was the vomiting or the paladin's outrage or his own muscles trying to sort out which movement patterns actually belonged to him.

"The kind who's lying in a ditch," he said. "That's what I've got for you right now."

***

He didn't go back into the farmhouse. Orla brought the bowl out — held it at arm's length, like something that might bite — and set it on the path beside the ditch without speaking. She didn't ask for the twelve coppers. She didn't ask anything. She went back inside and the bar slid into place, and that was fair enough.

Maren sat on the edge of the ditch with the bowl in his lap, turning it in his hands. The chip on the rim where he'd dropped it three years ago in Kenning was smooth under his thumb. The pine resin had darkened with handling. Eleven years of fake rituals, and the bowl was the most honest thing about the whole operation — it had never pretended to be anything other than a piece of fired clay.

The sun was lower. He'd been in the ditch longer than he'd thought. Shadows stretched from the farmhouse across the stubbled barley field, and the hum had settled from its earlier spike into something thick and constant, like a headache that had decided where to live.

*Your pulse is elevated. Your hydration is insufficient. You have lost approximately —*

"Stop."

*I am providing a medical assessment.*

"I don't want a medical assessment."

*You have vomited extensively. You are sitting in a ditch. You are, by your own admission, a fraud who has allowed contamination to accumulate in populated areas for over a decade. The least you can accept is a medical assessment.*

The voice had settled. It was still inside him, still occupying that impossible space behind his thoughts, but it had gone from raw and panicked to cold, the way a man who'd been shouting might suddenly drop to a quiet, clipped tone because shouting hadn't worked. Maren knew the shift. He'd used it himself, on marks who needed a different register of pressure.

"All right," he said. "Give me the medical assessment."

*Your System profile reads as follows —*

"I don't have a System profile."

*Incorrect. You have a profile. It is unusual. It reads as — it reads as empty. Not absent. Empty. There is a classification field and it contains nothing. There is a level field and it contains nothing. There are skill allocations and they —*

"They contain nothing. Yeah." He rubbed the grit from his forehead. "I'm System-blind. I've been blind my whole life. I can't see the interface. I can't see the overlays or the skill descriptions or the threat levels. None of it. I've never seen any of it."

The quality of silence changed. Maren had been reading silences professionally for a decade — the silence of a widow deciding whether to pay, the silence of a village elder deciding whether to believe, the silence of Derry when Maren told him the take was less than it actually was — and this one carried something he hadn't heard from the paladin before. Not outrage. Not disgust. Something more disoriented, like a man reading a map who'd just discovered it was upside down.

*You are System-blind.*

"That's what I said."

*And you have been performing the sin-eating ritual — you have been performing it for eleven years — without seeing the interface.*

"Without seeing anything. Yeah."

*Without seeing the contamination patterns, the residue concentrations, the — how? How have you been doing this?*

"I watch people." He rubbed his eyes. His fingers smelled like dirt and stomach acid. "I watch what they do when the System prompts them. When someone's threat indicator changes, they flinch — it's subtle, but they all do it. When a quest notification comes through, people's eyes track something I can't see. I learned to read the room instead of the screen. And Derry — he's my partner, my — he feeds me the details I can't get. The numbers, the levels, whatever I need to sound like I know what I'm talking about."

*You have been reading a room for eleven years instead of doing the actual work.*

"Yeah, well. Nobody complained."

*Nobody complained because nobody knew the contamination was not being cleared. They trusted you. They —*

"I know what they did."

His voice came out harder than he'd intended. His hands had stopped shaking, which was something, but his shoulders were knotted and there was a new kind of tension in his back — the paladin's posture trying to assert itself, a spine that wanted to be straighter than Maren had ever held his own.

The air smelled like contamination and beeswax smoke and the specific vegetable rot of a ditch in late summer. A fat black fly was working the inside of the bowl. Maren flicked it away.

*I am going to read you your current interface status. In full.*

"I don't —"

*You need to hear this. You have needed to hear this for twenty-eight years, and I will not sit inside the skull of an uninformed man. I find the prospect morally untenable.*

Maren almost laughed. Morally untenable. The dead paladin in his head found the arrangement morally untenable. The arrangement where Maren was sitting in a ditch covered in his own sick, having just lost the only steady work he'd ever had, Orla Dunnock's door barred against him and his reputation in this corner of the flats probably finished — *that* was the part the paladin found untenable.

"Fine," he said. "Read me the interface."

*System designation: Unregistered. No class assigned. No level assigned. Skill allocations: none. Active quest involvement: none. Passive quest contamination exposure: significant — I will return to that item. Local threat assessment: ambient. There are three active quests within a two-mile radius of your current position. The nearest is a contamination-clear request, priority low, assigned to a registered sin-eater operating in the Fenhold flats.*

The paladin paused.

*That would be you. Except you are not registered. And not a sin-eater. And the contamination has never been cleared. The accumulation log shows eleven years of uncollected residue in the surrounding agricultural district. Estimated affected area encompasses the entire Fenhold flats. Contamination density in several zones exceeds safe habitation thresholds.*

The voice did something it hadn't done before. It slowed, and the slowing was not emphasis but something involuntary — the cadence of a man who was reading and processing the data at the same time and not liking what the processing returned.

*There are children living in this.*

Maren said nothing. The fly came back to the bowl.

*Continuing. Nearby entity profiles. Orla Dunnock: farmer, level four, no combat classification, threat assessment negligible. Tam Dunnock: unclassed, juvenile, pre-assignment. The deceased —*

A longer pause.

*Haff Dunnock, farmer, level six, non-combat. Cause of death listed by the System as environmental exposure, subcategory: contamination-related organ failure. Long-term.*

Maren's hands stopped moving on the bowl.

*The farmer died of contamination exposure. Accumulated. Over years. In the agricultural district where you have been performing false rituals for —*

"I heard you."

*Then you understand that his death —*

"I said I heard you."

He stared at the chip in the rim. The pine resin. The fired clay underneath, grey-brown, ordinary. He'd bought it the same week he'd done his first fake ritual, over the body of a woman named Grella whose family had given him milk and bread afterward because they thought he'd done something holy. He could still taste that bread, the heavy grain of it, the warm crust.

Silence. Then the paladin continued, and the formal cadence had reconstituted itself, stiff and precise, the way a man who has nearly broken composes himself by retreating to the language of reports.

*Equipment loadout. One ceramic bowl, no System classification, no System value. One candle holder, iron, no System classification, no System value. One leather satchel, no System classification, no System value. Total combat readiness: nonexistent. Total threat profile to local entities: negligible.*

Another pause, shorter.

*Total threat profile to the System: I am unable to evaluate. Your ambient signature — the impression the System reads when it encounters your presence — is unlike anything in my reference frame. I had access to Order of the Veil records for fourteen years, comprehensive records, and nothing in those archives describes what your profile looks like from this vantage. You are not merely unregistered. You are something the System does not have a category for. And it has a category for everything.*

A bird called from somewhere behind the farmhouse. The sound was two short notes rising, and after them a silence that felt too large.

"So what does that mean?" Maren said. "For me. Practically."

*Practically. It means you are the most confusing thing I have encountered in fourteen years of service, and I once encountered a dungeon that had organized its threat creatures alphabetically.* A beat. *It means your System profile is a gap. A hole. And holes in the System are not natural and they are not ignored. It means I am dead and I am inside a gap, and I — I have no idea what happens next.*

The formal cadence wobbled. Just for a moment, under the military syntax and the moral certainty, the voice of a man who had been dead for however long he'd been dead and was registering what that meant — not just dead, but dead and gone from everything he'd been, the Order and the chapel and the dawn prayers and the woman whose face Maren had caught in the flood of memories, gone from all of it and put instead inside the skull of a man who faked miracles for twelve coppers, with no body and no way to kneel and no way out.

Then the cadence came back. Stiff. Deliberate.

*Continuing the interface reading. Your current stamina allocation: none. Your mana reserve: none. Resistance profiles: none. Speed modifier: base human, unenhanced. Perception modifier: base human, unenhanced, with the noted System-blindness anomaly. Strength modifier —*

"Base human. Yeah. I get it."

*I am being thorough.*

"You're reading me a list of all the ways I'm nothing."

*I am reading you the System interface. These are the contents. If they happen to constitute a comprehensive inventory of your inadequacies, that is a property of accurate data, not editorial commentary on my part.*

Maren pressed his thumb into the chip on the bowl. The resin was warm from his hands.

"You know what," he said. "Keep going."

*Your current position relative to the nearest dungeon mouth: one point three miles, bearing northeast. Contamination concentration along that vector: heavy, distributed across agricultural parcels, concentrating in low-lying terrain and water collection points. Number of occupied farmsteads within the contamination zone: fourteen. Estimated population of those farmsteads: sixty to seventy people, including approximately twenty children under the age of —*

"I said keep going. I didn't say kill me with it."

*I am reading the interface.*

"You're reading it like a man who's figured out it's the only weapon he's got."

Silence. When the voice came back, something in it had shifted — still cold, still formal, but the edge had changed from prosecutorial to something else, something Maren couldn't read yet because he'd only known this voice for an hour and hadn't built the map.

*That is not entirely inaccurate.*

Maren leaned back against the weedy bank of the ditch. The sky was enormous above him, late-summer blue going to copper at the edges where the sun was deciding whether to set. His body was mostly his again. The combat reflexes had quieted. His spine still wanted to be straighter than he'd ever held it, and there was a residual stiffness in his shoulders that felt like someone else's military posture trying to settle into civilian use. But his hands were his. His legs were his. His stomach was empty and sore and entirely his own.

*One additional item.*

"Go ahead."

*I can see the contamination. Not the data — the actual contamination, the residue itself. From this vantage, from inside you, looking through whatever this arrangement is, it reads differently than it did when I was alive. It is not merely data overlays. It has physical weight. Texture. Something close to color but not precisely color. The barley field there is saturated. The soil under your hands is saturated. The air around the farmhouse is — the contamination you were hired to clear, in this field and every other field you have visited for eleven years, is not a number on a screen. It is a physical presence in the ground where people grow their food and draw their water and raise their children.*

"Yeah," Maren said. "I know."

But he didn't, really. He'd always known, in the abstract, that the contamination was there. He could smell it. He could see the faint shimmer at the edges of his vision when the light was low. He'd just never had someone describe it to him with the measured fury of a man who had spent his life believing that every job existed because the world needed it done.

He sat there for a while. The bird called again. The fly found the bowl again. The paladin was quiet, but it wasn't the quiet of absence — it was the quiet of a man who was choosing, for the moment, not to speak, because he was afraid that the next thing he said would break something that couldn't be fixed.

Maren held the bowl. The ceramic was blood-warm from his hands. Eleven years of fake rituals, and the first real thing that had ever happened during one was a dead man reading him, in specific and itemized detail, the exact dimensions of the damage.

He flicked the fly away.

"I'm Maren," he said. "Since we're — since you're going to be in here. I'm Maren. Twenty-eight. From Fenhold, originally. I've been faking the sin-eating since I was seventeen. I can't see the System. I can't see anything the System does. And I don't know what you are or why you're here, but I'm not going to pray. I should tell you that now."

*I gathered.*

"And I'm going to need the bowl. Whatever happens next — I don't know what happens next — but I'll need the bowl."

*The bowl has no System properties whatsoever.*

"I know. I still need it."

Silence. Not hostile. There was nothing warm available between them, and wouldn't be — that was already clear — but the temperature had dropped from active fury to something closer to the resigned irritation of two people stuck in the same room during a storm, each offended by the other's presence but unable to leave. The paladin was inside Maren's skull the way a sound lived in a bell. Whatever had put him there wasn't offering a way back out.

*Then keep the bowl,* the paladin said. *And tomorrow you will explain to me in full what you have been doing in these villages. Every village. Every ritual. Every family who paid you for a service you did not perform. I want a complete accounting.*

"Tomorrow."

*Tomorrow. I have — I have read enough for today.*

Something in the repetition. In the way *I have read enough* landed — not as moral exhaustion but as something plainer, closer to the bone. The paladin had been reading the interface the way a man performs triage, cataloguing the damage because the cataloguing was the only tool available, and now the tool was spent. He was dead. He was in a ditch. His Order was somewhere behind him and his body was gone and his prayers would go to no one because he had no knees to kneel on and no hands to fold, and the mouth that could have spoken them belonged to someone who had just admitted to eleven years of spiritual fraud.

Maren didn't say anything to this. Sincerity wasn't a skill he'd practiced. He knew what it looked like — he'd watched it in other people for decades, the way their faces opened and their voices dropped and their hands went still — but performing it and feeling it were different operations, and he had only ever been trained in the first.

The sun set behind the barley field. The contamination smell didn't fade — it never faded — but the light changed the way it sat in the air, the faint shimmer of residue going from invisible in direct sun to something you could almost see at the edges, like heat-haze with a tint, pooling in the low places between the furrows.

Maren sat in the ditch with a dead paladin in his head and a chipped ceramic bowl in his lap and the taste of bile still on his teeth, and the thought that came was not large or dramatic or worthy of the moment. It was small, and practical, and it was his:

He was going to need water. And he was going to need to figure out what to tell Derry. And he was going to need to not be in this ditch when morning came, because Orla Dunnock would open that door eventually, and when she did, the look on her face would be worse than anything the paladin had read to him from the interface.

The hum went on. It always had.


# Chapter 2

Maren woke to a voice cataloguing the structural deficiencies of his ceiling.

*The primary beam has a lateral crack approximately two hand-spans in length. I would estimate six months before failure under current load. The thatch needs replacement along the southern face — there is daylight visible through the weave, which suggests water ingress during rain. There is also a bird's nest above the door frame. Swallow, I believe, though my ornithological training was limited.*

"Shut up," Maren said to his ceiling.

*Additionally, you sleep with your mouth open. I did not need to know that about you.*

The morning light came through the gaps in the thatch in thin bars, full of dust. Late summer light — the kind that promised heat by midday and kept its promise. Maren lay on his straw mattress and tried to piece together the walk home. The ditch. His vomit in the ditch. The Garsen family watching from the farmhouse door while he staggered upright and wiped his mouth and told them everything was fine, just the spiritual exertion, very normal, nothing to worry about. The long walk back to Fenhold with his hands shaking and his ceramic bowl tucked under his arm and a dead man explaining, in the patient tone of a quartermaster discovering weevils in the flour, exactly how many years of System data Maren had been living without.

Twenty-eight years. His entire life. Every notification, every quest prompt, every threat marker and skill update and ambient data stream that the rest of the world swam through from birth like fish in water — all of it, gone. Not dimmed or degraded but absent, as if someone had built a house and forgotten to install the windows.

He sat up. His body ached in places that made no sense — shoulders, the base of his skull, the tendons along both forearms. As if he'd been hauling stone for hours, though the heaviest thing he'd carried yesterday was a bowl that weighed less than a loaf of bread.

*The muscular soreness is consistent with fragment integration. My combat training is embedding into your motor pathways. You will find you have reflexes you did not possess yesterday. I would recommend a period of controlled physical activity to—*

"I'm going to wash my face," Maren said. "You don't need to narrate that."

*I am not narrating. I am providing operational context. The distinction is—*

"It's the same thing."

*It is not the same thing.*

Maren swung his legs off the mattress. The packed-dirt floor was cool under his bare feet — the clay walls held the night's coolness the way a cellar held preserves. His dwelling was one room. A sleeping pallet, a table he'd built himself seven years ago that listed slightly to the left and had never stopped listing, a shelf where the bowl sat next to the candles and the dried herb bundles he used as props. Everything in this room was a stage set. The ritual ash in its jar — ordinary fireplace ash, scooped from Derry's hearth. The binding cord on its hook — purchased from a rope vendor in the market and soaked in juniper water because it made the house smell like a practitioner's home. Eleven years of this. Eleven years of building a room that told a specific lie to anyone who stepped inside it.

He found the water bucket by the door. Still half full. He splashed his face, rubbed his eyes, pushed his wet fingers through his hair. The water smelled faintly of iron from the well.

*There is a System notification in your ambient field. Shall I read it?*

Maren stood there with water dripping from his jaw. Twenty-eight years of silence where the System should have been, and now there was a voice offering to fill it like a clerk reading appointments to a man who'd been asleep his whole life.

"No."

*It is a regional contamination advisory. Level two. The Garsen farmstead is flagged for residual quest activity — which, given that you performed a sham ritual over the body yesterday and the actual clearing was accomplished entirely by my arrival, is a detail that should concern you more than—*

"I said no."

Silence. Approximately four seconds' worth.

*Your skill tree is remarkable.*

Maren pulled on his shirt — the same one from yesterday, stiff with dried sweat along the collar. He owned two. The other was drying on the line outside and probably stiff with yesterday's dried sweat too. "If this is another—"

*I am reading your System profile. Or rather, the space where your System profile should be. You are registered as a sin-eater. Class designation: active. Level seven. Specialization: consumption. These are the public entries visible to any System user who looks at you. Behind them, there is nothing. No progression data. No consumption log. No fragment integration history. No capacity scores. The public entries are a painted wall. Behind the paint, there is — not emptiness. Absence. As if the System attempted to write on you and the surface would not take.*

Maren stopped with one arm through a sleeve. The light caught the dust floating between his body and the far wall, motes turning slow in the morning warmth.

"How do you know what my profile looks like to other people?"

*I can see both layers. The System presents a public face and maintains an architecture behind it. Most people see only the face. My order trained us to read deeper. Veil-sigil holders were — are —* A pause. The kind that held something heavy. *We were trained in architectural reading. Interface diagnostics. Your public profile says sin-eater. Your architecture says nothing. Not blank. Nothing. As if the space where the System should record your data is not a gap but a refusal.*

An image pushed into Maren's mind — not his own, but from a place behind his own thoughts. A silver sigil on dark armor, the shape of an eye half-veiled. The weight of it against a chest that wasn't his. The cool metal through a linen undertunic, the way it shifted when you moved your sword arm. The pride and the responsibility and the specific, quiet satisfaction of wearing a symbol that meant you were trusted with things most people didn't know existed.

Maren shook it loose. The memory faded like heat off a stone, slow and residual.

*There is something else. Your consciousness does not interact with the System in the manner I would expect from documented System-blindness. The Order kept records of the condition — rare, perhaps one in a hundred thousand births. In those cases, the profiles present as degraded. Partial rendering. Reduced functionality. The System equivalent of damaged sight. Yours is not degraded. Yours is — I do not have the appropriate term. The System is not failing to reach you. It appears unable to reach you. As if your consciousness is made of something the System was not designed to write on. I had been — I have been looking for precisely this quality, and—*

He stopped. The sentence hit a wall and dropped.

"You've been looking for it," Maren said.

*That is not what I said.*

"That's exactly what you said."

*I misspoke. The integration process is disorienting. My thoughts are not always well-ordered. I will rephrase: your profile presents an anomaly that my order would have considered significant. That is all.*

It wasn't all. The redirect was there the way a client's shift from grief to suspicion was there during a ritual — the slight tightening, the pivot to safer ground. Eleven years of reading people for a living had made him very good at hearing the shape of a lie, even when the liar didn't have lungs.

He pulled the shirt the rest of the way on and tucked it into his trousers. The bowl was on the shelf where he'd left it last night. Ordinary ceramic, brown-glazed, a chip on the rim from five years ago when he'd dropped it packing up after the Halversen ritual. He'd carried it for eleven years and it had never done anything except hold water and ash and the occasional handful of dried rosemary. He left it where it was.

He ate cold porridge from last night's pot, standing at his listing table. The porridge had set into something you could probably use to fill masonry cracks. He ate it anyway, scraping the pot with a wooden spoon, because food was food and he'd spent eleven years in a village that paid him just enough to eat poorly.

*Do you always eat standing up?*

"When the chair's got a broken leg, yeah."

*The chair has been broken for how long?*

"Couple of years."

*And you have not repaired it because—*

"Because I don't know how to fix chairs. I fake sin-eating. Different skill set."

*Clearly.*

The morning outside was warm and bright and full of small village sounds — a rooster that didn't know when to stop, someone hammering something wooden, the distant complaint of livestock. Maren stood in his doorway and let the sun hit his face. It was the kind of morning where everything looked like it always had — the packed-dirt path to the square, the leaning fence around Tam's goat yard, the scorch marks on the stone wall of the grain store where a hero patrol had tested some ability months ago and nobody had cleaned it up because nobody ever cleaned those things up. They just accumulated, a record of careless power that the village stepped around the way you stepped around horse shit.

He started walking.

*Threat assessment for this area: negligible. No active hostiles within standard scan range. Ambient contamination levels: low overall, concentrated near the eastern woodline and the Garsen property. Regional hero activity: one patrol, ranger-class, moving northeast along the main road at approximately four miles' distance. They are not heading toward this village.*

"I didn't ask."

*You did not need to. Morning briefing was standard procedure in my order. Situational awareness for the operating environment. It was — this was something I did every day.*

Another pause. Maren let that one sit. He didn't push it.

Fenhold's square was just a widening of the main path where three tracks met — a well, a bench, old Poul's horse trough, and enough open dirt for a dozen people to stand around in without bumping elbows. It was already occupied by the low commerce of late summer. Grenna was shaking a rug over her fence, beating it with a wooden paddle in steady, punishing strokes. Poul was leading his carthorse toward the trough, the animal's hooves raising clouds of fine dust. Two children ran between the houses, changing direction at nothing, stopping at nothing, reaching for things Maren couldn't see.

He'd never been able to see it. Twenty-eight years. And he'd never stopped watching the people who could.

Grenna paused mid-swing to glance at something above the roofline. Her eyes tracked left, held, then returned to the rug. A notification. Something in the System's constant ambient chatter had caught her attention for half a second before she dismissed it. Poul's horse balked at the trough and Poul's gaze flicked to a spot about three feet to the left of the animal's head — a threat marker, maybe, or a quest flag, though threat markers near a village water trough weren't usually worth the flinch. The children's path made no sense unless you understood they were following invisible waypoints, their trajectories determined by prompts only they could see.

Maren had learned this language the way a deaf man learns to read lips. Not the System itself, but the shadows it cast on other people's faces. The flinches and glances and micro-pauses. Where people looked when a notification appeared. How long they looked. Whether they smiled or frowned or dismissed it. He'd built a career on reading those shadows — matching his responses to what a blind man should do, should notice, should miss.

"Morning, Maren." Grenna's voice carried the particular half-pitch-up that people used with him. Warmer than normal, slightly louder. The voice you'd use with a child or someone recovering from an illness. "Watch the path by the bench, love — Poul's been spilling feed again."

There was no spilled feed. Maren knew this because Poul hadn't come from the feed store and because Grenna's eyes, when she said it, were focused on a point near the ground about two feet left of the bench — a System marker of some kind that her brain had translated, automatically, into a physical hazard she could warn a blind man about. She'd been doing this for years. The whole village had. A quiet, uncoordinated conspiracy of small translations. Every puddle that was really a quest residue. Every "broken step" that was really a scorch mark. Every "careful, the sun's in your eyes" that really meant a notification was flashing in the space between Maren and whoever he was talking to.

*She is lying to protect you,* Aldric said. *The marker is a minor resource node. Copper-grade. Barely worth collecting. She has translated it into a physical obstacle because she assumes you cannot process System information and she does not want you to appear confused in public.*

"I know," Maren muttered, quiet enough that Grenna wouldn't catch it. He smiled at her, stepped carefully around the nothing by the bench. "Thanks, Grenna."

*You are remarkably skilled at this.*

He kept walking. He wasn't sure whether that was meant as admiration or diagnosis.

*Both,* Aldric said.

"Stay out of my thoughts."

*I cannot. Your thoughts and my awareness share the same — the architecture is not partitioned. I hear what surfaces in your mind the way you hear what I say. It is not a choice I would make either, believe me.*

The walk to the well was a gauntlet of translations. Tam shouted from his goat yard about a "soft patch" in the road that was actually a low-grade contamination residue Maren could smell but couldn't see — that faint ozone sweetness, the one he'd always assumed was normal, the background scent of living near a dungeon mouth. Hella waved him away from her doorstep with something about spilled wash-water, which was actually a scorch mark that had warped the packed earth into a shallow depression. Each interaction required Maren to play two roles simultaneously: the blind man who relied on their kindness, and the fraud who knew the kindness was based on a misunderstanding he'd cultivated for over a decade.

*Skill assessment,* Aldric said, unprompted. *I have been reviewing the details of your registered capabilities. Your listed skills include: Consumption, rank three. Contamination sensing, rank two. Ritual binding, rank one. Fragment dissolution, rank one. These are — I should note — the minimum ratings consistent with an active sin-eater designation. They appear to be automatically assigned defaults rather than earned progressions. You have gained no experience in any sin-eating skill in eleven years of ostensible practice.*

"Are you going to read my entire file to me?"

*I already have. That was the entire file. Four skills, minimum rank, no progression. Behind the entries, the architectural data reads as — as I described. Nothing. Your profile has the depth of a drawing of a door on a flat wall. Someone might knock, but nobody is home.*

Maren reached the well. He lowered the bucket, the hemp rope rough and familiar in his hands. The splash echoed up the shaft, cool and distant.

"Useful comparison," he said. "Thanks."

*The comparison is accurate, not useful. There is a difference, though I concede the distinction may not matter to you.*

He hauled the bucket up and drank from it, cold water against his dry mouth, the iron tang of the well on his tongue. Around him the village was doing what it always did — Grenna beating the rug, the children chasing invisible objectives, Poul's horse finally drinking from the trough now that whatever marker had spooked it had either been dismissed or moved. The hum was there too, as it always was, low and constant near the eastern woodline where the contamination ran densest. A sound Maren had lived with his whole life without understanding what it was. The System's background noise, the operating frequency of the infrastructure that organized everyone's existence except his.

He'd always heard it. He just hadn't known what he was hearing.

---

The Wollen house was on the west side of the village, set back from the path behind a vegetable garden that was doing better than the garden deserved. Maren had visited three months ago to clear the contamination after the old woman died in her bed. He'd performed his usual ritual — the bowl, the ash, the murmured approximation of words that sounded appropriately solemn — and collected twelve coppers and left the contamination exactly where it was.

The daughter, Britt, met him at the door. She was forty and had the raw-knuckled hands of someone who washed things for a living — laundry, dishes, floors, the body of her dead mother. She'd hired Maren because the contamination had started making the cat behave oddly and she'd heard, correctly, that residual quest energy could affect animals before it affected people.

"Maren." She smiled the smile of someone who wanted to believe good news. "Come in, come in. How does it look?"

He stepped inside. The house was clean, sparse, smelled like soap and boiled cabbage and, underneath, the ozone-sweetness of contamination that Britt couldn't smell because she'd been living in it long enough for normal to recalibrate.

*Residual contamination, grade three,* Aldric said. *Concentrated at the shrine in the rear alcove but with lateral spread into the sleeping area and the food storage. The occupants — I count four individuals registered at this address — have been sleeping in low-grade contamination for approximately ninety days. Prolonged exposure at this level correlates with fatigue, joint pain, mood disturbance, and in young children—*

"Everything looks clean," Maren told Britt. "The clearing took well. She's at rest."

Britt's face did something that Maren had seen a hundred times and had never learned to watch without a specific, localized shame in his chest. Relief. Not the small relief of a resolved inconvenience but the deep, structural relief of someone who had been frightened for her mother's soul and had just been told by a professional that the fear was over. Her eyes went wet. She blinked it back.

"Thank you," she said. "Really. Maren, thank you."

She pressed three coppers into his hand. He took them.

*The contamination in this house has been building for three months,* Aldric said. *It will continue to build. The cat she mentioned is likely already symptomatic. In six to eight months the youngest occupant — there is a child registered here, age four — will begin showing early signs of exposure. Fatigue. Night terrors. You are taking money from this woman for work you did not do, and her family will suffer the consequences of your failure in specific, measurable ways.*

Maren closed his fingers around the coppers. They were warm from Britt's hand.

"Thank you, Britt. You take care."

He walked home. The afternoon heat was building — the air thick and still, the dust on the path warm through his boot soles. The sweet-ozone smell of contamination was stronger near the Garsen edge of the village, and the hum was louder there too, a low vibration that sat in his teeth. He'd walked this path a thousand times and he'd always noticed the smell and the hum and he'd always let them slide past because they were just the way things were, the background texture of a world he couldn't fully perceive.

He sat on the bench outside his door and put the three coppers on the wood beside him. They sat there, small and warm and damning.

*I will refrain from further comment on that transaction,* Aldric said. *My opinion is, I believe, adequately established.*

"Yeah."

The quiet between them had a different shape than the morning's quiet. The morning had been two people arguing. This was two people sitting with something that neither of them could fix and neither could stop seeing. Maren picked at a splinter on the bench. The wood was warm from the sun. A fly landed on his arm and he watched it clean its legs with the attention of a man who would rather look at anything than look at himself.

"How long were you with the Order?" he asked. Not because he wanted to know. Because the silence had become a third occupant and needed to be moved.

*Fourteen years. I was commissioned at twenty-one. Standard path — field postings, dungeon surveys, System-anomaly investigation. I reached senior investigator at twenty-eight.* A pause that had texture. *I was thirty-five when I died.*

"How?"

*A dungeon collapsed. Survey mission. Routine — a minor anomaly in the sub-architecture, the kind of entry we catalogued by the dozen. The collapse was instantaneous. No structural warning. No System alert.*

"Aren't there supposed to be warnings?"

*The System monitors structural integrity in active dungeon environments. A collapse without advance alert is either a catastrophic sensor failure or—* The wall again. He hit it mid-thought and the thought stopped. *The investigation classified it as structural failure. The inquiry lasted two days.*

"Two days."

*Two days. For a senior investigator. My order submitted six requests for extended review. All denied. The case was filed and the file was sealed and my name was added to the memorial wall in the eastern chapel and that was — that was the end of Aldric Venn.*

The full name landed in the space between them. Maren hadn't known it until now. Aldric Venn. A real name, the kind of name that had been spoken by real people in real rooms — shouted across training yards, murmured in evening prayers, written in reports and sealed in files. A name with weight.

"I'm sorry," Maren said. Then, because the sincerity startled him and he didn't know what to do with it: "That's shit. I mean — two days? That's really shit."

*Your capacity for eulogy is noted.*

"Did you have — was there anyone? Family, or—"

The quiet that followed was not the argumentative quiet or the offended quiet or even the strategic quiet of a man choosing his next sentence. It was the quiet of a room where something has broken and neither person wants to look at the floor.

*I had my order,* Aldric said. The formality was there but thinner, worn through in places, the way a uniform wears thin at the elbows and the knees. *I had duties. I had a barracks room with a window that faced east, and every morning the light came through at an angle that struck the far wall at the hour of first prayers. I maintained my own sword because I did not trust anyone else with the edge. I had — there was a woman in the commissary who remembered how I took my tea. Every morning. Without being asked. I never learned her name.*

A pause. Long enough that Maren's hands went still on the bench.

*I have thought about that more in the last day than seems reasonable. Her name. I did not think to ask it in fourteen years, and now I cannot stop — she had short hair. Brown. She poured the tea left-handed. That is what I have. That is everything I have of her. I had morning prayers. I had evening reports. I had the weight of my own body and the feeling of cold water on my face after training and the way bread tasted when you had earned it through physical work, and I did not appreciate any of it, I did not understand that these things were — that they could be taken, that everything could be—*

His voice fractured. Not a sob. Not a dramatic break. A structural failure — a place where the grammar that held him together simply gave way, and what was underneath the formality and the sarcasm and the moral certainty was a man who had lost his body, his order, his morning light, a woman's name he never learned, the taste of bread, the weight of his own hands, and who was now a voice in the skull of a man who took coppers from grieving families for work he'd never done.

Maren sat on his bench in the late summer heat with three coins beside him and a dead man's grief inside him and no idea what to say. He was good at talking. He'd built an entire career on knowing what people needed to hear and giving it to them in exactly the right voice. But the dead paladin wasn't a client. There was no angle here, no version of events that would make this better, no deflection smooth enough to redirect grief this specific.

"I don't know what to — I'm not good at this part," Maren said. His voice sounded wrong to him. Too plain. Not enough.

*That is the first honest thing you have said in my presence.*

"Yeah, well."

The fly had come back. It landed on one of the coppers and Maren watched it navigate the surface of the coin, its legs picking across the stamped face of whoever was important enough to be on a copper. The dog near the eastern woodline was barking again — it had been barking all day, probably at the contamination that sat in the trees like a smell the animal couldn't get away from.

*I am not asking for your sympathy,* Aldric said. The formality was rebuilding, brick by brick, but the foundation was different now. *I am stating facts. I had a life. It was taken. I am now — here. And I cannot leave. I have tried. The architecture does not permit separation. I am embedded in your consciousness the way — the metaphor escapes me. I am here. That is the operational reality.*

"Okay."

*Okay is not a response to what I have just told you.*

"It's the only one I've got."

Another silence. This one was almost bearable. Two people who didn't like each other, sitting with the fact that they were stuck together, and that one of them was grieving, and the other didn't know how to help and probably wouldn't have been able to help even if he did know how because the thing that was lost couldn't be returned by anything Maren had ever been good at.

*There is a quest notification for the eastern farmsteads,* Aldric said eventually. His voice had resettled into its usual register — formal, clipped, the voice of a man filing a report to an order that no longer existed. *Harvest season. The contamination will need clearing before the grain comes in.*

"Yeah."

*You cannot do it. You do not know how.*

"I know."

*The people in this village have trusted you for eleven years. They are sleeping in contamination you were paid to remove. The Wollen family is—*

"I know."

He didn't say it angrily. He said it the way you'd say the name of a road you'd been walking for a long time and had only just realized was taking you somewhere bad.

Aldric went quiet. Not the silence of strategy or offense or grief but the plain, used-up silence of two people sitting with a fact they couldn't fix. The sun was lower. The bench's shadow had shortened and Maren's feet were in full light now, his boot leather warm. The three coppers sat on the wood beside him. The fly had gone.

Somewhere inside his skull, a dead man who had lost everything was learning to exist without a body, and Maren was learning to exist with a conscience he hadn't invited that might have been right about everything.

"Okay," Maren said.

*Okay what?*

"Just — okay. That's it. Okay."

It was the closest to a truce either of them was going to manage. They sat with it — one body, two minds, the afternoon heat, and the slow accumulation of things that would have to be dealt with eventually but not right now. Not yet. For now, okay was enough. It was going to have to be.


# Chapter 3

Derry had brought eggs — two of them, brown-shelled, still warm from Marta's henhouse — and he'd been talking about leeks for what might have been five minutes. The whole west bed had gone to leeks this year. He didn't know why. Soil, maybe. Or Pell had been watering everything the same amount because she was four and believed all plants were equally thirsty, and Lenna was too tired in the evenings to correct her, and anyway who needed that many leeks? You could only make so much soup.

Maren cracked one of the eggs into a cup and drank it raw. The yolk was warm and thick and sat heavy in his empty stomach.

*He has been talking for four minutes,* Aldric said. *I have been timing it. He has not paused for breath. The man is terrified.*

Maren knew. He'd known Derry for most of his adult life, and the leek monologue was a tell — the worse things got, the more Derry talked about his garden, his daughter, what the cow did yesterday. The content didn't matter. What mattered was the noise, the filling of space, the refusal to let silence in where fear could pool.

"So the farmer's family," Derry said, his tone shifting abruptly, the way you set down something light and pick up something heavy. "They're telling people you had a seizure during the ritual."

"I heard."

"They're not angry, if that helps. More concerned. Breda's been saying you might've been touched — she means it the religious way, not the —" He waggled his hand. "You know."

*Touched,* Aldric said. There was something in his tone that sat between contempt and recognition, like a word he knew being used in a way he hadn't expected.

Maren drank the other egg. It was cooler than the first, the yolk thinner. He wiped his mouth with the back of his hand.

"I'm handling it," he said.

"You're handling it."

"Yeah."

"Maren, you were looking at the table just now."

"I look at the table all the time. It's where the table is."

"You were looking at it the way people look at someone who's talking. You tilted your head. Like you were listening to something near the table, except nothing was near the table, so."

The dwelling was quiet except for a chicken outside performing its daily complaint about the existence of morning. Light came through the single window in a bar that landed on the bowl — the ceramic one, the ritual prop, sitting on the table where it always sat, ordinary and specifically worthless.

"Derry —"

"And yesterday. In the square." Derry's voice had lowered, not for secrecy but for the weight the volume implied. "The threat notification that came through around midmorning — standard dungeon-mouth alert, nothing urgent. You flinched. You looked up and to the right, which is where the notification renders on the interface, and then you looked away like you'd caught yourself."

Maren said nothing.

"I've been standing next to you for eleven years while those come and go. I'm the one who tells you what they say. I've described hundreds of those notifications to you and you've never once — not once — reacted to one on your own. You've never looked where they actually are, because you can't see where they are. That's the whole point. That's why we work."

*He is more observant than you give him credit for,* Aldric said.

The silence had weight to it. Morning heat and dust and the faintly sweet smell of contamination that drifted from the north when the wind came off the fields wrong.

"Something answered," Maren said.

Derry went still. His hands, which had been pulling at a loose thread on his sleeve, stopped.

"At the ritual. Over the farmer. I wasn't — I mean, it was fake, same as always, but something — someone —" Maren stopped and started again, a worse sentence but a more honest one. "I've got a dead man in my head. A paladin. He died and ended up in the contamination and when I did the ritual he came through. He's been in my skull for two days and he reads me the interface."

He waited for the reaction. Derry's face went through a sequence — disbelief, then calculation, then a kind of stalled horror that didn't resolve into any single expression but sat there like a sum that wouldn't balance.

"A paladin," Derry said.

"Yeah."

"Talking to you."

"All the time. He's got opinions about what I eat, how I walk, whether I'm —" Maren cut himself off because the sentence was heading toward the fact that Aldric had critiqued his posture during urination yesterday and that was not a conversation for this morning. "The point is he can see the System. He reads it to me. And I'm reacting to what he says without meaning to — he tells me something and I flinch, or I look, and I can't stop doing it."

*You could, in fact, stop it,* Aldric said. *With discipline. Which you do not have.*

"He says it's my fault," Maren added.

Derry rubbed the back of his neck. His hand was trembling, a fine vibration visible in the sweat that caught the morning light on his knuckles.

"So the fraud's done."

"The fraud was done the second I threw up in that ditch."

"And he's not — this paladin isn't going away."

*I would prefer to go away. I am in this room against my preference and my will, same as every room I have been in for the past two days.*

"He's not going away."

Derry looked at the eggs, the bowl, the stained table. He looked at them the way a man memorizes a room before leaving it, cataloguing the objects not by use but by what they'll look like remembered.

"All right," he said. "All right. So what do we do?"

***

They walked to the well because water still had to be fetched, even now, even with a dead paladin commentating on the structural integrity of the well rope and the ambient contamination levels of the drainage ditch and the specific threat classifications of three birds sitting on the chapel roof.

*Residual contamination at the northwest field boundary reads point-three,* Aldric said. *Climbing. Yesterday it was point-two-six. Consistent with uncleared accumulation from six to eight months of inadequate sin-eating maintenance.*

Maren pulled the bucket up. The rope was rough and familiar in his hands and the bucket came heavy with cold water that sloshed against its sides.

"Six to eight months of inadequate maintenance," he muttered. "That'd be me."

*I was not going to say that.*

"You were absolutely going to say that."

Breda was on her doorstep with her morning broth, watching the square with the alert, unhurried attention of a woman who had been watching this square since before Maren was born.

"Morning, Maren. You're looking better."

"Thanks, Breda. Bit of a rough patch." The performance rose automatically — the easy warmth, the slightly sheepish smile, the posture of a man with nothing to hide. He'd been performing since seventeen and the muscles of it were deeper than thought.

"My sister's boy had something similar," Breda said. "Fits. He grew out of it."

"I'm twenty-eight, Breda."

"So was he, at the time. A man can grow out of things at any age." She smiled and sipped her broth.

Derry had stopped a few paces back, hands in his pockets, watching the exchange with the expression of a man counting exits.

"She doesn't know anything," Maren said quietly once they were past.

"I know she doesn't." Derry's voice was tight. "That's not — I wasn't thinking she —"

"Then what?"

"I'm thinking about who does know. You flinched in the square yesterday, Maren. I'm the one who noticed because I know what to look for, but I'm not the only person in this village with eyes."

Maren splashed water on his face. The cold sat on his skin, a sensation with no data attached — no System overlay, no numbers. Just cold water on warm skin on a summer morning.

*Maren.*

Aldric's tone had changed. The running commentary — the contamination indices and threat assessments and moral objections to Maren's hygiene — had been constant for two days, a stream that Maren had started learning to push to the back of his attention the way a person stops hearing a clock. But this was different. This was the voice Aldric used when the formal architecture of his speech went rigid, when the sentences got shorter and the words got more precise, and Maren had known him for only two days but he already understood that Aldric getting more formal was the same as a normal person screaming.

*The System has been performing broad-spectrum classification updates across this region. I have been monitoring them since last night. They are not routine.*

Maren dried his face on his sleeve. Derry was watching him, waiting, reading the pause the way he'd learned to read Maren's pauses over eleven years of partnership.

"What?" Derry said.

"Hold on." Maren put the bucket down. His hands wanted to grip something and the bucket handle was the available option.

*The System has classified you. Designation: unclassified entity. Threat level: active. I have never —* A pause. *This designation is not standard. It is reserved for systemic anomalies. Infrastructure failures. The classification itself is a contradiction — a designation for the unclassifiable. And the System has attached a bounty.*

"A bounty," Maren said, and the word came out quieter than he'd meant.

*Four thousand gold crowns.*

The bucket handle bit into his fingers. He was gripping it hard enough to feel the grain of the wood, the places where years of hands had worn the surface smooth and the places where splinters still caught, and these details mattered because the number didn't make sense and the handle at least was real.

"That can't be right. Say it again."

*Four thousand gold crowns. Capture or elimination. Unclassified entity, Fenhold region. The bounty is live and broadcasting at maximum range, which at this threat designation I estimate to be — considerable. Far beyond this village.*

"Maren." Derry's voice had changed. Not the chatter, not the nervous fill, not even the low-and-serious register from this morning. Something else. Something flat. "Maren, there's something on my interface."

Maren looked at him. Derry's eyes had the unfocused quality of a person reading System text — the slight divergence, the small involuntary tracking movements that Maren had spent eleven years learning to mimic and never actually experienced.

"Bounty notification," Derry said. "Four thousand — Maren, that says four thousand gold crowns."

"I know."

"That's —" Derry's voice went high and cracked. "That's not real. That can't — bounties like that don't — people put together whole companies for numbers like that. Real companies, with gear and party compositions and — four thousand crowns buys you a hero-class squad and enough left over to retire on, and it's got — Maren, it's got our region marked."

"I know."

"Everyone can see this. Everyone with the interface. Every farmer, every trader, every hero between here and—"

"I know, Derry."

They were standing at the well in the village square on a late-summer morning and the blacksmith was hammering and a dog was barking at the chapel door and three children were chasing something down the lane with the directionless joy of children running, and all of it was the same as yesterday and the day before and every day for eleven years, and none of it mattered now because every System-sighted person within range had just received a number and a direction, and the direction was here.

*At this bounty value, organized parties will mobilize within hours,* Aldric said. *I am uncertain of the nearest hero staging area, but this is not a sum that inspires patience.*

Derry's face had gone the color of wet clay. His hands hung at his sides and his wedding ring — a plain copper band gone green at the edges from years of soil and sweat — caught the sun the way metal catches the sun, without caring what kind of morning it is.

"We have to go," he said. "Right now. We have to — Maren, this village, these people — if heroes come here tracking the bounty and start asking about the sin-eater—"

"They'll find out I've been faking."

"They'll find out we've both been faking. And they'll find out there's an unclassified whatever-it-is living in the village where their neighbors live, where the children are, and that's not — people don't handle that well, Maren, they don't — and if anyone in Fenhold figures out what that bounty means before we're gone—"

*He is correct. Your departure should have been immediate. The bounty has been broadcasting for —* A careful pause. *Approximately four hours.*

Four hours. Maren had been sleeping for part of that. Eating eggs. Walking to the well. Making small talk with Breda while every interface in range lit up with a number that could finance a small war, stamped with the name of the place where he lived.

"How fast can you be ready?" Maren asked.

Derry looked at him. And there was the thing Maren had been refusing to see all morning — the thing he'd been reading on Derry's face with the fluency of a man who read faces for a living and the cowardice of a man who didn't want to know what this particular face was saying.

"Lenna," Derry said. "And Pell."

A wife and a daughter. Two words that weighed more than four thousand gold crowns.

"They can't come." Maren didn't phrase it as a question. He'd run through the arithmetic already — heroes tracking a bounty traveled fast and light, they didn't stop for families, and a woman and a four-year-old on a hunted road was not escape, it was a slower way to be found.

"I know they can't come." Derry's voice was still flat but something moved underneath it, a pressure that made the flatness sound less like calm and more like a man holding a door shut. "I know. I'm just — give me a minute to—"

He didn't finish. His mouth pressed thin and his hands found each other and the fingers laced and squeezed, the green-edged ring pressing between his knuckles.

"Go talk to her," Maren said. "I'll pack."

"Yeah." Derry took a step toward the lane that led to his house and then stopped. "Maren."

"What?"

"This is because of you. All of this — the bounty, the ritual, whatever happened at the farmer's house, this paladin — and my wife and my daughter are in that village while that number's hanging over it with our location on it, and I'm not — I'm not saying it to be cruel, I just need you to know that I know."

The words landed one at a time, each one settling into a silence that the village continued to fill with its ordinary sounds — hammering, barking, children, the hum underneath everything that only Maren could hear. He watched his friend's face and what was there was not hatred. It was something worse and more durable — a man who loved his friend and resented his friend and was terrified of what his friend had become, all at the same time, and no single feeling was winning because they were all true.

"I know," Maren said.

Derry went.

***

Maren packed. The bag took two minutes.

He rolled the spare shirt around the ceramic bowl so it wouldn't crack against anything. The bowl was light and plain and the cheapest he'd been able to find at market eleven years ago, when he'd first decided to fake sin-eating rituals and needed a prop. It had no System properties. It was not special. He'd held it over sixteen bodies and murmured the words he'd memorized from watching a real sin-eater work once, at a distance, in a village that wasn't Fenhold, when he was sixteen and hungry and already learning that the world gave you nothing you didn't steal or fake.

He tucked the candle stubs into the shirt's sleeves and counted his money: forty-three coppers in a leather fold. He counted them twice because the first count felt wrong — not the number, which was accurate, but the scale of what the number needed to cover.

*Forty-three coppers,* Aldric said, having apparently done the same arithmetic. *The bounty on your head is four thousand gold crowns and your total wealth is forty-three coppers.*

"I didn't ask for commentary."

*That is not commentary. It is arithmetic.*

Outside, the village went on being a village. The blacksmith's hammer found its rhythm, the uneven percussion of a man who'd been at the work long enough to stop caring about consistency. The dog had settled into a less committed position on barking. The children had moved their project to the far end of the lane. The contamination smell drifted from the north — faintly sweet, faintly acrid, the ambient smell of a village that sat too close to dungeon territory and had been serviced by a fake sin-eater for over a decade.

Maren stood in the doorway of his dwelling and looked at Fenhold. The low stone houses. The mud lane that kinked left near the chapel. The fields beyond where the late-summer crops were standing — wheat and barley, and somewhere in there Derry's leek empire, the west bed that had gone entirely to leeks this year for reasons neither soil chemistry nor a four-year-old's watering habits could fully explain.

The drainage ditch behind the chapel held its usual thin pooling of quest-light — the pale shimmer that Maren had always been able to see, even blind. Not the data overlay, not the classification text. Just the raw glow, like moonlight in the wrong place at the wrong time of day. He'd walked past it every morning for eleven years. He'd never known the contamination reading or the threat level or any of the numbers that hung in the air above it for everyone else. He hadn't needed to know. The not-knowing had been a kind of freedom, and the freedom had been a kind of lie, and now both were over.

He'd lived here for eleven years. Sixteen bodies. Twelve coppers a ritual, split with Derry. Meals in rotating households because the sin-eater was a community resource, even a fraudulent one. He'd known these people's children and their animals and the shapes of their daily worries — weather, crops, the distant hum from the dungeon mouth a day's walk north.

None of it had been real. None of what he'd offered had been real. But the life had been real, or at least the shape of a life, and he was leaving it now — the second time in three days that he'd lost something he hadn't known he valued. First the comfortable lie of blindness. Now the comfortable lie of home.

*You are wasting time,* Aldric said, but his voice was quieter than usual, and Maren chose not to examine what that meant.

Derry came back.

He'd been gone twenty minutes. His bag was larger than Maren's — stuffed with bread, a blanket roll, a second pair of boots strapped to the outside with twine he'd tied badly because his hands were shaking. His eyes were red. His face was set in the particular expression of a man who had finished crying and was now doing the thing that came after crying, which was carrying and walking and not looking back until looking back became unavoidable.

He smelled like his house. Woodsmoke. The herb soap that Lenna made from whatever grew in the garden that wasn't leeks. And underneath, something smaller and sweeter — the particular warm scent of a child's hair pressed close, breathed in deep, held as long as the breathing would allow.

He didn't say anything about Lenna. He didn't say anything about Pell.

"South?" he asked.

"South. North puts us closer to the dungeon mouth and the hero patrols."

*The southern route adds approximately two days before intersecting the main road, but avoids the highest concentrations of quest-light and active hero presence. Given the bounty's range, this is the correct decision.*

"South works," Maren said.

They walked. Past the blacksmith, who nodded at them without pausing his hammer. Past Breda's doorstep, where her broth was finished and the cup sat empty on the stone beside her chair, and she raised a hand in the absent, unhurried wave of a woman who expected to see them again tomorrow. Past the chapel with its contamination haze in the ditch. Past the well where half an hour ago the number had fallen on them and the morning had emptied out.

At the village edge, where the lane turned to packed dirt and the first fields opened out toward the southern road, Derry stopped.

He turned and looked back at Fenhold. The roofs. The forge smoke. The lane, kinked left. The house — his house, the one he'd built most of himself over six years of weekends and borrowed tools, the one with the leek garden and the herb soap and the daughter who watered everything equally because she believed all growing things had the same thirst.

His face was a man looking at the specific, ordinary, irreplaceable mass of his own life, and the mass was too heavy to carry and too heavy to leave.

Maren didn't look back. He'd spent eleven years performing a life in this village and the performance was over, and turning around was what you did when the leaving was real — when the thing behind you had substance and roots. His hadn't. It had been a fraud and he'd been a fraud in it and the only honest thing he could do now was keep walking.

But Derry's life had been real. His wife was real. His daughter was real. The leeks were real.

"Come on," Maren said, not unkindly.

Derry turned from Fenhold. He hitched his bag higher on his shoulder and fell into step, and they walked south on the packed dirt road while behind them the village sat in the late-summer sun and the bounty hung in the air above it like a sign that only Maren couldn't read, pointing at the place where he used to live.

*The southern road,* Aldric said. *Approximately six days of travel to the market town of Silt, assuming no encounters. I will provide updates as conditions change.*

"Thanks," Maren said.

They walked south, and the hum came with them.


# Chapter 4

The road south from Fenhold was packed earth going to powder in the late-summer heat, and Maren's boots left prints that filled in behind him like the ground couldn't be bothered to remember he'd passed. Two days of walking. Two days of Aldric's commentary — quest markers, threat assessments, ambient contamination readings, hero patrol routes — delivered in the cadence of a man filing reports to an order that no longer existed.
