Two days of Derry's nervous chatter about anything that wasn't the bounty, wasn't the voice in Maren's head, wasn't the fact that they'd abandoned everything and the abandoning hadn't made them safe.

*Quest notification. Range: approximately three hundred meters, northeast. Level eight, expired. Residual contamination minimal.*

"Thanks," Maren said.

Derry glanced back from two steps ahead — the distance he'd been keeping since Fenhold, not leading so much as placing space between them without committing to it. "What?"

"Nothing."

Derry's shirt was dark between the shoulder blades. He'd been adjusting and readjusting the straps of his pack every half mile, a habit that had nothing to do with the fit and everything to do with needing his hands occupied. He was carrying bread from the last village they'd skirted — he'd traded a copper for a loaf and a copper for directions south, and both transactions had taken him three times as long as necessary because he'd gotten the baker talking about her oven and the directions man talking about road conditions and Derry filled silences the way water filled holes, automatically and without apparent effort.

"You keep talking to yourself," Derry said. He wiped his face with his sleeve. "People notice that."

"There's no people."

"There will be. Settlement somewhere past this ridge — I saw chimney smoke this morning."

*Correct. Settlement bearing west-southwest, approximately two miles. Population estimate based on ambient System activity: forty to fifty individuals. Agricultural. No hero presence detected.*

The information scrolled through Maren's skull in Aldric's voice. Three days of this and Maren still couldn't decide if the constant narration was a gift or a punishment. Both, probably. The paladin translated the entire visible architecture of a world Maren had lived in for twenty-eight years without seeing, and he did it with the slight, persistent distaste of a man reading ingredients off a package he wouldn't eat from.

They walked. The sun sat heavy on the road and the dust had a specific quality out here, finer than Fenhold's, more like chalk than dirt. It got into Maren's throat and stayed. Each stone came through the thin soles of his boots — he'd been meaning to get them resoled for two seasons, and now that was the kind of problem that belonged to a life he'd walked away from.

Derry started talking about whether they should cut around the settlement or push through it, whether the detour added an hour or two, whether there'd be a well. He talked the way he always talked when he was scared, which was a lot and about nothing, each sentence linked to the next with "and" and "but" until the original thought was buried under qualifications. His hands kept going to the pack straps. Pull. Adjust. Release. Pull.

Maren let him talk. It was familiar.

Then the hum changed.

He'd been hearing it his whole life — that low-frequency drone at the edge of perception, louder near dungeon mouths and contamination zones, the sound of the System doing whatever the System did. Most people filtered it out unconsciously. Maren couldn't. His brain had nothing to filter it against. It was always there, under everything else, like the sound of his own blood.

But now it thickened. Got denser, the way a single note becomes a chord. Something ahead of them, past the next rise where the road dipped between two low hills.

"Stop," Maren said.

Derry stopped. "What?"

*Hold on.* Aldric's voice shifted — less narration, more attention. The difference between reading aloud and actually looking at something. *Quest marker. Abandoned. Level twelve, deactivated approximately three weeks ago. The contamination was not cleared after deactivation. It has been pooling.*

The smell hit next. Ozone-sweet and rotten underneath, the specific reek of quest residue left to ferment in summer heat. Fresh contamination smelled almost clean, chemical. This had been sitting for weeks, baking in the warmth, and it had turned — that sour undertone like fruit going to vinegar, the smell of something biological beginning to break down.

"Yeah, I can smell it," Derry said. He'd gone still. His hands were locked on the pack straps and not moving. "That's contamination. From a quest?"

"Abandoned one." Maren walked to the top of the rise and looked down.

The hollow was maybe thirty paces across, a natural depression where two low ridges met. Quest-light pooled in it like rainwater — Maren could see the shimmer, that pale glow that was all the System's brilliance amounted to from his side of the blindness. Without the data Aldric could read overlaid on it, it was just light caught in a dip in the ground. Pretty, almost, the way something dangerous could be pretty if you didn't know what you were looking at. The contamination sat above the pool like humidity, thick enough that the grass at the edges had yellowed and gone brittle. No insects. Even the flies had pulled back.

*Contamination density is increasing at approximately four percent per day. The topography is funneling the drift westward — toward the settlement. At current rate, it reaches the settlement boundary in six to eight days. If it contacts their water source first, which is likely given the drainage pattern, the timeline may be as little as four.*

*In children especially,* Aldric added, and his voice had changed register, moved from System narration into something that sounded almost like the prayer cadence Maren had heard in those first terrible hours, *the effects of sustained exposure at this density are — significant.*

"We should go around," Derry said. He'd come up behind Maren and was staring at the hollow with the expression of someone who understood exactly what he was looking at and wanted no part of it. "There's got to be a way around. We add an hour, maybe two, and we just—"

"Four days until it hits their water."

"And someone will clear it. A real sin-eater, someone who actually — Maren. Come on."

Derry was right. They had a bounty on their heads — four hundred gold, visible to every hero in three regions. Every hour they stood still was an hour for someone to close the distance. The smart play was to walk wide, add the detour, keep moving. Let whoever was supposed to clear the contamination clear it.

But nobody was going to clear it. The quest had been abandoned three weeks ago and no sin-eater had come, and Maren knew — the way he knew the weight of his own ceramic bowl in his pack, the specific weight of eleven years of fraud — exactly why. There weren't enough sin-eaters. There never were. The work piled up faster than the workers could manage, and the System kept generating quests and the quests kept generating contamination and the contamination kept pooling in hollows like this one, drifting toward settlements where farmers drew water from streams and children played in the fields and no one thought about what was seeping through the soil because someone was supposed to have cleaned it up.

Someone like him. Who hadn't.

"Maren." Derry's voice was careful now. "We've got a bounty. Every hero in range can see it. We don't have time to—"

"I know."

*You cannot be serious.* Aldric's voice had gone clipped, which meant he'd already assessed the situation and was marshaling objections in formal order. *You have never performed a genuine consumption. You do not know the ritual sequence. You do not understand the containment protocols. The failure modes of a Level twelve consumption attempted without training or supervision—*

"What happens if nobody clears it?"

Silence from Aldric. Then, quietly: *You know what happens.*

"Tell me how to do it."

*I am a paladin. I studied sin-eating theory within the Order. I did not practice the discipline. The difference between theoretical knowledge and practical execution in this specific—*

"So it's theoretical." Maren was already walking down the slope. His boots slid in the dry grass and he caught himself with one hand against the warm earth. "Walk me through the theory."

---

He set up at the edge of the pool the way he'd set up a thousand times before. Bowl on flat ground, candle to the left, himself kneeling with his palms on his thighs. The postures of his old life. He could do these in his sleep, had done them in his sleep once — Derry shaking him awake at a farmer's bedside and Maren's arms already moving through the opening gestures before his eyes were open.

But the intention was different, and the difference sat in his chest like a fist, and his chest didn't want to open.

*The containment pass must come first. You establish the boundary of the consumption zone before attempting any clearing action. Think of it as — you are creating a vessel around the existing pool. Not with the bowl. With your awareness. Your attention. The real ritual requires genuine openness to the contamination. You allow it to contact your consciousness without your consciousness rejecting the contact. This is the component that cannot be faked.*

"Great. How do I do that?"

*I — that is precisely the gap between theory and practice. The texts describe it as a form of acceptance. You are not consuming the contamination yet. You are acknowledging it. Allowing it proximity.*

Maren looked at the pool. The shimmer was right there, an arm's length away. Up close, the contamination had a physical weight — not the visual glow, which was faint and directionless, but a pressure on the skin, like standing too close to an oven. His forearms broke into gooseflesh. The sweet-rotten smell was thick enough to coat his tongue.

His body knew what contamination meant the same way it knew what cliff edges and snapping dogs meant. By staying away.

"Maren." Derry stood at the top of the ridge, both hands wrapped around his pack straps, his knuckles pale. "I don't — look, this doesn't seem like a good idea, and I know you don't want to hear that, but I don't — do you actually know what you're doing? Because if you don't, maybe we could find someone, or — I mean, we could leave a message at the settlement, tell them there's contamination, let them send for—"

"Send for who, Derry?"

Derry's mouth worked for a moment. Then he looked away.

Maren knelt. The ground at the pool's edge was damp and warm, heat radiating up through his knees, and the dampness soaked through his trousers immediately. He placed his hands on his thighs and tried to do the thing he'd been pretending to do for eleven years.

Opening. That was Aldric's word. Opening yourself to the contamination. Letting it in.

For eleven years, every ritual Maren had performed was about closing. Closing the performance. Sealing the lie. Keeping the boundary between what he appeared to be and what he was. Every gesture, every murmured word, every careful angle of the bowl was designed to keep the contamination out while looking like it was being let in. His whole career was a locked door dressed up as an open one.

Now he had to actually unlock it, and every instinct he had pulled the other way.

He breathed. The contamination smell flooded his sinuses — ozone and rot and that chemical sweetness, like something organic being dissolved. His eyes watered. His throat clenched and he swallowed bile and kept breathing because Aldric had said openness and openness meant not pulling back when every nerve said pull back.

The first contact was heat. Not from outside — from inside, as if the contamination had found its way through his skin and was warming him from the blood outward. His fingers tingled against his thighs. The hum rose in pitch, or his hearing sharpened, or both.

*Good. That is — yes, that is initial contact. Now the containment pass. You must establish the boundary before—*

But Maren's body was already moving.

The old choreography — the broad sweeping arm motion he'd used in a thousand fake rituals, the one he always did first because it was dramatic and the paying families liked drama — pulled at his muscles like a reflex. Before Aldric finished speaking, Maren was halfway through the clearing pass, his right arm tracing the arc that had never cleared anything, that had always been pure theater.

Except now the contamination was inside him. And the clearing pass, done before containment, opened a channel that wasn't bounded by anything.

*No — Maren, stop. That is the clearing motion. You have not established containment. You are doing it in the wrong—*

The contamination surged.

The pool of quest-light flared — bright enough that Maren could see it without any help, not shimmer but actual light, the pale yellow-white of a wound going septic. The hum spiked to a whine that drilled through his back teeth and settled behind his eyes. And the contamination that had been sitting in its hollow pushed outward through the uncontained channel his botched ritual had opened, hitting him like swallowing a river.

It went through him. Not around, not against — through. And it brought everything that was embedded in it.

She hit like a blade between his eyes.

Not like Aldric. Aldric's arrival had been weight and grief, a presence settling into Maren's skull with the heft of a man sitting down in a room he didn't want to be in. This was edges. Fast, angular, precise even in violence. Something landing in his consciousness the way a knife lands in a doorframe — point first, quivering, still carrying the force of the throw.

Maren's hands spasmed. The bowl tipped sideways and rolled into the warm mud. The candle went out. He was on all fours with his fingers digging into the wet earth while something new rearranged itself behind his eyes.

*What—* Aldric started.

*Where am I.*

Not a question. A demand. And in the half-second before Maren's body seized — his back arching, his fingers clawing furrows in the mud — the new voice was already cataloguing. Location unknown. Position compromised. No weapons. Body not hers. Someone else's hands in the dirt, someone else's lungs pulling someone else's air, and another presence already here, reading as — holy order, formal classification, a paladin —

*You have got to be—*

The collision happened.

Not the fragment's arrival — that was already done. The collision was what happened when two opposed System classifications occupied the same unclassifiable space. Maren felt it the way you feel a bone breaking — not pain first, but wrongness, the deep structural protest of something being forced past its design tolerance. His vision whited out. His jaw locked. Something inside his skull crackled like ice splitting in a glass, and his body moved without his direction — his hands lifting from the mud and tracing patterns he'd never learned, his head turning to track something that only the fragments could see, his spine straightening and then curving as if pulled by two different forces at once.

The quest-light around them flared to a brightness that hurt. The hum climbed past hearing into something felt, a vibration in his teeth and his sternum and the bones of his wrists. Even the ground seemed to shift — the warm damp earth going cold under his knees and then hot again, as if the land itself couldn't decide what it was near.

Then it stopped.

He was face-down at the edge of the hollow, covered in mud and his own sweat. The candle was out. The bowl was somewhere behind him. And inside his skull, where there had been two voices — his own thoughts and Aldric's careful narration — there were now three, and the three didn't fit.

*— the hell are you,* the new voice said, directed at Aldric. No warmth. No curiosity. The tone of someone clearing a room.

*I am — I was — Aldric, Knight-Servitor of the Order of the—*

*A paladin. In this host.* The word "host" landed with a contempt so specific it had texture. *And the host is — what's wrong with this body? Why can't I see the interface? Why can't I—*

She stopped. The silence lasted two full breaths.

*I'm dead.*

Maren pushed himself up to his knees. His arms shook. He could taste copper and ozone and the sweet rot of contamination that had passed through him and left residue on everything — his tongue, his teeth, the lining of his throat.

*I was on a contract.* The voice had gone flat — not calm but forcibly stabilized, like a table propped level on an uneven floor. *Torren Blackhall. I had his schedule. I had his guard rotations mapped for ten days. Three days until the window between third bell and the shift change. I was—*

She stopped again. Recalibrated. Maren could feel her doing it — a mind faster than his reorganizing its own architecture around information that changed everything.

*How long.*

"What?" Maren's voice came out wrecked, scraped raw from the bile and the ozone.

*How long have I been dead.*

"I don't — I don't know. The quest's been abandoned three weeks, but you could've been in the contamination longer than—"

*I didn't ask about the quest. How long since I died.*

*The contamination layers suggest the fragment has been embedded for—* Aldric began, falling back into his report cadence.

*I wasn't asking you.*

"I don't know," Maren said. He sat back on his heels. His hands had gone still, which surprised him, because nothing else had. "I'm sorry. I really don't know."

Derry was shouting something from the ridge. Maren could hear him but couldn't process the words, because two voices had started talking in his skull and his own thoughts were trying to find room between them.

*The host is System-blind,* Aldric said, addressing the new presence with the careful diction of a man explaining a fire to someone standing in it. *He cannot see the interface. I have been serving as—*

*System-blind.* A pause — not hesitation, calculation. *And you're reading the interface for him. A paladin, playing guide dog for a System-blind — what is he? What's his class?*

*That is — somewhat complicated.*

*Uncomplicate it.*

*He is a sin-eater. Fraudulently. For eleven years. He has no genuine training. What you just experienced was his second real consumption. The first was me. He does not know what he is doing. The System has placed a bounty on him because his classification has—*

*A fraud.* The voice — Vess, the name surfaced from somewhere in the fragment, sharp-edged and exact — went flat in a way that made Aldric's formality sound like warmth. *I was consumed by a fraud. In a botched ritual. In a ditch.*

"It's a hollow," Maren said, because his mouth still worked faster than his judgment.

*The distinction doesn't help your case.*

Aldric made a sound that might have been agreement. The first thing the paladin and the assassin had agreed on.

*So I'm dead.* Each word deliberate, placed like a stone on a scale. *I'm a fragment. In a System-blind fraud. With a paladin. My contract is open. Blackhall is still alive. My guild doesn't know what happened to me—*

She hit a gap. Maren felt it — a hitch in the current of her thoughts, like a foot stepping where a stair should be and finding air. She'd reached for something and it wasn't there.

*My handler. I should — the name was—*

The gap again. Wider this time, and Maren could feel the edges of it — not blurred, not uncertain, but cleanly cut, as if something had been removed with precision rather than lost through decay.

*There's a piece missing.* Not panicked. Clinical. The careful notation of a professional discovering a tool was broken. *I can feel the relationship. I know the protocols, the drop locations, the code sequences. But the name is gone. That shouldn't—*

*Fragment degradation.* Aldric's voice had gone quiet — genuinely quiet, not his usual loaded silence. *Embedded consciousnesses lose coherence over time without proper consumption. Your memory structure has — gaps. This is consistent with prolonged embedding in uncleared contamination.*

*I know what degradation means.* She said it the way she said everything — once, precisely, finished with the subject. *What's the tactical situation.*

---

The tactical situation was this: two fragments from opposed System classifications — Order of the Veil, holy alignment; Nightguild, shadow alignment — were sharing a skull that the System couldn't write on, and whatever had happened during the collision had changed something fundamental about the space they occupied.

Maren sat on the slope above the hollow with his back against a scrub pine, breathing through his mouth because the contamination smell was still thick in his nose. The bark dug into his spine through his shirt. His trousers were caked with warm mud to the knees. Derry crouched three feet away, opening and closing his hands on the pack straps, his face the color of old whey.

"So there's two of them now," Derry said.

"Yeah."

"In your head."

"Yeah."

"And one's a paladin and one's an — assassin? She's an assassin?"

"Was."

Derry absorbed this. His hands went to the pack straps, released, went back. "And they don't get along?"

Inside Maren's skull, Vess and Aldric were occupying the same space the way a knife and a hymnal occupied the same drawer — technically possible, fundamentally wrong. Vess was conducting a systematic inventory of her situation with the efficiency of someone packing for an operation: what she retained, what was missing, what capabilities were functional. She did it without self-pity, which was worse than self-pity would have been. The gaps were damage. She documented them and moved to the next item.

Aldric was providing a strategic briefing that Vess hadn't asked for and wasn't acknowledging.

*— the bounty is four hundred gold, highest priority bracket for hero intervention. Current heading south-southwest, nearest hero staging point is Harrow, approximately four days on foot—*

*I can read the threat data myself. What I'm asking is why you let him attempt a consumption he wasn't qualified to perform.*

*I did not "let" him. He does not take orders. He does not take orders from anyone. This is the central difficulty.*

*Then the central difficulty is that I'm stuck in a skull with an incompetent host and a paladin who can't maintain operational discipline over his own—*

"They're talking about me," Maren said to Derry.

"Bad?"

"Depends on your definition."

Derry looked at the hollow, at the quest-light still pooling in it. "Did it — the ritual. Did it work?"

Maren looked at the pool. The shimmer was different now — dimmer in patches, brighter in others, the contamination disturbed but not dissolved, like mud stirred in water. The smell had shifted too, more acrid, the way contamination changed when it had been disrupted without being properly processed.

"No," Maren said.

*It did not,* Aldric confirmed, and his voice had an odd quality — careful in a way that was different from his usual formality. This wasn't the carefulness of protocol. This was the carefulness of a man choosing words because the wrong ones would break something. *The contamination has been disrupted but not cleared. The fragment — Vess — embedded rather than dissolved because the dissolution process requires System access that a System-blind host cannot provide. This is what occurred with my arrival as well, though the circumstances—*

*Were different,* Vess finished. *Because when you arrived, there wasn't already a fragment in residence. Two opposed classifications colliding inside a single host — a System-blind host — should not have been survivable.*

*I am aware.*

*And yet he's breathing.*

*Yes.*

*So either the theory is wrong, or he's something the theory doesn't cover.*

Aldric didn't answer. Maren could feel the silence — not the paladin's usual rhetorical pauses, where the quiet was doing the work of emphasis or judgment. This was different. This was a man looking at something and running out of framework.

"Aldric," Maren said.

*During the collision — when her fragment entered and the two classifications made contact inside your consciousness — the System attempted a reclassification. I could observe the process. The classification protocol initiated, cycled through its taxonomic categories, and—*

He stopped.

"And what?"

*Failed.* The word landed in Maren's skull like a dropped stone. *The classification protocol failed. It could not assign a designation to what you are. Before, you were unclassified — an absence, a blank entry. Now you are — I lack the correct term. You are something the System's taxonomy cannot describe. Not a gap in the classification. An entity that the act of classification cannot reach.*

"Is that worse?" Derry asked, because Maren had been repeating fragments of the conversation aloud without meaning to, the way he'd started doing on the second day of walking.

"I think so."

"How much worse?"

Maren didn't answer, because inside his skull, Aldric had started to say something else and then done something Maren had never heard him do.

He abandoned a sentence.

Not trailed off. Not paused for emphasis or moral weight. Abandoned it mid-structure, the formal architecture collapsing like a bridge losing a support, and started again.

*When I look at you from inside — from this position, within a System-blind host — I can see the—*

Again. The sentence stopped. Not because Aldric was being interrupted or choosing restraint but because the words weren't there. The paladin who had spent three days translating the entire visible architecture of the System into language — who had found precise, clipped, report-cadence words for threat levels and quest markers and contamination densities and the color and behavior of an interface he used to wield — could not complete a sentence about what he saw when he looked at the thing his host had become.

*I am going to stop talking now.*

"Aldric."

Nothing.

*He's frightened,* Vess said. Her voice was flat, observational — someone noting the weather. *Whatever he's looking at, it scared him enough to stop his mouth. And from what I've heard in the last ten minutes, that mouth doesn't stop easily.*

"It doesn't."

*Then that's the first problem.* A pause. *We have several. But that one's first.*

Maren leaned his head back against the pine bark and closed his eyes. Inside his skull: Vess continuing her inventory with methodical calm, documenting capabilities and gaps with equal precision. The handler's name, gone. Operational details intact — drop protocols, code sequences, the muscle memory of techniques she couldn't physically perform without a body that had been trained for them. Professional infrastructure preserved. Personal connections excised. She noted each absence without comment and moved on. Aldric, silent. The most complete silence Maren had experienced from the paladin since the moment of arrival — not the loaded, judgmental quiet of a man withholding his opinion, but the empty quiet of a man who had looked at something and lost the ability to narrate it.

Three days Maren had known Aldric. Three days of relentless commentary — moral assessments, threat briefings, interface readings, observations about Maren's posture, diet, hygiene, and character. Three days of a dead man who organized reality through language, who processed grief and displacement and the horror of his own condition by describing everything around him in the precise, clipped sentences of a report filed to an authority that no longer existed.

And now the paladin had looked at Maren — at whatever the fragment collision had made of him — and found nothing to report.

"Hey." Directed inward. "Whatever you're seeing, I need to know."

Nothing.

"Aldric."

The silence held for ten more seconds. Then: *I will — when I can find the language. I will tell you. I am not withholding for strategic reasons. I genuinely do not have words for what I am observing.*

*That's reassuring,* Vess said.

Derry was watching Maren's face. Watching the expressions shift in response to voices he couldn't hear, watching his oldest friend have two conversations with the dead while the living stood three feet away with nothing to contribute. The fear on Derry's face had changed quality. It wasn't the sharp, immediate kind anymore — not the bolt of seeing Maren seize up, hearing him scream during the collision, watching his body move in ways a body shouldn't move. This was the slower kind. The kind that settled in. The kind that came from watching someone you'd known for eleven years become something you couldn't follow.

"Two of them," Derry said. He said it like he was trying to make it real by putting it in the air. "There's two of them now."

"Yeah."

"And the — he's gone quiet? The paladin?"

"Yeah."

Derry looked at the road. The road south, away from Fenhold, away from Lenna's first attempt at tomatoes and the cow that wouldn't stay fenced and Pell counting past ten for the first time while Derry held up fingers for her to match. The road that was their only plan, their only direction. He wiped his palms on his trousers, leaving streaks in the dust.

"Well," he said. "Well, I mean — what do we do? Do we keep walking? Because I don't — I mean, we should probably keep walking, right? Even with — all of this?"

Maren looked at the hollow one last time. The contamination was still there. Disrupted, not cleared. He'd tried to do something real and instead he'd made something worse, or something different, or something that didn't have a name because the System had attempted to name it and the naming had failed. The pool would keep drifting. The settlement would keep drawing water. And Maren would keep walking south with three people in a skull built for one — two arguing, one silent — and the hum in the air louder than it had been an hour ago.

He got to his feet. His knees ached from the damp ground and his hands smelled like ozone and rot. Inside his head, a dead assassin was methodically cataloguing the holes in her own memory while a dead paladin refused to speak.

"Yeah," Maren said. "We keep walking."


# Chapter 5

Aldric hadn't spoken since the night before.

Maren had walked for hours with that silence inside him — not the ordinary absence of noise but the specific pressure of someone choosing not to speak. He knew the shape of it. He'd heard it from priests who caught him mid-fraud, from Derry's wife the night they left Fenhold, from villagers deciding whether to pay him for a ritual they suspected was fake. It was the loudest kind of quiet he knew: the kind that meant someone had seen something they hadn't decided how to carry yet.

The road ran between two fields that had gone fallow long enough for the weeds to turn woody — knee-high tangles of dry yellow scrub that rattled when the wind shifted. Late-summer sun pressed against the back of his neck. The pack chafed the same raw spot on his right shoulder it had been working on since Fenhold. Derry walked a half-step behind, the way he'd been walking since they left the hollow where the botched consumption had scrambled everything sideways, and neither of them had spoken since the creek crossing.

Vess was not quiet.

She'd been inside his skull for less than a day and she was already doing something Maren could only describe as *watching* — a constant low-level alertness that sat behind his eyes like the leading edge of a headache. She scanned the road ahead, the treeline to the east, the ditch running alongside the packed dirt, the angle of shadows on the ground. She didn't narrate the way Aldric narrated the interface. She just did it, and Maren felt it as a prickling presence behind his eyes — uninvited attention, constant, thorough.

*Two hundred yards. South. Two, armed, approaching from the sheep fold.*

She delivered this the way you'd tell someone their soup was getting cold.

"What?" Maren said, out loud, and his voice came out wrong — hoarse, scraped thin from disuse.

Derry's head turned. "Hm?"

*They've been paralleling the road from the fold. They're closing now.* A pause, almost clinical. *They look like they've been waiting.*

"Someone's coming," Maren said.

Derry's hand went to his belt — not for a weapon, because Derry didn't carry weapons, but for the camp knife, the short utility blade he used for cutting rope and splitting kindling and prying lids off jars. His fingers touched the handle and stayed there. The act of touching it was as far as his courage went.

"How do you—" Derry started.

*Aldric.* Vess's voice, sharp. Not angry — demanding. Like she'd knocked on a door she expected to find open.

The paladin's voice came back like a latch lifting. Flat, controlled, every word set down with the precision of a man filing a report for an audience that no longer existed.

*Two hero-class combatants. System profiles active. They have been tracking the bounty marker. The younger one is reading your classification field and seeing nothing. He is confused. The woman is not confused.*

"How many?" Maren asked. The question was aimed at both of them — the dead paladin and the dead assassin — and the absurdity of asking two ghosts for a tactical count while standing on a road in the afternoon sun was something he would have found funny thirty seconds from now if thirty seconds from now weren't about to be a different kind of experience entirely.

*Two,* Vess said.

*Two,* Aldric confirmed. Then, with something that might have been reluctance: *The bounty is flagged for hostile engagement. They are authorized to kill.*

They stepped out from behind the sheep pen's collapsed wall at maybe sixty yards. The late-afternoon sun was behind Maren, which meant they were walking into it, squinting, and for one stupid second he thought: I could wave. I could wave and keep going and they'd think I was some farmer heading home.

The woman was the closer threat. He knew this without knowing how he knew it — the knowledge arriving in his muscles before his mind could name it. She was forty, maybe older, with weather-darkened skin and bracers that had been repaired more than once, the leather patched over with strips of mismatched hide that said years of field work and no budget for new gear. A short sword on her hip, its pommel wrapped in a strip of faded blue cloth — wind-frayed, knotted with the tight, automatic knot of something retied every morning. Someone's color. Someone she carried with her.

Her partner was younger. Mid-twenties. He held a mace in one hand and kept adjusting his grip, shifting his fingers the way someone does when they've practiced a thing in a training yard but haven't yet done it against a body that bleeds. He had a port-wine birthmark on his left jaw that ran from his ear to his chin, and a pack that sat wrong on his shoulders — too new, too stiff, the buckles still bright.

"That's the mark," the woman said. Her voice carried easily. Calm, professional, the tone of someone who had done a version of this before.

The young one squinted at Maren. "His profile's empty. There's nothing — I mean literally nothing. I've never seen that."

"I know."

*The younger one's threat level is moderate. The woman is high. She has a class-enhanced blade — System augmentation on the edge. Her stance indicates—*

Aldric's assessment dissolved into irrelevance because Maren's body was already moving.

His right hand found the camp knife. It was in Derry's pack, slung over Maren's right shoulder — the short blade, ash handle, edge decent but meant for rope and kindling — and his fingers closed on it the way a hand closes on a railing when the stair gives out. Not decision. Not thought. The body knowing where the weapon was before the mind finished registering the word weapon.

He pulled the knife free. The pack shifted on his shoulder. Behind him, Derry made a sound — a small choked syllable that might have been "Maren" if it had gotten far enough to form.

The woman drew her sword and the hum changed. Not louder — denser. Something in the System's local field activated around the blade, a thickening that Maren could hear before he could place it — a drone, insect-close, pressing against the inner ear. The air around the edge rippled with a faint heat-shimmer that was not heat. Aldric could read it. Maren could only hear the drone.

She came forward. Not running — walking with a specific practiced economy, each step covering exactly the distance she wanted, her blade in a guard position that her System probably overlaid with optimal angles and threat trajectories and all the other combat mathematics Maren would never see. He could see only the woman: her weight on her toes, the set of her shoulders, the way her eyes stayed on his center mass.

He ran toward her.

He didn't choose to run. His legs did it — Aldric's training, the paladin's doctrine of meeting an engagement rather than absorbing one. But the body running this doctrine was Maren's, six inches shorter and sixty pounds lighter than the body the training had been built for, and the stride length was wrong. His feet hit the packed dirt at intervals that didn't match the pattern his muscles wanted, and the misalignment made him stagger on the second step.

*Lower,* Vess said. *You're too tall for this stance.*

That didn't make sense — he was short, he'd always been short — but his knees bent and his center of gravity dropped and the running worked. Not a paladin's advance. Something closer to the ground, faster, wrong-looking. He could feel the competing instructions in his own legs — Aldric's long training stride fighting against Vess's crouch — and the result was a lurching, ugly sprint that covered the distance in less time than it should have because neither set of instincts would give.

The woman swung.

She was good. The blade came in flat, a lateral cut aimed at his ribs, and the System enhancement on the edge made the air whine with a sound like a finger drawn across wet glass. Maren's left arm came up — forearm turned out, palm flat, the guard position for a shield he didn't have and had never held. Aldric's body memory. Fourteen years of training with a steel disc on his left arm, deployed onto bare flesh and thin cotton.

The blade caught him below the elbow. Not deep — the System enhancement was for piercing, and the flat angle converted the cut to a scraping drag across the outer forearm — but the pain expanded from the point of contact in a single wave that went up his arm, into his shoulder, along his jaw. His arm went cold below the cut. Blood ran down his forearm and into his palm and between his fingers and it was hot — the only hot thing on his suddenly cold arm — and the specific warmth of his own blood pooling in the creases of his palm was the most physically real thing he had felt in six days.

His right hand came up.

This was Vess. Not the motion but the intention underneath it — the way his hand found the gap between the woman's sword-arm and her neck, the geometry of a body opening itself in the follow-through of a swing. She was committed to the arc. Her sword was out wide, pulling her shoulder with it, and the left side of her neck was exposed in the space between her jawline and the collar of her jerkin. The camp knife was in his fist and his fist was moving and he was inside her guard, closer than a sword could reach.

He stabbed upward into the soft triangle below her jaw.

The knife went in. He felt the whole thing through the handle — the brief resistance of skin, then the give of something underneath that was not muscle and not fat, then something cartilaginous that the blade pushed through with a small wet pop. The handle twisted in his grip as the knife seated itself, and the twist wasn't his — Vess, turning the blade a quarter rotation to widen the channel, the automatic, practiced motion of someone who knew how bodies came apart the way a carpenter knew how joints fit together.

The woman grabbed his right wrist.

Her fingers dug into the tendons above his hand — each ridge of her grip distinct against his forearm, each finger a separate pressure point. She was strong. She was dying and she was stronger than he was and her hand on his wrist had nothing to do with combat and everything to do with the body's refusal to accept what was happening to it.

*Pull it. Side-draw. Don't lift.*

He pulled the knife sideways. Something opened in the woman's throat — not the great arterial spray of a traveling minstrel's war ballad, just a steady dark flow that ran down her collar and soaked the neck of her jerkin and dripped from the bottom edge of the leather onto the road. The blue cloth from her sword pommel was visible in the dust where the sword had fallen. She'd dropped it when her hand went to his wrist.

Her grip loosened. Not gradually — all at once, like a rope going slack when you cut it. She sat down. Not fell — sat, in the road, her legs folding under her with a strange neatness. Her mouth moved. No sound came out. Her eyes were on his face and they were present — fully present, alert, processing — and then they weren't. The change was invisible. One moment someone was looking at him and the next moment something that used to be someone was looking at nothing, and the instant between was so thin he couldn't have pointed at it if his life depended on knowing where it was.

She died sitting up in the dust with her hand still raised, reaching for the wrist of the man who'd killed her.

The young one was already running. He'd dropped his mace somewhere behind him — Maren heard it clang on the packed dirt — and his too-new pack bounced against his spine as he ran, the bright buckles catching the sun in small flashes. He ran the way people run who have never had to run seriously: all elbows and knees, no economy, his breathing already ragged at twenty yards.

Maren's legs tensed. Vess. The professional's reflex against leaving a witness — the instinct to pursue, to close the angle, to finish the geometry she'd started.

*No.* Aldric. The first word he'd spoken with force since the fragment collision.

Maren's legs stopped.

He stood on the road. The air tasted like dust and copper. His left arm hung at his side and the blood from the cut had run down to his fingertips and was dripping — each drop hitting the dirt with a small sound he couldn't stop hearing, couldn't stop counting. The camp knife was in his right fist and his fingers were locked around the ash handle — physically locked, the tendons contracted into a cramp that his conscious effort couldn't reach. He tried to open his hand. The hand stayed shut. He tried again, harder, and his forearm muscles stood out against the skin and the fingers didn't move and the knife stayed where it was.

Derry was behind the sheep pen. Maren could hear his breathing — fast, shallow, pressed flat against the stone as if less space meant less danger.

The woman sat in front of him. The dust had settled in a haze around her that the low sun turned amber. A fly had already found the blood on her collar. The blue cloth lay in the dirt beside the sword. The knot was still tight — a simple knot, the kind you tie on things you expect to keep.

He looked at his right hand. At the knife he couldn't drop. At the dark slick on the blade and between his fingers and in the lines of his palm. He thought, with a clarity that felt borrowed from someone more capable of thinking: Those are not my hands.

---

He threw up behind the sheep pen wall, twenty feet from the body.

The vomiting was mechanical — his stomach clenching, the thin bile of a body that hadn't eaten enough to produce anything substantial coming up in two hitches, and then nothing. Just the clenching, the dry heaves, his diaphragm spasming against an empty cavity. He braced his right hand against the stone — the knife had finally dropped when the vomiting started, his fingers releasing all at once the way the woman's had — and the wall was warm from the sun and gritty against his palm and he focused on that. The texture of the stone. The warmth. The specific grain of it under his fingertips.

Derry was sitting with his back against the wall's far end, his hands between his knees. He'd been there during the fight. Behind the wall. He hadn't run and he hadn't helped and neither of those facts were the kind of thing either of them would mention.

"— should move, I think we should — the other one ran, right, he'll go back to wherever they came from, and there'll be more, won't there? So we should probably — I mean not right now, you're — you need a minute. But soon. We should move soon. I've got the cloth strips, the ones Lenna cut for — she cut them from that old shirt of mine, the one with the—"

He stopped. He'd talked himself into a domestic detail that belonged to a life that was now very far away, and the words dried up.

Maren spat and straightened. His left arm had started bleeding through the sleeve again, a slow dark spread that the drying blood at the edges made look worse than it was. Six inches, outer forearm, shallow enough that the edges were already trying to close. He'd need to cut the sleeve off to clean it. He'd need to pick up the knife.

He didn't pick up the knife.

"Let me—" Derry was already up, already rummaging in the pack for the cloth strips. His hands were shaking. Not as badly as Maren's, but shaking, and he was talking while he rummaged, the words spilling out continuous and unremarkable: about whether there was a creek nearby, about whether this was the right roll of cloth or the other one, about how Lenna always said you should boil water before washing a wound but they didn't have a fire so maybe just—

He wound the cloth strip around Maren's forearm with the same care he used for binding fence posts. Tight, even, methodical. His hands steadied as they worked, the way hands do when they have a task that's smaller than the thing they're frightened of. He tied the knot and patted it once, lightly.

"There," he said. "That's — it's not deep. You're okay. Are you okay? That's a stupid question. I mean the arm."

*A clean kill,* Aldric said.

The voice was quiet. Almost gentle. Maren recognized the register from the memories he carried — the paladin's tone for addressing young soldiers after their first blood. Calm. Affirming. The specific trained kindness of a man who had watched people die for fourteen years and had developed, over those years, a language for making it bearable.

*She felt no extended pain. The strike was precise and the angle correct. You — your body — performed with discipline under—*

"Shut up."

Derry's hands stopped on the bandage.

"Not you." Maren's voice was rough. Scraped. "Him."

Derry nodded. He went back to smoothing the edges of the cloth strip, pressing them flat against Maren's arm with careful fingers.

*I understand that the shock of—*

"You don't understand shit." The words came out harder than he intended — blunt, stripped of the charm and deflection he usually wrapped around things that mattered. The performance had dropped and the voice underneath was just anger and fear and the raw misery of a man whose body had done something unspeakable while he watched from inside it. "Don't — don't put your words on it. Don't call it — a clean kill? That's the framing you're going with? A clean kill?"

Aldric was silent.

"Because it wasn't clean. Nothing about that was — I had a camp knife. A camp knife, Aldric. The thing Derry cuts rope with. And I stuck it in a woman's throat and she held onto my arm while she died. She grabbed me. She *held on.* And you want to tell me that was clean?"

*I was attempting to provide—*

"I know what you were attempting." He was half-sitting against the wall, his back against the warm stone, his left arm cradled against his chest and the blood seeping through the bandage already. "You were attempting to make it okay. To — to put it in whatever box you've got. 'Clean kill, no suffering, proper engagement.' You've done this before. You've got a whole system for it, a whole way of talking about killing people that makes it fit inside your — your doctrine, or your framework, or whatever the word is for the thing that lets you sleep after you've—"

He stopped. Not because he'd finished. Because his throat had closed up and the next word was stuck somewhere behind his sternum and it wasn't coming.

The blue cloth was visible from here. Lying in the dust beside the dropped sword. The knot, tight and habitual. Someone's color, carried into a fight that ended with the carrier sitting dead on a road she'd probably walked a dozen times before.

*I have no framework for this.* Aldric's voice had changed. The trained gentleness was gone. What was left was something rawer, something without the architecture of protocol underneath it. *I had a framework. I am attempting to use it because it is the only thing I have. Because the alternative is describing what it felt like to watch your body kill that woman with a precision that was partly mine and partly Vess's, and I do not have—*

He stopped. The sentence didn't end. It just stopped, which was — from Aldric, whose sentences always closed like doors — its own kind of alarm.

Vess spoke into the space his silence left.

*They were a scouting pair. Standard bounty protocol for unknown targets — send two to confirm, then dispatch a full party. Based on their approach vector, the staging town they came from is less than a day east. When the younger one reports, someone will come looking.*

She said this the way someone reads a weather forecast.

"How long," Maren said. His throat had loosened enough for two words.

*Tomorrow. Maybe tonight, if he ran the whole way.*

Derry was standing with the remaining cloth strips in his hands, watching Maren's face with an expression that Maren recognized because he'd spent eleven years reading faces for a living. It was the look of a man who had just seen something he was going to spend a long time not looking at directly — not horror, not fear, something worse than both. It was the look of a person realizing that someone they've known for years is capable of things they aren't, and the distance between who they thought their friend was and what their friend can actually do is a gap that no amount of normal conversation will close.

"We should move," Derry said. "Before — yeah. We should move."

They moved.

Maren left the knife on the ground by the sheep pen wall. He didn't look at it. Derry picked it up — Maren heard the scrape of blade on dirt, heard Derry wipe it on something, heard the blade go back into the pack — and neither of them mentioned that it was Derry who picked it up or that the blade had been cleaned or that the thing that needed cleaning off it was a person.

They walked. The sun moved across the sky and the shadows stretched east and the road went the way roads go — the same dust, the same ruts, the same dry weeds rattling in the ditch. Maren's left arm bled through the bandage once and Derry retied it without speaking. The hum from the nearest dungeon mouth was a distant pressure on the inner ear, rising and falling with the terrain. Quest-light pooled in the ditch to their right — faint and pale in the late-day sun, the thin shimmer that looked like heat haze to Maren and looked like data to everyone else alive.

His hands shook. He held them against his thighs as he walked, pressing his palms flat against the fabric of his trousers, and the pressure helped for a few seconds and then it didn't and the shaking came back. It wasn't fear. The fear had burned off somewhere during the first mile. The shaking was the body's record of what had happened — written in the small muscles of his hands and the tendons of his forearms, a physical testimony that his mind could try to outpace but his hands couldn't.

Aldric said nothing. His silence was different now — not the held-breath quiet of the night before, not the silence of a man who'd seen something he couldn't describe. This was the silence of someone who had tried to help and been told, accurately, that his help was the wrong shape for the wound.

Vess said nothing because Vess had already said everything she considered relevant.

Derry talked. About the road — how the ruts were deeper here, which meant carts came through, which probably meant a market town ahead, which was good because they needed water and his waterskin was nearly empty and did Maren remember the well in Fenhold, the one by the south wall, with the rope that always jammed? Pell used to hang on the crank handle and try to pull it up herself. She was too small to pull the rope but she'd hang on with both hands and her feet would leave the ground and she'd laugh—

He stopped. He'd talked himself to the memory and the memory was his daughter and his daughter was behind them, getting farther away with every step, and the talking had carried him to exactly the place the talking was supposed to carry him away from.

"Sorry," Derry said.

"Don't be sorry."

"I just — she keeps — I keep thinking about her. Is that stupid? We're out here and you just — and I'm thinking about whether she can count to twenty yet. She was stuck on fourteen. She'd go thirteen, fourteen, fourteen, fourteen."

"Not stupid," Maren said.

The light failed slowly. Derry found a spot off the road where a fallen oak made a windbreak, its trunk mossy and soft enough on top that sitting against it didn't hurt. They sat. Derry across from him, his pack between his knees. Neither of them built a fire. The evening air was still warm — late summer, the kind of night that wouldn't cool off until the small hours — and the light drained from the sky in stages, the blue going grey going colorless, and the quest-light in the ditches along the road brightened as the dark came on. Pale. Cold. The System's residue, pooling in every low place, patient as groundwater.

Maren sat with his back against the fallen trunk and his hands pressed flat against his thighs and listened to the hum. It was louder here than it had been on the road — they were closer to a dungeon mouth, maybe, or the residue in the ditches was denser than usual. The sound sat just below the range of normal hearing, a bone-deep vibration that Aldric and Vess and every other System-sighted person in the world filtered out without noticing because the interface told them what the hum meant and meaning was enough to make it disappear. Maren had heard it his whole life. Twenty-eight years of that low, persistent sound, and he'd never known what it was until Aldric arrived and told him it was the world running.

He had killed a woman. He didn't know her name. She had blue cloth on her sword — someone's color, a person she carried into the field the way a soldier carries a letter. She had bracers patched with mismatched leather. She was approximately forty and she had been good at what she did and she had died sitting in the road with her hand reaching for the wrist of the man who'd killed her.

He had killed her with a camp knife and he could still feel the resistance of the blade going in — the skin, the give, the quarter-turn that Vess's muscle memory had executed through his tendons. He could feel it in his palm the way you feel a splinter after you've pulled it out. The ghost of the entry. The body's memory of the thing the mind was trying to put down.

Eleven years. Eleven years of fake rituals, practiced gestures, borrowed grief, the careful architecture of a lie maintained through charm and observation and Derry's whispered System readings from the back of the crowd. Eleven years of nothing real — no genuine connection to the work he pretended to do, no authentic act, nothing true all the way down. And then something answered. And then the System noticed. And then his body — operated by dead people whose skills lived in his muscles like tenants he hadn't invited — had put a blade into a woman's throat on a road outside a sheep pen while he watched from inside, unable to stop it and unable to look away.

The first real thing he had ever done.

He pressed his shaking hands flat against his legs and held them there and watched the quest-light brighten in the ditches — pale, cold, permanent — and did not sleep.


# Chapter 6

The bread sat in Maren's hand like a stone. Yesterday's bread — the crust had gone the color of old bark and the inside was dense and cold, the kind of cold that comes from being carried against a body all night and still not warming. Derry had torn off the worst of the heel, the part that had gone greenish where the cloth had been damp, and handed the rest over without looking at Maren's face.

They were sitting by a stream maybe half a mile off the road, in a spot where the bank had been cut back by spring flooding and then left alone. Birch roots clawed out of the exposed dirt, pale as tendons. The water was shallow and moved without much purpose, collecting in a flat pool before finding its way through a gap in the rocks and continuing. Late summer light came through the canopy in uneven pieces. It was a good place to stop. It would have been a good place to eat.

Maren brought the bread to his mouth and his throat closed.

Not dramatically — no gagging, no heave. Just a quiet, total refusal. The muscles in his neck went tight and his jaw locked and the bread touched his lips and his body said no. The smell of it — yeast and flour and the faint sourness of age — hit his stomach and his stomach moved in a slow, oily roll and he put the bread back down on his knee.

His hands were still shaking. Not much. A fine tremor, the kind you had to look for, the kind you could hide by pressing your palms flat against your thighs. He'd been doing that since yesterday, pressing his hands into things. The bark of a tree when they'd stopped to piss. The fabric of his bag when they walked. His own knees, now, the bread balanced on top of his left thigh, untouched.

*You should eat,* Aldric said.

The voice was flat. Not the usual delivery — no lecture underneath, no implicit comparison to the dietary habits of men who had served the Order with discipline and purpose. Just the statement.

Maren didn't answer.

*Your body requires fuel. The tremor will worsen without caloric —*

*He knows,* Vess said. *Leave it.*

A silence inside his skull. The two of them pulling back to their separate corners, or whatever the equivalent was when three people occupied the same head. Maren could feel them like pressure changes — Aldric's disapproval a dull weight behind his right eye, Vess's watchfulness a cooler thing, lower, settling somewhere near the base of his skull where his neck met his shoulders.

Derry was eating. Methodically, without apparent enjoyment, tearing pieces off his half and putting them in his mouth and chewing and swallowing. His jaw worked with the steady, mechanical patience of a man who had spent his life doing necessary things without passion. He sat with his legs stretched toward the water, his boots unlaced, the sweat stains on his shirt dried to pale maps of salt. His face was red from the walking and from the heat and from something else that Maren would not look at directly.

"Pell counts everything now," Derry said.

Maren looked at him.

"She's — I don't know when it started, but it's everything. The steps to the door. The chickens. She counted my buttons the other morning, stood there in her nightshirt and pointed at each one and said the number." He tore another piece of bread. "She gets stuck on seven. She can do one through six and then she says seven and then she says seven again and then she says eight. But the second seven — she knows it's wrong, you can see it on her face, she just can't —" He chewed. Swallowed. "Lenna thinks it's because I taught her the song wrong. The counting song. I probably did. I can't remember which one has seven in a different — anyway."

"That's good," Maren said. His voice came out strange. Thin, like he was talking through cloth.

"Yeah." Derry pulled at the bread. "And the garden — you know Lenna tried herbs this year? Not the useful ones, not the ones you cook with. The other kind, the ones the apothecary buys. She read somewhere that fennel grows in anything, which is — I mean, I could've told her, fennel's a weed where I'm from, my mother couldn't keep it out of the onions. But Lenna planted it in that little bed by the south wall, the one that gets no sun because of the shed, and she was so — she had this idea about selling it at the Silt market. Dried fennel. Three coppers a bundle."

Maren watched Derry's hands on the bread. The way he held it was the way he held everything — firm, careful, the grip of a man who'd spent his life picking things up and putting them down. Turnips. Fence posts. His daughter.

"It didn't grow," Derry said. "Or it grew about an inch and then just sort of — stopped. She'd go out every morning and look at it. Just stand there. And I didn't say anything because I'm not — I mean, you know Lenna, she doesn't want to hear it from me." He ate. "But she's going to try tomatoes next year. She told me before I — before we left. She said, 'When you get back, I'll have tomatoes.' Like it was a promise she was making, but to the garden, not to me."

*His wife is growing produce in shade,* Aldric said. *That is not going to —*

*Shut up,* Vess said.

The stream made a sound like someone running their thumb along the edge of a bowl. Maren listened to it and to Derry's chewing and to the nothing that was happening inside his own body where hunger should have been. He could feel the absence of appetite like a shape — a hollow where the mechanism of eating normally sat, gummed up and stuck.

"And the cow," Derry said. He said it the way he always said it, with the particular exhausted affection of a man who has been bested by livestock. "She got out again. The day before we — the day before. I spent an hour and a half walking her back from Garron's field. An hour and a half. She doesn't run, that's the thing. She just walks. Slowly. In the wrong direction. With this look on her face like she's — like she's doing it on purpose but she wants you to know she doesn't care if you know."

Maren almost laughed. It came out as a breath through his nose, a small convulsion that died somewhere in his chest, but Derry heard it and glanced at him and something in Derry's face opened a crack. Not a smile. An easing.

"I reinforced the fence three times," Derry said. "Three times, Maren. And she just — she finds the one spot. Every time. Like she's testing the whole fence with her face and she just knows where the —"

He stopped talking. He was looking at Maren's untouched bread.

"You should eat," he said.

"I know."

"You really should."

"I know. I'm going to."

Derry didn't push it. He went back to his own bread and for a while they sat there and the stream moved and the light shifted in the canopy and the day happened around them the way days do when nothing is required and nothing is resolved. A bird did something in the birches — Maren couldn't see it, just heard the branch bounce and resettle. The air smelled like warm dirt and water and, underneath that, the faint sweetish ozone that meant quest-light was somewhere nearby. He'd smelled it his whole life and never known what it was until Aldric told him, four days ago, in a voice that made the information sound like an indictment.

*We need to discuss direction,* Aldric said.

Maren closed his eyes.

*The main road is untenable. We were visible leaving the road yesterday and anyone who finds the — who finds what happened will have a trajectory. There is a route through Selwick Pass that avoids the main road for approximately —*

*Wrong,* Vess said. *Selwick narrows at the bridge. One approach, no cover. Any tracker with half a class would position there.*

*I am not suggesting we linger at the bridge. I am suggesting the pass itself provides —*

*Terrain advantage doesn't mean anything when he can't fight.*

The word sat in Maren's skull. Fight. What had happened yesterday — his body moving without him, the dead hero's weight pulling at Maren's blade as Maren's arm, which was Aldric's arm, which was nobody's arm, drew it free — that wasn't fighting. He didn't know what it was. An event that had happened inside his body while he watched from somewhere behind his own eyes.

*He fought adequately,* Aldric said, and the correction in his tone was careful, almost gentle, which was worse than his usual disdain. *The combat instincts are sound. With practice —*

*He doesn't need practice. He needs to not be in situations where combat is the variable. We go east, stay off the roads, move at night when the interface is harder to read at distance. We find another quest pool. He eats it. We see what happens.*

*And if what happens is another fragment? Another consciousness? At what point does the accumulation —*

*That's not how it works.*

*You do not know how it works. None of us knows how it works. That is precisely my —*

*Your point is that we should understand before we act. My point is that understanding is a luxury he doesn't have. There are heroes behind us who aren't going to pause for his moral education.*

Maren opened his eyes. The stream was still there. The birch roots were still there. Derry was lacing his boots back up, his thick fingers working the leather through the eyelets with the patience of a man who laced boots every morning of his life and would lace them every morning until he was dead.

"Hey," Maren said.

Derry looked up.

"Tell me about Pell again."

Something happened in Derry's face — a complicated thing, several expressions arriving at once and then settling into one that Maren couldn't have named if pressed. Gratitude, maybe. Or just the relief of being asked to talk about something that was real and good and had nothing to do with any of this.

"She's doing this thing now," Derry said, "where she puts all her shoes in a line by the door. Not just her shoes. Everyone's shoes. She'll take mine and Lenna's and line them up smallest to biggest, and if you move one she'll put it back. She's very — she has opinions about the order. Strong opinions." He pulled the lace tight. "She's three. She's three years old and she has opinions about shoes."

Inside Maren's skull, the argument continued — Aldric making a case for finding the sin-eater community at the Crook, where proper practitioners could examine the unclassified entity and possibly stabilize it; Vess countering that approaching a gathering of sin-eaters with a bounty that size was painting a target on every practitioner in the valley. They were both right. That was the problem. Aldric's version of right was moral and long-term and required Maren to submit to examination by people who might decide the safest option was to consume him. Vess's version of right was tactical and immediate and required Maren to keep eating quests until he was strong enough that the bounty stopped mattering, which might never happen, or might make him something worse than what he already was.

He let them argue. He watched Derry lace his boots and he listened to the part about Pell and the shoes and he held the bread on his knee and he tried, again, to want to eat it.

His hands were still shaking.

---

The quest-light was in the water.

Maren saw it the way he always saw it — a faint shimmer, like heat haze, except the air wasn't hot enough and the shimmer moved against the current rather than with it. It pooled in the flat stretch of the stream where the water slowed, collecting in the still places behind rocks and in the eddies where fallen leaves turned in slow circles. To Maren it looked like moonlight would look if moonlight had weight and opinions about where to gather. He'd seen it a hundred times. A thousand. In ditches outside dungeon mouths, in the corners of rooms where someone had died, in the air above the ceramic bowl when he'd performed his fake rituals and nothing had answered.

He was standing at the edge of the stream because he'd gone to wash his hands and then forgotten to wash them. The water moved over his boots — he'd stepped in without thinking, and the cold was a clean, specific thing against his ankles, and he'd just stopped. The light was doing something in the pool. Twisting. Catching on itself.

"There's quest-light in the water," he said. Quietly, because Derry had gone to refill the water skins upstream and Maren wasn't really talking to Derry.

*I see it,* Aldric said.

A pause. Usually the reading came fast — the paladin's narration of what the interface laid over the world, the data that everyone else saw and Maren never had, delivered with the clipped rhythm of a man cataloguing someone else's mess. Threat assessment, residual quest class, contamination grade, probability of hostile emergence. The information had been useful and humiliating in roughly equal measure.

But Aldric didn't start reading.

*It is a residual pool,* he said. *Low-grade. Passive. No active quest structure, no threat emergence within — I would estimate none.* Another pause, longer. *The light is refracting through the water in a way that — I have not seen this particular configuration before. The interface shows standard residual markers, but the visual phenomenon is — separate from the data. The data says it is ordinary. The light suggests otherwise.*

Maren watched the shimmer. It moved the way light moves through glass that isn't quite flat — bending, stretching, pulling itself into shapes that had no name and didn't need one. Where it caught on the surface tension of the water it made something he could almost call beautiful, except that word had never had much use in his vocabulary and he didn't trust it now.

*The way it moves,* Aldric said, and his voice was different. Quieter. Stripped of the cadence he used for reports and corrections and the careful articulation of his ongoing disappointment. *It looks as though the light is trying to become something. Not a quest, not a threat. Something that does not have a classification. It simply moves through the water and it is — it is quite —*

He stopped.

Maren stood in the stream and the cold water ran over his boots and the quest-light turned in the pool and for a moment neither of them said anything. Not the loaded silence of disagreement or the pointed silence of withholding. Just quiet. The two of them — the con man and the dead paladin — looking at the same thing from different sides of the same skull, and the thing being, for once, not a problem to solve or a danger to assess or a failure to name.

The light twisted. It caught something in the rocks — a vein of quartz, maybe, or just a wet edge — and flared briefly, a pulse of warmth that Maren felt on his face the way you feel a candle when someone opens a door.

*It is beautiful,* Aldric said.

He said it the way he said things that cost him: completely, without qualification, and then not again.

Vess said nothing. Whether she could see through Maren's eyes the way Aldric described seeing, Maren didn't know. She hadn't offered, and he hadn't asked. Her silence now was neither agreement nor dismissal. It was the silence of someone watching someone else have a private moment and choosing not to be in it.

Maren stood in the water until his feet went numb. The light continued its slow, purposeless work in the pool. Upstream, he could hear Derry filling the water skins, the leather gulping and then the sound of him shaking the last drops off and capping them. Ordinary sounds. The sounds of a man doing a small task well because it was the task in front of him.

He looked down at his hands. They were in the water now — he'd let them hang at his sides and the stream had taken them, the cold wrapping around his fingers, his wrists, pulling the heat out of him in a way that felt less like discomfort and more like an offer. His hands were still shaking, but the water made the shaking harder to see, and that was enough. That was something.

He bent down and scooped water to his mouth and drank. It tasted like stone and cold and the faint sweet chemical edge of whatever quest-light left behind when it sat in water long enough. He drank again. His throat opened for the water the way it hadn't opened for the bread. It wasn't food, but it was something going in instead of everything coming up, and his body accepted it with the desperate, uncritical gratitude of a system that had been locked for a full day.

When he walked back to where they'd been sitting, Derry was repacking the bag. The bread was still on the ground where Maren had left it. Derry looked at it, looked at Maren, and put it in the bag without comment.

"East, I think," Maren said. "Off the road. Vess says there's better cover."

Derry nodded. He didn't ask who Vess was. He'd stopped asking about the voices after the second day, the way you stop asking about a neighbor's dog that barks all night — not because you've accepted it, but because the asking doesn't change anything.

*Acceptable,* Vess said.

*I did not agree to east,* Aldric said.

*You don't have legs,* Vess said. *You agreed when he did.*

They walked. The stream fell behind them and the quest-light stayed where it was, turning in the water, becoming nothing, meaning nothing, and Maren thought about it for a long time after they'd gone — the way it had looked, the way Aldric's voice had sounded saying the word beautiful, as if the word were something he'd put away in a drawer years ago and had just found again, surprised that it still fit in his mouth.

Derry talked as they walked. About the cow, again — a different escape, this time involving a neighbor's cart and a turnip field and a complicated chain of events that ended with Derry standing in the rain holding a rope attached to nothing because the cow had slipped the knot. About Lenna's plans for the front room, which involved moving a wall that Derry had built with his own hands three years ago and which he maintained was structurally important and which Lenna maintained was in the way. About Pell's new word, which was "no," and which she deployed with the conviction and frequency of someone who had just discovered the concept of refusal.

Maren listened. He let Derry's voice fill the space that the bread couldn't and the argument in his skull wouldn't, and he walked and he listened and the day went on and nothing happened and nothing was resolved and they were still alive, which was the only thing that mattered and the thing that mattered least.


# Chapter 7

Silt smelled like tallow and horse piss, which made it the largest settlement Maren had entered in six days.

The market town sat half a mile off the main road, down a packed-earth track rutted by cart wheels and scored with scorch lines from some hero who'd apparently discharged a fire-class ability while doing their shopping. The buildings were wood and daub, taller than Fenhold's cottages — two stories in places, leaning toward each other across narrow lanes like old men sharing a fence post. Late-summer sun pressed the smells flat against the ground: a tanner's vat somewhere behind the square, meat turning on a spit outside an inn whose sign had been charred off by quest-light residue, chickens making problems for a boy who clearly had opinions about better uses for his afternoon.

*Three hero-class signatures within four hundred yards,* Aldric said. *None currently targeting. The bounty overlay remains standard visibility — they would need to search for it specifically.*

*Two at the inn, one at the far stall,* Vess added. *The one at the stall is drunk. Afternoon drunk. Not a threat unless you fall on her.*

"Fine," Maren said, to no one visible.

Derry winced. "You've got to stop doing that."

"Doing what?"

"Answering them out loud. In public." He wiped sweat from his forehead with the heel of his hand. His shirt was already dark under the arms, and there was a mended tear at the shoulder — Lenna's work, tight small stitches — that he kept brushing at with his fingers, a gesture Maren had seen a hundred times on the road and never once in Fenhold. "People notice, Maren."

"Right. Yeah." Maren kept his voice low, moving between market stalls where a woman sold bruised apples and another sold linen that had clearly been curtains in a previous life.

The sin-eater. That was why they were here — a real one, the kind who actually cleared contamination instead of performing clearance while the contamination sat untouched in the soil underneath. If anyone could explain what the fragments were doing, what the thing Aldric wouldn't name was becoming, it was someone who'd spent a lifetime doing what Maren had spent a lifetime pretending to do.

He asked the apple seller. "The sin-eater. The woman who works the contamination routes. Where do I find her?"

The woman gave him a look he recognized — mild distaste, mild discomfort, the expression people wore when reminded that the toilet needed cleaning. "Thessa? She's out at the Vonn farmstead. Clearing contamination, if she hasn't finished already. Back past the stream, left at the dead oak." She picked up a bruised apple and turned it in her hands, looking for a side worth showing. "She works fast. You wanting a clearing done?"

"Something like that."

*The Vonn farmstead registered moderate contamination density this morning,* Aldric said. *If she has been working since dawn —*

*It'll be handled,* Vess said. *Obviously.*

*I was providing context.*

*You were providing the same thing with more words around it.*

They found the dead oak — a massive trunk split by old lightning, blackened on one side, the other still pushing out leaves as if half a tree was enough. Past it, a track led down to flatlands where a farmstead sat behind a low stone wall, barley fields half-harvested beyond, the stubble golden in afternoon light. Quest-light residue pooled in the low weeds along the track — pale, faintly warm, sitting in the ditches the way it always did near settlements. Maren had walked past pools like these his whole life. After eight days of Aldric reading the data overlaid on every surface, they didn't look the same. They had depth. They had information he couldn't read. They had consequences he'd spent eleven years ignoring.

"Maybe we should talk about what to tell her," Derry said. He'd been reaching for the back of his neck with his farmer's hand, a gesture that left the skin there red and rubbed. "How much does she need to know? Because if she finds out about the whole — the eleven years —"

"She's a sin-eater, Derry. A real one. She's going to see."

"Right, but can't we just — maybe start with the fragments? Without the rest of it? Just say there was an accident and —"

"Yeah. I'll handle it."

He wouldn't handle it. He already knew that. The same instinct that could read Derry's discomfort, the apple seller's distaste, every family who'd ever hired him — that instinct told him that a woman who'd spent thirty years doing the real work would strip any shaped truth he offered the way she stripped contamination from soil. She'd strip it because stripping was what she did.

Derry was quiet after that, which was worse than his talking. When Derry went quiet, it meant he was thinking about Pell, and when he thought about Pell, the fact that he'd left her sat in his face like something visible — the jaw set, the eyes down, the fingers working at the mended shoulder of his shirt.

Someone was working in the nearest field.

Maren saw her from fifty yards. Tall, angular, grey hair cut short enough that it didn't move in the breeze. She was kneeling in the dirt between stubble rows, her hands pressed flat against the ground, and even from this distance the staining showed — dark, mottled, the color of old tea or dried blood, worked so deep into the skin it had become permanent. She'd been doing this long enough that the work had marked her.

He looked at his own hands. Clean.
