*She is performing a second-stage residual extraction,* Aldric said, and his voice had shifted — lower, more careful, the register he used for things that earned his respect. *The contamination density around this farmstead has dropped significantly since we arrived. She is efficient.*

*She's good,* Vess said. Which was the closest thing to a compliment Vess had offered anyone.

Thessa stood without brushing the dirt from her knees. She looked at Maren and Derry at the edge of her field, and her expression went through something — not recognition of their faces but of their type. Then her eyes narrowed, her head tilting slightly, like someone catching a frequency that shouldn't be there.

"Stay where you are," she said.

She walked toward them with the stride of someone who didn't waste steps — each foot landing flat, deliberate, the gait of decades on contaminated terrain. Up close, the staining went past her wrists in mottled patterns that looked burned in rather than absorbed. Her face was weathered deep, lines cut around her eyes and mouth, and the tiredness in it didn't look temporary. It had gone into the bones, permanent as the staining on her hands.

She stopped six feet from Maren. Her nostrils flared once.

"What are you carrying?"

Maren had rehearsed something on the walk over — a version of the truth carefully shaped to hide the worst parts. Something about an unusual consumption, fragments he couldn't explain. A story with the bones of honesty and the flesh of misdirection, the kind he'd been constructing for eleven years.

Thessa held up one stained hand. "Don't bother."

She stepped closer. Her eyes moved over him — not his face but his chest, his hands, the air around him — reading something he couldn't perceive. The hum was louder here than it had been on the road. Or maybe a real sin-eater's proximity made things vibrate differently.

"Two fragments," she said. "Paladin-class and shadow-class. Opposed." Her voice was clipped, clinical — a surgeon cataloguing damage. "Lodged. Not dissolving." She looked at his hands again. Clean hands. "And something underneath them. Something that doesn't —" She stopped. Her expression changed for the first time: not fear, but the controlled alertness of a professional encountering a problem she'd only ever heard described. "That. Come with me."

She walked past them toward the road without checking whether they followed. Maren followed because the alternative was standing in a barley field knowing that a woman who'd spent three seconds assessing him had already learned more about his condition than he'd managed in eight days of living with it.

Derry followed Maren. His fingers found the mended tear at his shoulder again.

---

Thessa's house sat where Silt thinned into scrub and the track met open ground. One room below, a loft above, and it smelled like the work — that ozone-sweet contamination smell, but older, layered into the walls and the wooden floor and the single table where ritual tools sat in a careful row. Three clay bowls, each stained darker than the last. A grinding stone with a worn channel down its center. Glass bottles holding liquids Maren couldn't identify — amber, grey-green, one the color of a deep bruise. The floor in front of the cold hearth was worn smooth, the wood lighter than the rest, a rectangle of use where someone had knelt thousands of times.

"Sit." She pointed at a bench against the wall. Maren sat. Derry stayed by the door, gripping the frame with both hands, his breathing shallow and quick.

Thessa pulled a stool to the center of the room and sat facing Maren. Her stained hands rested on her knees. Afternoon light through the single window caught the grey in her hair.

"Show me your hands."

Maren held them out, palms up. Clean. Unmarked. The skin was rough from travel and from years of carrying equipment, but none of the mottled staining that covered Thessa's to the wrists. Nothing that showed the work had ever touched him.

She held one of her own hands beside his, palm to palm without contact. The contrast didn't need explanation — hers dark with thirty years of absorbed contamination, his showing nothing but the ordinary calluses of a man who'd carried a prop and walked a lot. She dropped his hand.

"Where's your equipment?"

"I've got a bowl. Ceramic. In my pack."

"One bowl."

"Yeah."

She glanced at her table — three bowls, darkest to lightest, each in its place. "Active contamination, residual work, structural assessment. Different grades need different vessels." She looked back at him. "But you wouldn't know that."

Maren said nothing. There was nothing to say that wouldn't make it worse.

"How long have you been working?"

"Eleven years."

"Where?"

"Fenhold, and the villages around it. Dunmore, Cress, the settlements along the —"

"I know the area." Her voice flattened. "I've worked the eastern routes for thirty years. Including the overflow from certified sin-eaters who supposedly handled their zones." She studied him. "Tell me your consumption sequence."

This was the question he could almost answer. He'd performed the fake version so many times the order was muscle memory — the bowl placement, the kneeling posture, the words spoken in the right cadence. "You set the bowl at the body's head. Kneel at the left side. The opening invocation, standard form, three repetitions. Then the hand positions, flat against the contamination line. The consumption pass —"

"Which hand?"

"What?"

"Which hand opens the containment seal before the consumption pass?"

He didn't know. He'd never known. The hand positions in his fake ritual were theatrical — they looked right because he'd studied what sin-eaters looked like from the outside, not what they did. "The — the right."

"Left." Thessa's voice closed like a door. "The left hand opens. The right holds the seal. Every sin-eater learns this in the first week because getting it backward channels contamination through the dominant hand and into the body instead of the bowl." She looked at his hands. "You've been channeling backward for eleven years."

"I haven't been channeling at all."

The words came out before he could stop them — the specific, self-destructive honesty that surfaced when his performance infrastructure collapsed. Derry made a sound by the door, small and strangled.

Thessa went very still.

"Show me a containment pass," she said.

Nothing. His hands stayed on his knees.

"A residual clear."

Nothing.

"A density assessment."

Each term fell into the silence between them, and each silence confirmed what Thessa already knew.

"Name the three contamination grades."

"Active, residual, and —" He knew this one. He'd heard the words. The third sat just out of reach, a shape he could feel but not name.

"Structural." Thessa set the word down between them like a stone. "Active, residual, structural. Foundational classification. Governs every assessment and every consumption."

She waited. The silence stretched. Then:

"What does structural contamination smell like?"

Maren knew the general contamination smell — everyone who'd lived near a dungeon mouth knew it. That ozone-sweet thickness with the undertone of decay. "It's — sweet, with a kind of —"

"That's active contamination. The smell you know." Thessa's voice had the cadence of someone reciting knowledge she'd taught before, to students who'd actually been worth teaching. "Residual smells drier, like burnt grain. Structural has no smell at all. It's been in the ground so long it becomes the ground. You can only identify it through the ritual assessment." She paused. "But you don't do ritual assessments."

She let that sit for a moment. Maren had nothing to offer into the silence.

"How do you assess whether a contamination site is safe to consume?"

Nothing. No guess, no bluff. Not even a fragment of overheard language to shape into an answer.

"You check the layering. Old contamination stratifies — recent deposits sit on top, older material compresses underneath. If the deepest layer has gone structural, you can't consume from the top down. You cut in from the boundary edges, work the perimeter first, then core the center." She recited this with the fluency of ten thousand repetitions, each word worn smooth. "Consuming from the top on a stratified site pulls the structural layer up through the active layer and into the sin-eater. That's how we die. Not from heroics. Not from quests. From taking a contamination in the wrong order because we were rushing or tired or didn't check the layering first."

She looked at his clean hands again, as if they were evidence she kept having to re-examine, each time finding the same absence.

Maren looked at the floor. At the rectangle worn into the wood by genuine practice. His own knees had worn nothing anywhere, left no mark on anything. Eleven years of kneeling beside bodies and the only evidence was clean hands and a ceramic bowl that had never held anything real.

"You're not a sin-eater," Thessa said. No question. The sky is blue. Water runs downhill.

"No."

"You never were."

"No."

*She is correct,* Aldric said. Nothing else. No qualification, no irony, no addendum.

"A hundred rituals," Thessa said. "More?"

"More."

"And zero consumptions. Every family that paid you, every farmer who died in his field and hired you to clear the ground before the contamination spoiled the crop — you took the money, performed the gestures, and left the contamination exactly where it was."

"Yes."

She stood. She walked to the table and picked up the darkest bowl — the one that looked like it had absorbed thirty years of practice — and held it in both hands. She didn't look at him.

"Do you know what residual contamination does to soil?"

"Thessa —"

"After one season, yield drops by a third. After two seasons, the barley comes up thin and the wheat gets a grey rot that no amount of turning fixes. After five years, the soil holds contamination the way wood holds smoke. You can't plant. You can't graze. The land is done." She set the bowl down with the precision she brought to everything. "It doesn't clear on its own. It accumulates. It concentrates. It sinks into the soil and the water and the food and the people."

Maren's fingers had found the fabric of his trousers and were digging in, knuckles white against the rough-planed bench. A splinter caught at the seam above his knee. He counted the bottles on the table. Six. Amber, grey-green, bruise-purple, three others whose colors he couldn't name. He counted them again.

"You worked Dunmore," Thessa said.

"Yes."

"I cleared Dunmore two years ago. Supposed to be routine maintenance. Instead I found contamination so old it had gone structural — into the water table, into the foundations of the houses. People sleeping in it every night." She paused — not for effect, but with the control of someone keeping fury subordinate to precision. "There's a girl in Dunmore. Maret. Nine years old. She can't hold a cup because her hands shake so badly her mother has to feed her."

Six bottles. Splinter in the bench. The worn rectangle on the floor. He was listing things because listing was what his mind did when the thing in front of him was too large to hold.

"Her contamination exposure started before she was born," Thessa continued. "Her mother carried her in contaminated soil. The soil was contaminated because the certified sin-eater never cleared it."

*She's right,* Vess said. Flatly, without comfort. *About all of it.*

Derry had slid down the doorframe to the floor. Not dramatically — his legs just stopped holding, and he sat on the threshold with his boots on the dirt outside and his back against the wood and his face the color of old paste. He'd been in Dunmore. He'd watched those rituals. He'd fed Maren the System details that made the performance look real, and afterward they'd split the coins and gone to the inn.

"You worked Cress," Thessa said.

"Twice. Maybe three times."

"The Holloway farm outside Cress lost six acres to contamination blight three years ago. The farmer, Eddin Holloway, had worked that land for twenty years. He moved his family to Harrow after the blight. His youngest boy has the tremors."

Six acres. Twenty years. A boy whose hands won't stay still.

"Fenhold," Thessa said.

Maren's jaw set, teeth together, the bench's grain pressing into the backs of his thighs.

"A farmer died in Fenhold recently. Contamination exposure. I hadn't reached the eastern route yet this season — too much work, not enough of me, there's never enough of me." She sat back on her stool. "How long has the contamination been building in Fenhold?"

"Eleven years."

"Eleven harvests. Eleven years of families living in residue that should have been consumed after every quest event in the area." She looked at him. "That farmer. Did you do his ritual?"

"Yes."

"And when you performed your fake ritual over the body of a man who may have died because you never did your job — did anything happen?"

"Something answered." His voice had gone wrong — stripped, the voice of someone whose entire performance infrastructure had been dismantled, leaving only the raw thing underneath. "A fragment. A paladin. That's why I'm here. That's why I came to find you."

Thessa studied him the way she'd studied the contamination in the field — a problem to be assessed and, if possible, resolved.

"You came to me," she said, "because in eleven years of fraud, you finally ate something real, and now you're scared."

"Yes."

---

The silence had weight. Maren sat in it without reaching for deflection or humor or any of the machinery that had carried him through a decade of professional lying. None of it worked on Thessa. She wasn't an audience to be charmed. She was a professional measuring damage.

"Help me," Maren said. "Teach me. If I can learn to actually consume — do the real work — maybe I can stabilize the fragments, or contain the —"

"No."

"The thing underneath them, the unclassified — you said you've heard of it. If you know what it is, how to —"

"I said no." Thessa folded her arms — not defensively, but the way someone holds themselves still when their hands want to do something they shouldn't. "You want me to train a man who faked my profession for eleven years. Whose fraud left contamination in villages where children can't hold a cup." She looked down at him on the bench. "Even if I wanted to help you — and I don't — you're too dangerous. The thing underneath your fragments is growing. I can feel it from here, sitting across the room from you. If I open you up with a genuine consumption technique and that thing expands, it could contaminate everything within a mile of this house. I'm not risking my territory for a man who spent a decade poisoning his."

"I'm not asking you to consume me. Just the basics — the real sequence, the hand positions, the —"

"The hand positions." Her voice was flat as a blade laid on a table. "You want me to start from the hand positions. With a man carrying two opposed-classification fragments and an unclassified entity that the System has already placed a correction bounty on." She shook her head. "I've read two accounts of what you're carrying. One from my teacher, who heard it from hers. One from a text so old the binding was dust. Neither described the mechanism — only the result."

"Then tell me the result. Give me something I can use."

"I'm giving you the only thing I have." She walked to the door. "You don't have time for hand positions."

"What does that mean?"

She opened the door. Late-afternoon light spilled across the threshold, catching the dust in the air. "Unclassified entities don't last. The System treats them as errors — not threats, errors. Things that shouldn't exist within the classification structure." She gripped the doorframe, and the staining on her hands stood dark against the pale wood. "The last recorded instance was roughly sixty years ago. A woman in the northern territories. Opposed fragments, accidental consumption, same as you. The System reclassified the area around her as a high-priority threat zone. Contamination spiked. Threat creatures spawned from the nearest dungeon mouths. Heroes converged on the zone."

*Go on,* Aldric said, very quietly.

"Nothing remained of the host." Thessa's voice was clinical, the anger from the examination replaced by something colder — professional assessment of a situation she considered unsalvageable. "The System didn't send heroes to kill her. It changed the territory. Made the ground around her incompatible with human habitation and let the correction happen through environmental pressure. The heroes who showed up were responding to the threat zone, not to her. She was collateral damage in the System correcting its own architecture."

The room was very still. Derry had pulled himself back to standing but he was pressed against the wall by the door, his mouth working around words that wouldn't form.

"How long?" Maren asked. "From when the thing formed to the correction. How long did she have?"

"The text didn't specify. Weeks, perhaps. Months at most." Thessa stepped back from the doorway. "If you want help, find a sin-eater who hasn't spent thirty years cleaning up after you. I'm done."

Maren stood because standing was something he could do. His legs held, which felt like a minor accomplishment.

"The girl in Dunmore," Thessa said as he moved toward the door. "Maret. She's named for her grandmother, who grew up on the same land before you ever touched it. The grandmother could hold a cup just fine."

Maren walked out into the street. Derry followed, shoulders hunched, the mended tear at his shoulder pulling where his posture strained the stitching. Behind them, Thessa's door closed — not slammed, because Thessa didn't slam anything. It closed with the finality of something that had nothing left to give.

They walked through the emptying market square — the apple seller packing up her bruised inventory, the linen woman folding her table, the boy and his chickens gone home. The light had gone gold and low, shadows reaching across packed earth, and the hum underneath everything was the same as always, the same as it had been for twenty-eight years. Maren had just never known what it was holding.

His legs worked. That was something. After the bench in Thessa's house, after sitting under the weight of named villages and numbered years and a girl who couldn't hold a cup, the simple mechanical process of walking felt almost generous. Left foot, right foot, the packed dirt solid under each step, the evening air warm but losing its heat. He'd walked out of worse than this — out of rituals over dead bodies, out of grieving families' houses with their fifteen coppers in his pocket, out of every village he'd ever failed to clear. He'd never thought about the walking before. He thought about it now because thinking about the walking was easier than thinking about Maret.

*She is right about the correction timeline,* Aldric said. His voice had the quality of someone delivering a report he wished contained different information. *The Order's records described similar patterns. Anomalies identified, territories destabilized, hosts removed. The corrections were always territorial — the System changed the ground and let the ground do the work. I have no reason to doubt her account.*

*How long do we have?* Vess asked. The question was aimed at Aldric, not Maren.

*I do not know. The Order's records were incomplete — they described outcomes, not intervals. The time between formation and correction was never specified clearly.*

*Then we assume weeks,* Vess said. *And we move.*

They reached the edge of Silt, where the town thinned into scrub and the track met the main road east. Maren stopped. He looked at his hands. Still clean. The only marks on them were calluses from years of carrying a ceramic bowl that had never done anything.

"Maren," Derry said. His voice was small, wrung out, the voice of a man sitting with the knowledge that he'd helped. He'd fed Maren the System cues. He'd watched the rituals. He'd taken his half of the fee. "That girl. Maret. We went to Dunmore, I remember the — did we do that to her?"

"Yeah."

"And the farmer. The Holloway farm in Cress. Was that —"

"Yeah."

Derry pressed his hand over his mouth. Held it there. Took it away. His eyes were wet and he was looking at the road and there wasn't anything to say about a girl who couldn't hold a cup and a boy with tremors and a dead farmer in Fenhold and eleven years of fake rituals performed over real bodies in real villages where real contamination sat in the soil and the water and the children while Maren and Derry split fifteen coppers and went to the inn.

They walked east. The road ahead was long and dust-colored and empty. Behind them, Silt's single column of chimney smoke rose into the late-summer sky. Inside Maren's skull, three voices were quiet, and the thing underneath the voices — the nameless, unclassified thing the System would eventually correct — waited.

Fifteen hundred coppers. A girl named Maret. Eleven years.

Maren walked.


# Chapter 8

The road past Silt smelled different. Not the sharp ozone-sweet of fresh contamination — Maren knew that one, had walked through enough of it faking his way across Fenhold's farmland — but something older, settled into the dirt like grease in a cookpot. Decay with a sweetness underneath, and under that a mineral flatness that stuck to the roof of his mouth. He breathed through it because there was no alternative. The road went forward and the smell went with it.

Scorch marks darkened the road stones every few hundred paces now. Some old enough that grass had crept into the blackened edges, others fresh — the surface still glossy, still faintly warm underfoot in the afternoon heat. Heroes had been through here. Were through here regularly, if the layering was anything to go by. Maren stepped around the marks without thinking about it, the way you step around puddles, and the hum was louder out here. Not painful. Present. A low steady drone that sat behind his teeth and wouldn't leave.

Derry walked ten paces ahead, his pack hitched too high on his shoulders. He'd been adjusting the straps since they'd left the outskirts of Silt — pulling at the leather, letting it go, pulling again — and Maren recognized the gesture. It was the thing Derry did when he wanted to talk but hadn't decided what to say yet. The strap adjustment was a placeholder for conversation. It meant he was thinking about Pell, or Lenna, or the cow, or all three, and wasn't sure Maren could handle hearing about any of them right now.

He was probably right.

*Your pace has dropped,* Aldric said. *We are losing daylight.*

Maren didn't answer. He was watching the quest-light in the ditch — a thin sheet of pale luminescence pooled in the low ground the way rainwater pooled after a storm, except rainwater didn't glow and wasn't warm. He could see it even without the interface. Everyone could. The difference was that the System-sighted saw it with data overlaid — quest details, threat levels, completion percentages — and Maren saw the raw thing. A soft, faintly golden shimmer, like light caught in shallow water. It was beautiful if you didn't know what it meant.

He'd been thinking about Thessa. Not the insults — those had landed where they belonged, heavy and specific and earned. Eleven years of contamination in named villages, children growing up in the residue of quests he'd only pretended to clear. That weight sat where it sat. He wasn't going to put it down.

The other part, though. The warning. Unclassified entities didn't last. The System corrected them. Sixty years since the last one, and nothing left of the host. Thessa had said it the way she said everything — clinically, as if the fact of his approaching dissolution was a drainage problem and she was describing the pipe.

Which meant whatever he was carrying was on the same clock he was.

"Vess."

No response. That was standard. She treated the first use of her name as a notification, not a summons. She'd respond when she was ready, or when the silence had lasted long enough to constitute acknowledgment.

"The contamination in that hollow. The quest I — the one that brought you in."

*What about it.*

"What was it? The quest itself. Before it went bad, before it pooled — what was the quest?"

The pause lasted six steps. Maren counted them. Vess's pauses were measured things — not hesitation but assessment, the silence of someone deciding how much information to declassify.

*A bounty quest,* she said. *Anchored to the holdings of a minor lord. Torren Blackhall. He held land east of the Silt road, maybe two days' walk from where your hollow was. He'd purchased quest access for the territory around his estate.*

"Purchased."

*Landholders can buy quest generation for their territory. The System creates quests, heroes come through, spend money, generate trade. Blackhall's holdings were close enough to the dungeon approaches that the purchase was cheap. Standard practice for minor nobility.*

Maren didn't know that. Eleven years of performing a System role and half the basic mechanics were news to him. The fraud had been so thorough that its gaps were showing up now like cracks in plaster — everywhere, and all at once.

"So the quest was his. Generated on his land. And it went bad?"

*It didn't go bad. He used it.*

"Used it how?"

*There were tenant villages on the eastern edge of his holdings. Forty or fifty families, mostly agricultural labor. Timber workers, some of them. They'd disputed his cutting rights for two growing seasons. There was a boundary disagreement — whose forest, whose trees, whose profit. The kind of thing that goes to a magistrate in most holdings. Blackhall didn't take it to a magistrate.*

Derry had stopped ahead, waiting where the road bent around a stand of scrub oak. His silhouette was a stocky question mark against the yellowing sky.

"You coming?" he called.

"Yeah. Just — give me a minute."

"You're talking to them again."

"Yeah."

Derry nodded. He sat down on a flat stone by the road's edge and pulled his water skin from his pack. He drank, wiped his mouth, drank again. He didn't ask what the voices were saying. He'd stopped asking after Silt.

Maren kept walking, slower now, closing the gap between them but not all the way. Close enough that Derry could see him. Far enough that talking to himself looked like mumbling instead of conversation.

"Blackhall killed them," he said to Vess. Not a question.

*He hired mercenaries. A company of eight. They went through the eastern villages over three nights. The quest activity on his land generated enough System noise — bounty notifications, threat assessments, the normal chatter of an active quest zone — to mask the contamination signatures of what happened. Anyone checking the area afterward would read the deaths as quest casualties. Dungeon-adjacent attrition. Common enough that no one investigates.*

The words arrived in Vess's usual register — flat, precise, billing by the syllable. She could have been describing supply routes or weather patterns. That was her voice. The voice of a woman who'd been trained to separate information from feeling, to deliver operational data clean.

"Fifty families."

*Approximately. The contract didn't specify an exact count. I wasn't hired for the aftermath. I was hired for Blackhall.*

"The Nightguild."

*Yes.*

"You were going to kill him."

*I was going to complete the contract. A client in one of the surviving settlements had reached the guild through the standard channels. Payment was adequate. The target was accessible. I had his schedule within a week of deployment. I had the estate mapped in ten days. The purchased quest gave me operational cover — I could move through the contamination zone without triggering secondary System alerts because the zone was already noisy with quest activity. The same trick Blackhall used to hide the killing. His own purchased cover, turned against him.*

There was something in the cadence that Maren couldn't place. Not emotion — Vess didn't traffic in audible emotion. But a structural wrongness, like a sentence built with load-bearing walls and some of the walls were hollow.

"What happened?"

*I was inside the quest's operational radius. Moving through the contamination perimeter at the edge of the estate. Two days from completion. The approach was clean. Everything was — the approach was clean.* A pause that wasn't tactical. *And then I was inside you. The interval between those two states isn't present. I don't remember the consumption.*

"A gap."

*The memory isn't degraded. It's absent. The distinction matters. Degraded memory is fog — I had a head injury at fifteen, lost three weeks of training, and those weeks feel like looking through dirty glass. The details are present but distorted. This isn't that. This is a room that's been emptied.*

Maren reached the bend in the road. Derry was still sitting on his stone, water skin balanced on his knee, watching a beetle navigate the road dust between his boots. He glanced up when Maren stopped.

"Sausage?" Derry said. He held up a leathery coil. "Got it in Silt. Vendor charged too much but it's not bad if you pull the casing off."

"Maybe later."

"You look like you're about to be sick."

"I'm fine."

"That's what you said before the ditch outside Fenhold."

"Different ditch."

Derry didn't push. He put the sausage back in his pack and went back to watching the beetle. It had reached a scorch mark and was navigating the glossy black edge with the careful deliberation of something that didn't understand the obstacle but was going to get past it anyway.

Maren sat down on the road's edge, close enough to Derry that the silence between them was companionable rather than strained. The quest-light in the ditch beside him glowed faintly. The hum droned. The sun was low enough that the scrub oak cast long shadows across the road, and in the shadows the scorch marks were invisible, which was a small, stupid mercy.

"Vess."

*Yes.*

"You had a sister."

The silence that followed was unlike anything Maren had heard from her before. Not assessment. Not tactical calibration. Something mechanical encountering an obstacle that its design didn't account for.

He hadn't planned to say it. It came from a conversation days earlier — one of the fragments' arguments, Vess and Aldric sniping at each other while Maren tried to eat, and she'd said something sharp about the Order of the Veil and then, in the same flat register, had mentioned a sister. One word. Then she'd moved on. Maren had filed it automatically — the con man's reflex, cataloguing every detail because detail was currency — and now the word had come out of him like something he'd been holding in his mouth.

*I had a sister.* A beat. *Have. Had. The tense is unclear.*

"What was her name?"

*I —*

The pause stretched. Not the kind Vess controlled. Something worse: the silence of a mechanism reaching for a function that had been removed.

*The name starts with — there should be a sound here. I can feel the shape of it. Two syllables. The first is open, like a vowel sound, and the second is — it closes. I can feel where it was. The space it occupied. I can tell you that I knew it better than any name I've ever known, better than Blackhall's, better than my own, and it isn't here.*

Maren's hands were flat on the warm road stone. He pressed them harder against the surface. The stone was rough, gritty, real.

*I can give you the distance from Blackhall's bedroom to the servants' staircase,* Vess said. *Fourteen feet. I can give you his steward's name and his breakfast preferences and the rotation schedule for his perimeter guard. I can tell you the precise weight of the blade I carried and the angle it enters between the third and fourth rib. All of that is here. Complete. My sister's name isn't.*

*She is describing selective absence,* Aldric said. His voice had gone stiff — stiffer than his usual formality, which for Aldric meant the architecture had gone load-bearing. He was processing.

"I didn't ask you."

*No. But the pattern is relevant.*

"In a minute."

"Vess. The massacre. When you — when you talk about the families. The fifty families. You don't sound —"

*Angry. I know. I was angry. I can tell you that I was angry. The anger was — it was the reason I took the contract. Not the money. Not the guild's assignment protocols. This one was personal. The villages were — they were —*

She stopped. Not the way she stopped when she chose to stop. The sentence didn't trail off. It hit a wall.

*The anger starts,* she said, and her voice was the same flat, measured voice it always was, and that was what made it horrible. *I can feel it begin. The beginning of the feeling is present. And then it stops. Not fades. Stops. The way a sound stops when you close a door. Except I didn't close it. The feeling was there, and then it was taken, and what remains is the knowledge that I had it. I can observe the room where the feeling was. I can see the door. I can't enter it.*

The beetle had reached the other side of the scorch mark. Derry was still watching it, seemingly absorbed, but his jaw was set in the way it set when he was pretending not to listen to something that frightened him.

Maren breathed. The contamination smell was in his mouth and in his throat and the hum was steady and the quest-light in the ditch glowed its soft, indifferent gold.

Vess wasn't a tool. She wasn't a tactical readout with a voice. She was a woman who had taken a contract to kill a man who'd massacred fifty families, and she'd taken it for reasons she could no longer feel, and she had a sister whose name occupied a space in her memory shaped exactly like a name but containing nothing.

*I have them too,* Aldric said. Quiet. Formal. The voice of a man filing a report he didn't want to file. *The gaps.*

---

They camped in a hollow off the road where the scrub oak thinned enough to see the sky. Derry built the fire — small, careful, the fire of a man who'd been sleeping outdoors for a week and had gotten efficient about it. He cut sausage with his belt knife, ate slowly, stared at the flames with the particular distance of someone whose thoughts were two hundred miles away and centered on a girl who was learning to count.

He offered Maren a piece. Maren took it. The casing was tough and the meat was too salty and it was fine.

"Tomorrow's worse," Derry said. "More patrols. Some of the roads have checkpoints, or they used to — my cousin went through here four years ago and said there were heroes just sitting at the crossroads, scanning everyone who passed."

"Scanning."

"Checking profiles. Class, level, party affiliation. The whole thing. And you've got —"

"Nothing."

"I was going to say a situation." Derry cut another piece of sausage and inspected it in the firelight. "You've got a situation."

"I've got a situation."

"Right. So tomorrow, you're the cousin. Quiet. Head injury. I'll — we'll figure it out." He ate the sausage. "Lenna always said I was a bad liar but honestly I think I'm all right when it's — you know. When it matters."

"You're a terrible liar."

"Yeah." He almost smiled. "Yeah, I know."

The fire popped. Something moved in the scrub oak — an animal, probably, or the wind rearranging branches — and Maren felt Vess's tactical assessment ripple through his awareness, a brief sharp scan that catalogued the direction, distance, and threat probability of the sound before dismissing it. Automatic. A reflex from a woman who couldn't feel her own anger but could calculate threat vectors in her sleep.

They sat without talking. Derry finished his sausage, wiped his knife on his trousers, folded it, and put it in his pocket. He leaned back against his pack and closed his eyes. Not sleeping yet — Maren could tell from his breathing — but making the shape of sleep, the way you make the shape of something when you want it to come.

"Aldric."

*Yes.*

"The gaps."

The pause was long enough that Maren almost spoke again. But Aldric's pauses weren't evasion — they were construction. He was building the answer to his own specifications before he'd release it.

*My service record is complete,* he said. *Fourteen years with the Order of the Veil. I can provide the name of every member of my cohort, every deployment, every site investigation. My training in System theory is intact. The research Commander Dellan authorized — the investigation into structural anomalies in the System's deeper architecture — I remember the commission, the scope, the findings.*

"But."

*I remember the dungeon. The one where I died. I remember the briefing, such as it was. The approach march. The entry. The first chamber — standard layout, moderate contamination, residual quest activity from a previous clearing. I remember the hum there. It was different. Lower, more sustained. A harmonic I had not heard in any previous site.*

He paused again. When he continued, his syntax was tighter, more clipped — not loosening but compressing, the formal architecture contracting under weight.

*I remember reaching the second chamber. The wall. Except it was not the wall of the dungeon. What I was looking at was — the System's architecture was visible. Not the interface, not the overlaid data. The structure beneath. I remember seeing it. I remember that I understood something about what I was seeing. I remember the understanding arriving, the shape of it, the magnitude. It was —*

He stopped.

Aldric completed every sentence. It was a defining feature of his voice, as fixed as Vess's flat delivery or Derry's trailing qualifications. When Aldric started a sentence, it ended. Properly, grammatically, with the finality of a door closing. That was who he was. A man of complete sentences.

*I do not remember what I understood,* he said. *I remember that the understanding was there. I remember its weight. And then the memory ends. Not fades, not degrades. It has an edge. A clean edge. If I reach past it, there is nothing, and the nothing is — it is not empty. It is emptied. There is a difference.*

"Like Vess's."

*Yes. The quality is the same. The edge is too precise for natural degradation. Fragment memory does degrade — I have experienced it in smaller ways, peripheral details that blur over time. This is not that. This has the character of removal. Something was there, and it was taken, and what remains is the exact negative space of what was taken.*

Derry's breathing had evened out. He'd fallen asleep with his head on his pack and his mouth slightly open. In the firelight his face was slack, younger, scrubbed of the worry that had been tightening it since they'd left Fenhold. He looked like what he was — a farmer, thirty years old, who should have been home.

*My death,* Aldric said. The word came out with the specific precision of a man who had trained himself never to flinch from a report, even when the report was about himself. *The understanding I reached, and then my death. Both absent. Both removed with the same precision. If the dungeon collapsed on me — which is what the Order's records state — then the memory of that collapse has been excised. If something else happened —*

He didn't finish. A second unfinished sentence. For Aldric, that was a building falling.

*If something else happened,* Vess said, *then the removal of the memory is itself evidence that the death wasn't what the records claim.*

*Yes.*

"The System," Maren said. He said it aloud. Derry didn't stir.

Neither fragment answered, but neither needed to. The thing that classified everything. That assigned quests and generated bounties and structured the architecture of the world that everyone except Maren could see. The thing that had put a bounty on Maren's head because it couldn't classify what he carried. It didn't just absorb consciousness when a person died in a contamination zone. It processed the absorbed material. Took inventory. And it removed what it didn't want preserved.

From Vess: the sister's name. The emotional content of the massacre — the personal anger, the human reason she'd taken the contract. The details of her consumption. Everything that made her a person with motives rather than a set of operational capabilities.

From Aldric: the moment of his death. The understanding he'd reached about the System's architecture. Whatever he'd seen on that wall in the second chamber. Everything that might explain what happened to him and why.

The operational data was left. The approach routes, the threat assessments, the professional competencies, the mechanical knowledge of how to kill or how to pray. The System kept what was useful and stripped what was human. Like emptying a house and leaving only the furniture.

"There could be more," Maren said. "Things that were taken that you don't know were there. You can only see the gaps in things you remember having. If a whole category of memory was removed —"

*Then we would not know it was ever present,* Aldric said. *That is correct. The observable gaps may be a fraction of the actual editing.*

The fire was low. Maren put another stick on it — a piece of scrub oak that was dry enough to catch quickly. The new flames threw shadows across Derry's sleeping face and across Maren's hands, which were rough and dirt-lined and holding a stick and shaking slightly, though not from cold.

He was carrying two people. Not tools. Not curses. Not voices to be managed or argued with or used for tactical advantage or moral commentary. Two people who had been opened and edited by the only authority with the power and precision to do it, and then sealed back up with their operational capabilities intact and their humanity reduced to negative space.

Vess, who couldn't feel her own anger. Whose sister's name occupied a hole in her memory shaped exactly like a name, two syllables, the first one open. Who could map every room in a dead man's estate but not the face of the woman who raised her.

Aldric, who couldn't remember dying. Whose investigation into the thing he'd served for fourteen years had ended in a gap with edges too clean for accident. Who had been a man of complete sentences and total certainty, and whose certainty now had a crack in it in the exact shape of the truth he'd discovered and lost.

Maren sat with them. The hum droned in the dark, steady and low, and the quest-light in the ditch beyond the hollow reflected the stars — a second sky, fainter, pooled in the low places of the world. The contamination smell had settled into something almost ignorable, the way any constant discomfort becomes background if you sit with it long enough.

Derry snored once, softly. The sausage he'd been saving had slipped from his pack and lay in the dirt near his boot. Maren picked it up and brushed it off and put it back. A small thing. The kind of thing you did for someone without thinking about it. The kind of thing the System's editing would probably leave in, because it was functional, and strip the feeling from, because the feeling was the part that mattered.

He thought about Thessa's warning. The clock he was on. The correction coming. And he thought about the two people inside him who were on that clock too, who had already been corrected once — not destroyed, but diminished, their memories trimmed, their feelings cut — and who didn't know what else they'd lost.

*You should sleep,* Vess said.

"Yeah."

*Your body will function at approximately sixty percent capacity tomorrow if you don't.*

"I know."

*Then sleep.*

He didn't. He sat with the fire going to coals and the stars turning overhead and the hum in his teeth, and he thought about what it meant to carry people who'd been robbed and not know the full inventory of what was taken. Thought about the System out there in the dark, vast and invisible to him and apparently capable of reaching inside the dead and taking what it wanted. Thought about his hands, which had faked a hundred rituals and killed a man and failed Thessa's test and were now, apparently, the only hands that could hold what these two people had left.

He didn't sleep. But he stayed.


# Chapter 9

They smelled Harrow before they saw it. Ozone and char, layered thick enough to close Maren's throat while the town was still just a smudge between hills. Underneath, something sweeter — hot stone baking in late summer, and the particular warmth that quest-light left on surfaces after it had pooled long enough. The road had been getting worse for two hours. Scorch marks overlapped on the packed dirt until the whole path looked like the floor of a forge, burn patterns crossing at angles that made no sense until you understood that heroes didn't clean up after themselves. Some of the marks were old, pale gray, scuffed by cart wheels. Others were darker. One was still warm when Maren's boot caught its edge, and he moved his foot off it the way you'd step off a hot coal.

*Thirty-seven,* Aldric said. *Thirty-eight. Thirty-nine — that one is fresh, less than a day old. Someone deployed a class-three fire projection in the middle of a public road.*

"How do you know it's class three?" Maren muttered, barely moving his lips. He'd gotten better at that — the ventriloquism of talking to his own skull. A week of practice, and he could almost pass for a man muttering to himself, which was at least socially acceptable if not dignified.

*The scorch radius. The char depth. The residual heat signature is still readable on the interface. Do you want the full damage assessment or can I trust you to walk around it?*

"I can see a burn mark on the ground. I've been seeing burn marks."

*You have been seeing scorch marks the way a child sees a hearth fire. I am reading you System-verified damage signatures with class attribution, decay rates, and—*

*Four,* Vess said.

Aldric stopped. Maren waited.

*She does that on purpose,* Aldric said.

*Four heroes ahead. Two at the gate, one on the wall, one buying something from a stall just inside. The gate pair are low-tier — two, maybe three. Wall is tier four. The one at the stall—* She paused. *Interesting.*

"Interesting how? Interesting meaning they won't bother us, or interesting meaning we turn around right now?"

*Interesting meaning I can't get a clean read at this distance and I don't like not knowing.*

Derry was already sweating. He'd been sweating since they'd spotted the first road marker for Harrow, a charred wooden post with something carved into it that Derry could read and Maren couldn't. Whatever it said had drained the color from Derry's face and replaced it with a thin, damp sheen.

"Big place," Derry said, which was unnecessary. He wiped his forehead with his sleeve and the sleeve came away dark. "Lot of people, though. That's good, right? More people means it's harder to spot one — harder to pick one person out of—"

"Derry."

"Yeah."

"You're fine."

"I know I'm fine. I'm saying it's good. The crowd thing. That's — yeah."

Derry stopped talking for about fifteen steps, which under these conditions was something like a personal best.

Harrow sat in a shallow bowl between two hills, and the hum hit Maren before he could see the town proper. He'd always heard it — the low vibration that System-heavy areas made, a wasps' nest buried in a wall. In Fenhold it had been background, the kind of sound you stopped noticing the way you stopped noticing your own breathing. Here it was a pressure. It sat behind his sternum and stayed. The air felt denser, like walking into a room where too many people were talking. His ears popped twice on the slope down.

*The interface density is remarkable.* Aldric's tone shifted — less irritation, more the careful attention of a man cataloguing something he'd only read about in reports. *Quest notifications are layered three deep in some areas. I can barely isolate individual prompts. There — that board ahead, the tall frame by the market square. Do you see it?*

A wooden frame taller than he was stood nailed to a post at the edge of the market square. Things were pinned to it. Papers, maybe. Leather strips. Some kind of thin fabric that moved in the breeze. To him it was a bulletin board outside a village hall, just bigger and busier.

*That is a quest board. There are forty-one active quest postings. Sixteen standard dungeon clearances. Eight contamination reports — the residue levels listed on some of these are, frankly, appalling. Nine bounties. Four escort requests. And four that I would classify as — * He paused. *Recreational.*

"Recreational."

*Someone is offering three gold pieces to recover a cat from a low-tier dungeon mouth. The cat is named Brindlefur. There is an illustration. It appears to be hand-drawn. The cat looks — Maren, the cat appears to be wearing a small hat in the illustration, and I cannot determine whether that is artistic license or an actual hat.*

Vess said nothing, which was its own kind of commentary.

"Three gold for a cat," Maren said.

*The bounty on your head is four hundred times that amount,* Aldric said. *In case you needed perspective.*

They passed through the gate without challenge. The two heroes there barely looked at them — one was eating something that dripped grease onto her gauntlet, and the other was arguing with a cart driver about road-damage fees. The argument was loud and specific, involving scorched wheel rims and a dispute over which hero's fire ability had done the actual damage. Maren kept his eyes forward, his pace unremarkable, his mouth shut.

Aldric did not keep his mouth shut.

*Gear shop. Gear shop. Another gear shop — that one specializes in elemental resistances, judging by the signage. Tavern. The sign says "The Cinder" and there are scorch marks on the sign itself, which I suspect is intentional. Tavern. That building has no sign and I have no idea what it is, but there is a significant amount of quest-light pooled against its foundation and someone has drawn a rude picture on the wall in what appears to be—*

The quest-light was the only part Maren could verify independently. It gathered in the gutters like pale, lukewarm water, faintly luminous even in full daylight. In Fenhold it collected in ditches and hollow stumps, barely enough to notice. Here it ran in actual rivulets between cobblestones, and where it pooled against walls the stone had taken on a glow like bone held against a candle flame. Maren stepped over a stream of it that ran across the road from a cracked drain and felt the warmth through his boot.

A man walked past them and raised one hand, casually, the way someone might wave to a friend. Something happened — a sharp, localized heat on the left side of his face, a crack like a branch splitting, and then a scorch mark appeared on the wall beside him, black and smoking. The man hadn't broken stride. He was adjusting the strap on his shoulder bag with the hand that had just put a burn mark on a building.

"What," Maren said, very quietly.

*Class-two fire projection. Casual discharge. He appears to be — ah. He moved up one rank on the damage board. He was testing a new calibration.* Aldric's tone carried the specific disbelief of a man who had once served an order that regarded System abilities as solemn responsibilities. *He scorched a wall. For a leaderboard position.*

*Damage rankings,* Vess said. *Overhead. Every building has one. It is a local hero ranking system based on verified damage output. Updated in real-time.*

Maren looked up and saw nothing but rafters and awning poles and the pale sky between them. The rankings were invisible to him, like everything else. He was standing in a town built entirely around information he couldn't access, navigating by scorch marks and smell and the commentary of dead people.

*The man who just scorched the wall is ranked sixty-three,* Aldric said. *He is now sixty-two. He appears to be — yes, he is checking. He has stopped walking to verify his new position. He looks pleased.* A pause. *A woman across the street appears displeased. I believe she was sixty-two. There is a — she is gesturing at him. He is gesturing back. This may escalate.*

The woman fired something at a wall further down the street. Another scorch mark. The heat reached him from twenty feet away. A child walking between them didn't flinch. Nobody flinched. This was weather to them.

Maren walked under the invisible leaderboards and the visible scorch marks and tried not to think about the four hundred cats' worth of bounty floating over his own head.

---

The market square was worse. Heroes were everywhere — not fighting, not questing, just existing in the specific way that people with System-enhanced abilities existed in their natural habitat. Two women were comparing weapon stats loudly enough that Maren could hear numbers he couldn't verify. A man in scaled armor was doing something to the air around his hands that made it shimmer and pop, drawing a small crowd of onlookers who apparently found this entertaining. A knot of younger heroes — teenagers, maybe, with the shiny-faced confidence of people who'd just gotten their first class assignments — clustered around the quest board, arguing about which dungeon had the best loot-to-risk ratio.

*She'd kill you in two moves,* Vess said.

Maren flinched before he realized she wasn't talking about anyone nearby. She was looking through his eyes at a woman leaning against a wall across the square, cleaning under her fingernails with a short knife.

*The woman with the knife. Two moves. Maybe one and a half, depending on your reaction time, which is poor.*

"Thanks."

*The man behind her — four moves. The boy at the fruit stall, the one in the green coat — he looks young but his stance is trained. Three, if he doesn't hesitate. Five, if he does. The woman with the ale — she's not a threat, she's had too much. The tall one at the corner—*

"Can you stop counting?"

*No.*

"Is there anyone in this town who couldn't kill me?"

*The cat on the quest board, presumably. Though I lack information about the hat.*

Derry had fallen behind again. Maren turned and found him three stalls back, nodding vigorously at a vendor who was showing him something wrapped in leather. Derry's smile was so wide it had to hurt. His shirt was soaked through at the collar and the dark stain was working its way down between his shoulder blades.

"What are you doing?"

"Just looking!" Derry's voice cracked upward, too loud, too cheerful. "This is — what did you say this was? A grip thing?"

The vendor — a broad woman with forearms like rolled rope — looked at Derry with the flat patience of someone who'd dealt with confused farmers before. "Grip wrap. For polearm users. Absorbs impact on the shaft."

"Right, right, grip wrap! My cousin uses a — he's got a thing, a pole, a spear thing, and he always says the grip is the most important—"

"You don't have a cousin with a spear," Maren said.

Derry's smile didn't falter so much as solidify, like plaster setting wrong. "I might. You don't know all my cousins."

"I know all your cousins. There are two. One raises goats and the other one can't lift his right arm above his shoulder."

The vendor looked between them with the expression of someone deciding whether to be amused or annoyed. Maren put a hand on Derry's arm and steered him away from the stall. Derry resisted for a moment, turned back, waved at the vendor, said "Sorry, we might come back, it's really nice stuff, the grip thing," and then let himself be moved.

"You have to stop doing that," Maren said, low.

"Doing what?"

"Buying things. Talking to people. Smiling like that."

"Like what?"

"Like a man who's about to be arrested and wants everyone to think he's having a nice day."

Derry wiped his face with both hands. The sweat came right back, beading along his hairline within seconds. "I'm trying. I am. I just — every time someone looks at me, my mouth opens, and it starts — I told that woman back there about Pell's counting. With the beads. The little counting frame with the beads. She didn't ask. She was selling rope. I told a rope vendor about my daughter's arithmetic."

*His cortisol levels must be extraordinary,* Aldric observed. *The man is producing enough sweat to—*

"Not helpful," Maren said, to Aldric and to no one visible, which earned him a glance from a passing hero that he pretended not to notice.

They needed food that would keep, and they needed to know which road past Harrow went toward the Crook without cutting through active dungeon territory. They needed to accomplish both without anyone looking at Maren long enough to see what wasn't there.

*The dried-goods vendor three stalls east,* Aldric said. *But the man sitting beside him — armored, large, on a crate — has a passive scan ability. I can see the active aura. He is reading the classification of everyone who passes within ten yards.*

"So we go around."

*The next nearest food vendor is across the square. Past the quest board. Past the two taverns. Past the table.*

Maren looked. The table was, in fact, on fire. A woman in scalemail sat next to it, laughing, while a man in a leather coat poured ale over the flames. The ale did nothing useful. A third person — a boy, maybe sixteen — was trying to smother the fire with his jacket and the jacket was also now on fire.

"Do they know," Maren said, "that the table is on fire?"

*They appear to consider it recreational.*

"And the jacket?"

*Also recreational, I must assume, given that no one is alarmed. The boy is — he is laughing now. The jacket is ruined. I want you to know that in fourteen years of service I never once set furniture on fire without tactical justification.*

Derry had stopped beside a stall selling polishing compound and was asking the vendor about the proper way to maintain leather boots, which was a topic he did actually know something about, and the vendor was answering with the weary tolerance of a man who'd had this exact conversation six hundred times. Derry's hands were in his pockets, but Maren could see the tremor in his forearms.

Maren reached the food vendor on the far side of the square — a thin man with sun-bleached hair and a stall that smelled like salt and old smoke. His provisions looked road-worthy: dried meat, hard bread, something in waxed paper that might have been cheese or might have been soap. Maren was reaching for his money when the vendor's eyes shifted.

Not to Maren's face. To a point slightly above and to the left of Maren's head — the place where, Aldric had explained weeks ago, System-sighted people saw the classification data that accompanied every person like a name pinned to a shirt.

The vendor looked at the spot. Looked at it again. His expression did something subtle and wrong — not suspicion, but the face a person makes when they reach for a step that isn't there.

"Class?" the vendor asked. Conversational. The way someone in any other town might ask *where are you from.*

"Sorry?"

"Your class. Just — you passing through? Most of my regulars are ranged-damage types, they like the jerky. Melee goes for the bread. What's your build?"

The hum in his chest intensified, though that might have been his heart. "Support class," he said, and he said it the way he'd said a thousand things over eleven years of fraud — with the easy, tired confidence of a man stating something too boring to lie about. "We're just passing through. Need road food."

"Support should still display, though." The vendor squinted at the empty space. "Everyone displays. Even noncombat roles. Healers, buffers, scouts — they all show a classification."

"Gear interference. It's been — look, it's a whole thing with my interface. It's a known issue."

The lie was smooth enough. Eleven years. But the vendor's hand was slow reaching for the jerky, and his eyes kept drifting back to the void where Maren's classification should have been, the way a person's eyes will return to a gap in a fence where a board should be — not alarmed, but bothered in a low, persistent way.

*He is not satisfied,* Aldric said. The sarcasm was gone. *He is going to—*

"Hey, Korr?" The vendor turned his head toward the armored man on the crate three stalls over. "Can you run a—"

Maren put coins on the counter. Too many — enough to buy the vendor's entire stock of jerky twice over. "Just the bread and the jerky. Keep the change. We're in a rush."

The vendor looked at the coins. Looked at the void. Looked at the coins again. Greed and curiosity fought a brief, visible war across his face, and greed won, but not by enough to stop his mouth.

"I've never seen a blank read," he said, handing over the food. "Not once. Not even broken gear gives you a blank. You'd get an error code, at least. Some kind of—"

Maren took the food and walked away. Behind him, the vendor was still talking — not to Maren anymore, but to the woman at the next stall, and Maren heard the word *blank* and then *weird* and then something he couldn't catch because Derry was beside him now, breathing hard, clutching a small jar of boot polish he'd apparently purchased.

"You bought boot polish," Maren said.

"She was very convincing."

"You're wearing sandals."

Derry looked down at his feet. He was, in fact, wearing sandals. He'd been wearing sandals for the entire trip. He looked at the boot polish in his hand with the expression of a man who has done something he cannot explain and knows it.

"We're leaving," Maren said. "Now. Before—"

*The woman at the stall,* Vess said. *The one the vendor spoke to. She has turned her head. She is looking at you.*

"Before that."

---

Maren didn't turn around. The road north forked past a tavern with a green-painted sign — he could see that much — and the left fork should take them toward dungeon territory and eventually the Crook. He angled toward it.

*She has stood up,* Vess said. *The woman. Blue cloak. She was seated at the stall where the man with the passive scan sits. She looked at you when you passed and now she is walking.*

"Could be nothing."

*She is walking at pursuit pace. Not running. Matching your speed at fifteen yards. She signaled with her off hand — military shorthand, two fingers, lateral sweep. That is a flanking directive.*

The market noise continued around them. The burning table. The leaderboard. The boy with the smoldering jacket, who had given up on his jacket and was now watching it burn on the cobblestones with his arms crossed. None of it had changed, but the air around Maren had turned thin, and the hum in his chest had become a specific, directional pressure, like a finger pressing on his sternum.

*Two more are moving from the tavern entrance on your right,* Vess said. *They were seated. They are not seated now. The one in front is tier four, minimum. He moves like someone who has done this before.*

Derry hadn't heard any of it — the fragments were inside Maren's skull — but he'd seen something change in Maren's face, or his pace, or the way his shoulders had come up, because Derry's own face drained to the color of old cheese.

"What?" Derry whispered. "What is it? What's—"

"Don't look behind us."

Derry immediately looked behind them.

"I said—"

"Sorry, sorry, I know, I just — there's a woman, she's sort of — she's walking fast—"

*She is not the primary concern,* Vess said. *The man from the tavern is. He has a scan active. A real one, not the passive ambient type. He is actively querying you. And he is getting—*

*Nothing,* Aldric said. His composure had cracked and the words came faster than his usual measured cadence. *He is getting nothing. The bounty posting describes a physical type that matches approximately forty percent of the male population in the region, which is why physical identification alone was never sufficient. But the System anomaly — the unclassified entity designation — is unique. She did not identify you by your face. She identified you by the absence. By the void where your classification should be. Your blankness is — in a town where every person, every animal, every piece of furniture with a System tag is visible and readable — your blankness is the loudest thing in this square.*

A voice behind them, not shouting but pitched to carry across a market without sounding like a command: "Hey. Support class. With the — stop walking, please."

*Please,* Vess noted. *That is professional. That is someone who has been trained to de-escalate before escalating.*

Maren did not stop. He walked faster. The distinction between walking fast and running was closing rapidly, and Derry was beside him, breathing in those awful wet hitches, the boot polish still in his hand.

"Oh god," Derry said. "Oh god, oh shit, Maren—"

"Walk."

"I'm walking. I'm walking, but they're — she's—"

*Run,* Vess said.

The word was flat, professional, a field assessment delivered without emphasis, and that was what made Maren's legs move. Vess didn't waste syllables. If she said run, the window for walking had closed.

They ran. Maren's pack bounced against his spine. Derry's sandals slapped the cobblestones — too loud, too flat, the wrong footwear for flight and the wrong man for it too. Behind them the voice called out again, louder, and other voices picked it up — not a mob, not yet, but the specific rising attention of a crowd that had noticed something interesting happening and was turning to watch.

*Left at the fork,* Aldric said, his formality abandoned entirely, directions barked like a man reading a map while the house burned. *Left, then straight, the road opens past the—*

*Right,* Vess said.

*I said left.*

*You said left because the road goes north. I said right because there are three heroes between us and your left fork and two of them have already turned.*

Maren went right. The turn took him between a shuttered gear shop and a squat building that stank of animal fat and tallow. Quest-light pooled in the alley's gutter, warm against his ankles when he splashed through it, and the scorch marks on the alley walls were so dense they'd merged into a continuous dark stain, years of hero traffic burned into the stone like a record of carelessness.

Derry was behind him. Still running. Still making that sound — not panting but something closer to the noise a man makes when he's trying very hard not to scream and the effort is coming out through his breathing instead.

*Alley opens onto a side street in twelve yards,* Vess said. *The side street runs downhill toward a secondary gate — a breach, actually, a wall section damaged by a high-tier ability that was never properly repaired. There is a gap wide enough for two.*

"How do you know that from in here?"

*I can see the structural data on the interface. The damage report is still filed. No repair ticket was ever issued. This town does not maintain its own walls.* A pause. *That is remarkably poor infrastructure management.*

They burst out of the alley onto the side street and Maren forced himself to slow. His lungs burned. His legs wanted to keep running but the side street had people on it — a woman with a handcart, two children playing some kind of game with System-visible markers Maren couldn't see, a man loading sacks onto a mule.

"Walk," Maren said. "Normal. Like we belong here."

Derry was doubled over beside him, hands on knees, making bellows sounds. The boot polish had fallen somewhere in the alley.

"I can't — I don't remember what normal—"

"Walk like you're going to check on the cow. The cow got out again. You're annoyed but not surprised."

Derry made a noise that was either a laugh or the first stage of something worse, but he straightened up and walked. The walk was terrible — stiff-armed, too rigid, a man wearing the idea of casual movement like an ill-fitting coat — but it carried him forward through the gap in the wall and onto the dirt road beyond.

The hum lessened. The quest-light thinned to occasional dull patches in the roadside ditches. The scorch marks became older, paler, spaced apart enough that the packed dirt between them looked like actual road again.

Behind them, Harrow went on — voices, the clatter of the hero economy, a distant crack that was either a System ability or another table meeting its end — but no one came through the gap. Not yet.

They walked. The road bent north into scrub hills where the soil was darker and the faint sweetish smell of old contamination hung in the motionless air. Late summer heat pressed down. The dust stuck to Derry's soaked shirt and turned it a muddy brown.

*They saw your face,* Aldric said, after a silence long enough to feel deliberate. He'd recovered his composure, or enough of it that the syntax was back — full sentences, subordinate clauses, the architecture of a man filing a report to an order that no longer existed. *Multiple heroes. In a town with a quest board, a bounty roster, and real-time interface updates. Your physical description combined with the unclassified entity anomaly is now a confirmed sighting. Not a rumor. Not a second-hand report. A verified reading by at least three System-equipped observers in a populated staging area.*

Maren didn't answer. He walked. The jerky and bread were in his pack, at least. His money was considerably lighter.

*Whatever approach relied on anonymity is finished,* Aldric said. *Moving quietly through populated areas is no longer viable. They will distribute the sighting through the standard bounty network. The next town, the next patrol, the next hero with a passive scan — they will know what to look for. Not a face. A void. Your absence is now your identifying characteristic.*

"I heard you."

*I am not certain you understand the—*

"I understand."

The paladin went quiet. Maren could feel him in there — the hovering disapproval, the half-composed lecture on tactical awareness — but for once Aldric chose not to deliver it, and the restraint was worse than the lecture would have been.

Derry was walking beside him with the rigid, mechanical gait of a man whose legs hadn't caught up with the fact that they were no longer sprinting. His hands shook. Not the subtle tremor from the market but a proper shake, the kind that made his fingers useless, and he pressed them flat against his thighs to make them stop and they didn't stop.

"Maren," Derry said.

"Yeah."

"I talked to a woman about cloaks."

"I know."

"I don't wear cloaks. I've never — I told her about Pell. About the counting beads. She was selling cloaks and I told her my daughter was learning to count. And then I bought boot polish." He looked down at his empty hands. "I was wearing sandals."

"Yeah."

"My hands won't stop."

Maren looked at Derry's hands. The shake was bad — fingers spread, the tendons visible, the kind of tremor you got when the body's terror outran the mind's ability to manage it. Maren's own hands were steady, and he didn't know what that meant. Maybe the killing two weeks ago had burned something out, some connection between fear and its physical expression. Or maybe his fear had just moved deeper, past the hands and the sweat and the shaking, down into the flat dry recognition that things were worse now and would keep getting worse and the road ahead led into dungeon territory because the road behind had his face on it.

His absence on it. The shape of nothing where a person should be.

*The Crook is four days north,* Vess said. *Five if the roads deteriorate past dungeon-approach standards.*

*If we reach the Crook,* Aldric said.

Vess did not answer. She didn't need to. The silence acknowledged what the words had laid out — that reaching the Crook was no longer the expected outcome but a thing that would need to be managed, negotiated, survived toward, with Maren's empty profile moving ahead of him like a signal fire.

The sun was high. The hum sat in Maren's chest, low and constant. Behind them, somewhere in Harrow, a woman in a blue cloak was filing a report that would make the next town harder and the town after that harder still, and the bounty that floated invisibly over Maren's head — four hundred cats, twelve hundred grip wraps, a number so large it bent the System's attention toward him like light through a lens — had just become specific.

Not a rumor anymore. A confirmed sighting. A void that walked and bought jerky and ran badly through alleys. A man-shaped gap in the System's architecture, seen and recorded in a town that existed to see and record exactly this kind of thing.

Maren walked north. The scorch marks on the road grew older and fewer, until there were none at all, and the road was just dirt, and the dirt was just the world, and the world didn't care what the System thought he was or wasn't.

But the people in it did. That was the problem. That had always been the problem.


# Chapter 10

The road past Harrow turned to packed earth and stayed that way. No scorch marks out here — the heroes went the other direction, toward the dungeon mouths, and they left their damage there. Maren's feet had been swollen since yesterday and the cooler morning air was the first mercy in a week, settling into the backs of his hands like water. Late summer letting go. He could feel the season turning in the way the shadows fell longer and earlier, the grass along the road going from green to something brittle and pale at the tips.

Derry hadn't said much since they'd run from Harrow. Not nothing — he'd offered Maren some dried meat at dawn, asked if the road felt right, made a half-observation about the clouds that he didn't finish. But the chatter was gone. The Derry who filled every silence with stories about Pell's counting lessons and Lenna's tomato plants was walking beside Maren now like a man saving his words for something he hadn't decided to say yet.

*Two figures on the ridge. North side. Half a mile back.*

Vess, calibrating threats. She'd been doing it since Harrow — quiet updates delivered with the flat precision of someone checking instruments.

*Hero-class?* Maren thought.

*Farmers. Hay rakes. Not a concern.*

Aldric hadn't spoken all morning. When the paladin went quiet, it meant he was either seeing something in the System's architecture that he didn't know how to name or composing a lecture so thorough it required structural planning. Both demanded silence, and both usually ended with Maren feeling worse about himself.

They came around a bend where the road widened into a clearing — someone had cut back the brush on both sides, maybe for a seasonal market that nobody used anymore, the stumps sawed low and gone grey — and the man was sitting at the clearing's far edge. Sitting, not standing. His hands on his knees, empty.

Maren stopped.

Derry almost walked into him, then looked where Maren was looking, and his hand went to Maren's elbow. Not to guide him. To hold on.

The man wore ranger gear that had seen real weather — leather darkened at the stress points, stitching re-done along one shoulder where something had torn through it and been repaired without vanity. His pack sat against a stump, buckled shut. A short sword rested on the ground beside his right boot, positioned where he could reach it but clearly not where he was reaching for it. His hair was cropped close and his face had the colour of someone who slept outside more often than in. He favoured his right knee — Maren could see it in how he sat, the leg extended slightly, not quite willing to fold all the way.

He had the look of a man who used to smile more. That was what Maren noticed first, before the gear or the weapon or the deliberate stillness: the architecture of a face that had been built for expressions it didn't make anymore.

*Hero-ranger. Active class. High level. The bounty is open on his interface.* Vess, each word placed like a stone. *He can see what you are. He's been here at least an hour.*

*And he has not drawn his weapon,* Aldric said. The paladin's voice was careful, which was different from his usual formality. Careful like a man stepping around something fragile on the floor. *That is not a good sign.*

Maren understood why immediately. A ranger who attacked was a problem you could solve — by running, by fighting, by being lucky. A ranger who sat down and waited for you to walk to him wanted something that couldn't be solved with any of those.

"There's two ways this goes," the man said. His voice carried the clearing without effort — trained to project over distance, the habit so deep it operated even in conversation. "One of them involves me trailing you for another three days, and honestly my knee isn't up for it this week. So I'd rather the other way."

Derry's hand tightened on Maren's elbow.

"What's the other way?" Maren said. His own voice came out steadier than the inside of him warranted, the performance booting up on its own — charm and deflection and the right tone, mechanical as breathing.

"We talk." The man shifted on the stump, resettling his weight off the bad knee. "I'm Caul. I've been behind you since Harrow. Ahead of you now, which I realize looks staged, but the road only goes one way from here and I walk faster than you do."

*He is telling the truth about his name. Caul Henning. Registered with the Wayward Reach ranger company. Twelve years active service. His party roster shows —* Aldric stopped. A silence that felt different from his usual pauses — not deliberate, not tactical. Arrested.

*What?*

*His party roster shows two members deceased. Recent. Three months ago.*

"You can sit," Caul said. "I've got water. Your friend looks like he's about to fall over."

Derry did look bad. The colour had left his face and his shirt was damp at the collar despite the cool morning, the sweat of a man whose body had decided to be afraid before his mind caught up. But he was looking at Maren, not at Caul, and Maren could read that look with eleven years of professional accuracy: *Do we run?*

*Running would be inadvisable,* Vess said. *He has sight lines on both exits from this clearing. Brush cover to the south but the terrain drops into a gully thirty metres in. He chose this spot.*

"Yeah," Maren said to Derry, though it wasn't really an answer to the question Derry was asking. He let go of the tension in his shoulders — a conscious decision to rearrange his body into something that looked like ease — and walked to the middle of the clearing and sat on the ground. Not because he wanted to sit. Because standing in front of a seated man with a sword on the ground made the power too obvious, and Maren's whole professional life was about rearranging the power in a room until he could find a way to work it.

Derry sat too, but farther away. Not beside Maren, the way he would have a week ago. About four feet to Maren's left, knees drawn up, arms wrapped around them. The distance was small and Maren measured it the way he measured everything — automatically, with the part of himself that catalogued what people were telling him with their bodies before they'd decided to tell him anything with their mouths.

"So," Maren said.

"So." Caul looked at him. His eyes were the light grey of old iron and they held Maren's face with a patience that wasn't threatening — worse than threatening. The patience of a man who had already decided what this conversation was and was waiting for Maren to arrive at the same understanding. "You're the unclassified. The bounty."

"I've been told."

"Your profile had a sin-eater registration before the classification scrambled. Did you know that? It's still there underneath. Eleven years of service logged."

Something in Maren's chest went very still. Not a tightening — a cessation, the way a room goes quiet when everyone hears the same sound at once.
