"I know who I am," Maren said. "I don't know what you want."

"I want to tell you something." Caul recapped a water skin he'd been holding and set it beside his boot. The gesture was unhurried, almost domestic — a man settling in for a conversation over a table, not confronting a fugitive in a clearing. "After that, you can go. For a while." He looked at Derry, then back at Maren. "Your friend should hear it too."

Derry said nothing. His arms were locked around his knees and he was staring at the dirt between his feet with the focused attention of a man trying to disappear into the ground.

"Three months ago," Caul said, "my party cleared a dungeon near the Ashfold border. Standard contract. Minor threat creatures, containment perimeter, System-flagged quest. We'd done a hundred of these. Probably two hundred. You stop counting after the first year."

He spoke the way someone reads aloud from a document they've memorized — each sentence placed, cadenced, delivered with the even rhythm of a field report. But the rhythm was too even. Too practiced. And Maren recognized it immediately because he recognized performance, because performance was his entire professional skill, and this man had rehearsed what he was about to say. Not to be convincing. To survive saying it.

"The area was supposed to be clean. The report said so — class-certified sin-eater, registered, verified. System records showed the contamination cleared eighteen months before we got there. Haley checked it herself. She always checked. She was — " The practised cadence held, barely. "She was careful about that."

*Haley Keth,* Aldric said, very quietly, reading from whatever the System kept on file for the dead. *Healer-class. Twenty-six at time of death. Renn Ostara. Shield-bearer. Twenty-nine.*

Caul's hands stayed on his knees. "The dungeon was routine. Threat level minimal. But the contamination wasn't gone. It had settled into the substrate — below what a standard scan reads, in the deep residue that a sin-eater is supposed to pull. It was old contamination. Layers of it. Years' worth, built up because someone had been marking the area clean and walking away."

The clearing was quiet. Somewhere behind them a bird was repeating a three-note call, over and over, and Maren wanted it to stop because the sound was too ordinary for what was happening in his chest.

"Haley went down first. The contamination spiked when the quest activated — the old residue interacted with the fresh System energy and it all came up at once. Like pulling a tarp off a floor that's been rotting underneath." Caul paused. The practised rhythm broke and what was under it showed briefly — a man trying very hard to hold a shape that grief kept pulling out of true. "Renn tried to get her out. The spike caught him too."

Maren's hands were in his lap. He was looking at them. They were still, which surprised him, because the rest of him felt like something had come loose inside his ribs and was swinging free, knocking against the walls of him with each breath.

*He is describing contamination persistence from incomplete sin-eating,* Aldric said. The paladin didn't sound like the paladin — not lecturing, not moral, not correcting. Something Maren hadn't heard from him before. Something that sounded like a man reading a report he'd already known the contents of. *A genuine sin-eater clears the contamination from the substrate. A fraudulent one only removes the surface. What he is describing is exactly —*

*I know.* Maren thought it hard enough that the thought had edges. *I know what he's describing.*

Caul was watching him. The ranger's face hadn't changed — still the settled, practised expression, the field-report composure — but his eyes were doing something that wasn't professional. They were looking at Maren the way you look at a word you're trying to read in bad light.

"The sin-eater who certified that area was real," Caul said. "Registered. Verified. Had the class designation, the proper ritual sequence on file. Everything checked. And the contamination was still there, because the rituals were empty. The motions without the work." He paused. Let the silence sit. He was good at that — the silence of a tracker who'd spent days waiting for movement, comfortable with stillness as a tool. "Do you understand what I'm telling you?"

"Yeah," Maren said. "I understand."

"There are more of you than people think. Fake sin-eaters. Some of you are System-blind, some just found out that doing the ritual wrong pays the same as doing it right." Caul's fingers pressed into his kneecaps, the knuckles whitening briefly. "The System doesn't audit the work. It checks the certification, the registration. It doesn't check whether the contamination actually cleared, because the System assumes — " He stopped. Drew breath through his nose. When he continued, the rehearsed cadence was back, but thinner, more audibly a structure he was leaning on to stay upright. "The System assumes sin-eaters do their jobs."

Derry made a small sound. Not a word — a tight exhaled noise, the kind of sound he made when he was trying to process something too large for speech. Maren didn't look at him. He couldn't look at Derry right now, because if he looked he would see Derry's face doing the thing it was doing, which was understanding exactly what Caul was describing and knowing exactly who it applied to, and that understanding would be written in every line of Derry's open, honest, terrible face, and Maren would not be able to keep looking at Caul's grey eyes with anything resembling composure.

"Haley liked birds," Caul said. The shift was sudden — a crack in the practiced frame that he didn't try to repair. "She kept a journal. Drew them. She wasn't any good at it — the proportions were always wrong. Heads too big, wings too short. Renn used to tell her they looked like angry potatoes with beaks." Something moved in his face that was not a smile. "He was better at drawing than she was but he'd never have told her that. He just — he let her show him and he'd say they were good."

*Don't say anything,* Vess said. The assassin's voice carried its usual operational flatness, but there was a precision in it that Maren had learned to recognise as something more than professional — a quality of attention that only appeared when Vess was seeing something she considered real. *He isn't asking for a response. He's showing you what he lost. Let him.*

Caul reached into the front of his ranger's jacket and took out a folded piece of paper — creased and re-creased so many times the folds were nearly worn through, the paper gone soft at the edges. He didn't unfold it. He held it in his hand for a moment, then put it back inside the jacket.

"I've been tracking since they died. Not your bounty specifically — I picked that up in Harrow. Your profile matched reports I'd been collecting. I've been tracking the problem. The certification gaps. Areas where the contamination records don't match the service history." He smoothed the front of his jacket where the paper sat. "There are a lot of gaps."

"I'm not — " Maren started, and the lie was right there, fully formed, ready to deploy: *I'm not what you think, I don't know what you're talking about, you've got the wrong man.* Eleven years of practice had made the lie as natural as swallowing. He could feel it in his mouth, shaped and familiar, comfortable as an old shoe.

He didn't say it. Not because honesty won — honesty had never won a fight inside Maren, had never even shown up to one with real conviction. But the lie would have required him to look at Caul's face while he said it, and Caul's face was the face of a man carrying a piece of paper with a dead woman's bad bird drawings on it, and some performances just couldn't survive the audience they were playing to.

"You're not what?" Caul said. Patient. Waiting the way a stone waits.

"I don't — I'm not going to — " The self-correction kicked in, the compulsive mid-sentence revision, and this time it wasn't strategic. This time it was the honest confusion of a man who had three or four things in his mouth and couldn't find one that was safe to say. "I don't know what to tell you," he said. "That's — yeah. I don't know what to tell you."

It was the truest thing he'd said in thirteen days. The confession sat in his chest like a river stone and the lie sat next to it and neither could move because both were exactly the same weight.

Aldric said nothing. The silence from the paladin was enormous, and Maren understood it completely: this was what Aldric had wanted Maren to hear since the first day. The cost. The specific, named, human cost of eleven years of fraud, delivered not by a dead paladin's moral lectures but by a living man holding a dead woman's drawings. Aldric didn't need to speak because the whole conversation was his argument, made by someone who had standing to make it, in a clearing on a road that Maren couldn't walk fast enough to leave behind him.

"I'm not asking you to tell me anything," Caul said. "I'm telling you. So you know what's out there. What's been left behind in the villages where sin-eaters collected the fee and didn't do the work." He looked at the sky — checking the light, or checking something in himself that needed sky to find. "You're headed for the Crook."

Maren didn't confirm or deny. His mouth was dry and his hands were very still in his lap.

"I know you are, because there's nowhere else from here unless you're planning to walk into dungeon territory and die. Which I don't think you're doing." Caul stood up from the stump. The right knee gave him trouble — Maren could see the hitch, the way he braced one hand against the stump and pushed through the stiffness, the joint complaining in a way he'd clearly learned to manage but not to hide. "I'm going to give you a day. Not because you've earned it. Because the Crook is where sin-eaters answer for this kind of thing, and I'd rather they handle it than me doing something on a road that I'd have to write up afterward." The rehearsed cadence cracked again, just slightly, and the voice underneath it was tired in a way that went deeper than a bad knee and a long walk. "I want this done right. Haley would've — she would've wanted it done right."

"A day," Maren said.

"Starting now. I'll be a day behind you. If you're at the Crook when I arrive, we do this properly. If you're not — " He picked up his short sword and sheathed it in a motion so practised it looked involuntary, the hand finding the scabbard the way a tongue finds a missing tooth. "Then we do it differently."

He shouldered his pack. The buckles clinked against each other — a small, ordinary sound. He looked at Derry for the first time with something that wasn't tactical assessment. A moment of what might have been recognition — one ordinary man looking at another ordinary man and seeing the specific misery of being here because of someone else's decisions.

Then he walked past them, back the direction they'd come from. His footsteps on the packed earth were even and unhurried. The bad knee didn't slow him — just changed the rhythm, a slight hitch every other step, an asymmetry in the sound that Maren listened to until it faded into the distance and the bird started singing again and the clearing was empty except for the two of them and the three voices in Maren's skull, all of them quiet.

---

Maren sat where he was. The ground was cool through his trousers and a root was pressing into his left thigh — a specific, insistent discomfort that he leaned into because it gave him something to organise his attention around that wasn't the conversation that had just happened.

*Haley Keth,* Aldric said eventually. *She completed her healer certification at twenty-two. Three years of study at the Ashfold academy. She specialised in contamination triage — the medical side. How to stabilise someone exposed to quest residue.* A pause. *She should not have needed to use that training on herself. The area was supposed to be clean.*

*I know.*

*You do know. You have known for eleven years what your work leaves behind.*

*Yeah. I know that too.*

Aldric went silent. Not the pointed quiet of disapproval — Maren knew that version, could feel its edges. This was heavier. The silence of someone sitting with something too large for speech, which was exactly what the paladin had been accusing Maren of avoiding for weeks, and it turned out Aldric wasn't any better at it than Maren was. Just more willing to stay in the room with it.

Maren closed his eyes. The red behind his eyelids was warm from the morning sun and it looked like nothing and like the inside of a body and he kept them shut because opening them meant the clearing was still there and Derry was still in it, four feet to his left, quiet.

"Maren." Derry's voice. Small. The voice of a man who hadn't spoken in a long time and wasn't confident the words would come out in order. "That was — he was talking about — "

"Yeah."

"He's saying that the — that what we — that villages where someone — "

"Yeah."

Derry was quiet. Maren heard him swallow. Heard his hands move against the fabric of his trousers, a dry scraping sound, restless.

"His friends died because someone did what we did," Derry said. Not a question. A sentence he was testing the weight of, holding it out in front of himself to see if it would break something.

Maren opened his eyes. The clearing looked the same — the grey stumps, the brushed-back scrub, the road curving away in both directions, the bird still going at its three-note song as though the morning hadn't changed. The root was still pressing into his thigh.

"Not us specifically," he said. "He said the sin-eater was certified. Class-registered. We were never — I didn't have real credentials, I just had the bowl and the — " He heard himself doing it. The hedging, the careful distinction between their specific fraud and the general category of fraud, the precise parsing of guilt that let him find the gap where his particular culpability didn't quite fill the shape. He heard the performance and he hated it and he couldn't stop, because stopping meant saying the flat simple version, which was: *We did this. Maybe not to his friends. But to someone's friends, in some village, on some day, the contamination we left behind did this or will do this and the only reason we don't know whose friends is that nobody's come to sit on a stump and tell us.*

"But someone like us," Derry said.

"Someone like me."

"Like us, Maren. I fed you the cues. I told you what the System was showing so you could fake the responses. I was there. Every ritual. Every village."

*He is correct,* Vess said. No judgment. An assessment of fact.

Maren looked at Derry. The four feet between them had become architectural — not a gap but a structure, something Derry had built and placed himself on the other side of. His arms were still locked around his knees. His red-brown hair was dirty from travel and pressed flat on one side where he'd slept on it wrong. His face was doing the thing Maren had been afraid of: not anger — Derry didn't have the wiring for the kind of anger this warranted — but understanding. The slow, thorough, irreversible kind. A man looking at his oldest friend and reassembling every shared memory in light of a clearing and a ranger and two names that belonged to dead people who trusted the System to keep them safe.

"How many villages," Derry said. "In eleven years. How many places where the contamination is still — "

"I don't know." True. Maren had never counted. Counting would have required him to think about the villages as a collective, an accumulated weight, and the entire architecture of the fraud had depended on each village being an isolated event. Twelve coppers, a ritual, a bowl, a performance, a nod from the bereaved family. Move on. "I never counted."

"A lot," Derry said. Not asking.

"Yeah."

Derry nodded. He looked at the dirt between his feet again. Then he said, in the voice he used when he was retreating to the only safe ground he had: "Pell's going to be three next month. Lenna's been planning something — she told me before we left. She's going to make a cake with honey from the Grenning apiary. Pell's never had honey cake before." He paused. "She's counting everything now. Stairs, stones, peas in a pod. Lenna says she won't stop. She'll count anything you put in front of her."

Maren said nothing. He understood what Derry was doing — he'd watched it for years, the retreat to Pell and Lenna when the world became too large, the family used as a sea wall against things that couldn't be faced directly. But this time the retreat didn't sound like comfort. This time when Derry talked about Pell it sounded like measurement. Like he was placing his daughter's small ordinary life on one side of a scale and watching something on the other side rise to meet it.

"All those years," Derry said, quieter now, "the contamination in those villages. In our villages. In Fenhold." His voice was running but the connective words had gotten smaller, the sentences losing their cushioning. "People were just living in it, Maren. And we were — we were right there, doing the ritual, and they were watching us and thinking — " He stopped. "Pell was living in it."

The three words landed like a dropped weight.

*He is right,* Aldric said. Not to punish. Just to confirm. The gentleness was worse than the moral lectures had ever been.

Derry turned around. He was still kneeling by his patch of dirt and his eyes were red but he wasn't crying, which was harder to look at than crying would have been. Crying had a simplicity to it. What Derry's face was doing was more complicated — a reassessment, the features of a man who was good and knew he was good and was discovering the specific dimensions of a thing he'd done that wasn't good, and measuring it against his daughter's face.

"Did you know?" he said. "All those years. Did you know the contamination was staying?"

Maren opened his mouth. The truth was right there, simple and complete: *Yes. I didn't know the details or the mechanism or that it could kill people. But I knew the ritual was empty. I knew nothing was being cleared. I didn't stop because stopping meant losing the income and the identity and the one thing I could do, which was pretend to be something I wasn't, and I was good at that and I was afraid of what I'd be without it.* The truth was right there and it was the most dangerous thing in the clearing — not because Derry would hurt him but because Derry would understand him, completely and precisely, and then Derry would forgive nothing, and Maren would deserve both the understanding and the absence of forgiveness.

"I didn't think about it," Maren said. "Not the way you're — I didn't think about it staying. I thought about the ritual and the fee and the next village. I don't know, Derry. I don't know what I thought."

Half a truth and half a lie. Derry knew it. He'd spent eleven years watching Maren build sentences out of exactly this material — true pieces arranged in a shape that wasn't quite true — and he'd learned the architecture even if he'd never built one himself. His face did a small, specific thing: a tightening around the eyes that wasn't anger or grief but was the expression of a man revising his understanding of someone he loved.

He stood up. He turned toward the road without waiting for Maren, without offering a hand, without the half-second pause where he'd usually check Maren's direction and match his pace. He just started walking.

Maren got up. His left leg was stiff from the root and he shook it out. The ceramic bowl in his pack shifted when he shouldered it — he felt it through the canvas, the smooth familiar weight of it against his spine. The same bowl. The same one he'd carried into houses where people were grieving and used to perform a ceremony that did nothing while they stood in the doorway with the particular relief of people who believed the contamination was being lifted from the room where their husband or mother or child had died.

He followed Derry.

They walked. The road was empty and the packed earth was dry and the shadows had that longer, thinner quality that late summer takes on when autumn starts winning. The hum was louder out here — farther from Harrow's System-dense interference, the background frequency of ambient System activity settled into Maren's hearing like tinnitus, the operational noise of classification running beneath the world's surface. He'd heard it his whole life. Aldric had given him the name for it in the first week: the System's maintenance hum, the sound of the wall that held.

Derry walked ahead. Not far. Maybe ten feet. But he didn't fall back. In all their years walking between villages — carrying the fraud like a shared weight between them, splitting it the way they split the fee — Derry had always walked beside Maren. Matching pace. Filling silence. Making the con look like a friendship, or the friendship look like a con, or — and this was the thing Maren had never let himself look at directly — simply being his friend, genuinely, while also being his accomplice, and somehow holding both in the same open, readable, disastrously honest face without either one destroying the other.

Now Derry walked ten feet ahead and the distance was the realest thing Maren had measured in weeks.

*The ranger was not incorrect about the certification framework,* Aldric said after a long silence. The paladin had settled back into his formal register, but there was something under it — not righteousness, not correction. A fatigue that Maren hadn't heard before. The tiredness of a man who had been right about something for a long time and found no satisfaction in the confirmation. *The System does not verify outcomes. It tracks credentials and logs completed rituals. A registered sin-eater who performs the ritual sequence is recorded as having performed the ritual. The contamination itself is never independently assessed.*

*I know,* Maren thought.

*You have known for eleven years.*

*I said I know.*

The paladin went quiet. Not the sharp silence of a point being made. The heavy silence of a man sitting in a room with a thing that was too large and too real and that couldn't be argued away or reorganised through institutional language or official procedure, because the thing was just dead people in a dungeon that should have been safe.

Vess said nothing either, and her silence was its own kind — professional, contained, the silence of someone who had no operational recommendation for the situation and therefore saw no purpose in speech. But Maren had been carrying her long enough to hear the texture of her quiet, and it wasn't empty. It was the silence of a woman who knew what it felt like to carry a thing you'd done that couldn't be undone. Her contract. Her missing sister's name. The gaps in her that nobody was going to fill.

They walked through most of the morning. The road climbed gradually and the air cooled as they gained elevation. At one point they passed a farmstead set back from the road — stone walls, a thin line of smoke from the chimney, a dog that watched them from the gate without barking. Someone had been burning field stubble; the smell of it reached them on the breeze, sharp and ashy and ordinary. Harvest ending. The world doing its work.

Derry stopped at a stream crossing where the water ran fast and clear over flat brown stones. He knelt and cupped water to his mouth. He drank for a long time, then sat back on his heels and stayed there, looking at the water, his wet hands resting on his thighs.

Maren stopped a few feet behind him.

"My daughter's going to be three," Derry said to the stream. Not to Maren — to the water, or to the stones, or to whatever part of the world would listen without having an opinion. "Next month. Lenna's going to make the cake and Pell's going to — she's going to count the candles." He almost laughed. "Three candles. She'll count them six times."

"Derry."

"I keep thinking about — " His voice was running now, the way it did when he was processing something too big for a single sentence, but the usual qualifications and codas were stripped away. Just the bare sentence, trying to carry what it needed to carry. "I keep thinking about the contamination in those villages. In our villages. All those years, Maren. Every time we did the ritual and took the money and walked away and the — and it was still there. In the ground. In the houses where they were sleeping."

Maren stood behind him and looked at the back of Derry's head — the dirty red-brown hair, the sunburned neck, the collar of his shirt gone dark with sweat that had nothing to do with the temperature — and he thought about what it would cost to say: *I'm sorry.* Two words. He'd said them a thousand times to a thousand people about a thousand things he wasn't sorry about, smooth and easy, the apology as professional tool. He couldn't say them to Derry. Not because they wouldn't be true but because Derry would know they were true, and the genuine version of those words carried a weight that Maren wasn't sure he could hand to another person without both of them going under.

Derry stood up. He wiped his hands on his trousers. He started walking.

The ten feet between them held for the rest of the day — steady, precise, the distance of a man who hadn't left yet but had started to measure the cost of staying. They climbed into the cooling hills where the road narrowed and the trees grew closer together and the dungeon territory began its long approach, and the hum grew louder, and the shadows stretched, and neither of them said Pell's name again.


# Chapter 11

The road past Harrow got worse in a way that felt deliberate, as if the land itself was shedding the pretense of being traveled. Ruts deepened and multiplied. Scorch marks on the remaining flat stones grew older and more layered, the kind that came from years of hero-class abilities fired in the same direction without anyone repairing the surface underneath. The ditches were choked with quest-light residue — not the faint shimmer Maren was used to from Fenhold but thicker deposits, waist-deep in places, the pale glow moving sluggishly like something between water and fog.

Derry walked ahead of him. Not far — twenty feet, maybe, close enough to still be walking together — but the twenty feet mattered. Two days ago they'd been side by side, and Derry's silence then had been the shell-shocked quiet of a man processing what Caul had said to them. Now the silence was architectural. Derry had built it, filled in the gaps where conversation used to live with careful stretches of distance and the kind of attention to the road that suggested he was navigating something far more complicated than ruts.

He hadn't mentioned Pell since the night before Caul found them. That was the real tell. Derry talked about his daughter the way most people breathed — because it was necessary, because it happened without effort, because stopping would require a conscious decision that something had fundamentally changed. The absence of Pell's name in conversation sat between them like a third traveler, taking up more room than either of them acknowledged.

*The road drops about forty feet in the next half-mile,* Aldric said. *There is a creek bed at the bottom. The contamination density increases near the water.*

"Worse how?"

*Pooling. Quest residue collected in a natural basin. Roughly six feet by four, moderate depth. Active contamination, not residual — something was abandoned here recently enough that the residue has not fully settled.*

Vess, who had been quiet most of the morning: *Small enough to practice on.*

Maren's foot caught on a root and he stumbled. Derry glanced back, then looked away.

"I wasn't thinking about—"

*Yes, you were,* Vess said.

He had been. Since Caul's quiet, measured explanation of how Haley and Renn had walked into a dungeon that should have been clean, Maren had been running the numbers. Not the kind of numbers Aldric could read off the interface — the other kind, the human arithmetic. Eleven years. Roughly monthly rituals. Call it a hundred and thirty villages, farmsteads, crossroads where someone had died or a quest had expired and the contamination needed clearing. A hundred and thirty times he'd knelt with his ceramic bowl and his murmured nonsense and his careful, practiced sincerity, and the contamination had stayed exactly where it was.

He didn't know how many people that had killed. Thessa might. Thessa probably kept count.

The creek bed came into view through a stand of scrubby sycamores — the water barely a trickle this late in the season, running over dark stones that were darker near the banks where the quest-light had stained them. The pool was exactly where Aldric had said it would be: a shallow depression in the creek bed's widest point, the residue sitting in it like something poured, that faint pale luminescence Maren could see without the System's overlay. Just light. Warm-looking, almost inviting, if you didn't know what it was.

The smell hit him next. Ozone and something sweeter underneath, the specific contamination odor that most people filtered through their interface as data — threat levels, clearance requirements, class-appropriate warnings. To Maren it was just a smell. It had always been just a smell.

"I'm going to try something," he said.

Derry had stopped at the tree line. He didn't come closer.

"Maren—"

"I know."

"You don't know what I was going to say."

"You were going to say it's dangerous, or I don't know what I'm doing, or both, and you'd be right about all of it." Maren set his pack down on a flat stone near the bank. He pulled out the ceramic bowl — plain, chipped at the rim, the same one he'd bought from a potter in Fenhold eleven years ago when he'd first started the fraud. It had no System properties. It was a bowl. "I'm going to do it anyway."

Derry's mouth worked. Whatever he wanted to say got chewed up by the distance between them, and Maren was already kneeling, already arranging the bowl on the flat stone the way he'd arranged it a hundred and thirty times before, the familiar choreography of the fake ritual settling into his body like a language he'd spoken too long.

*That is not how you begin,* Aldric said.

"I know that too."

*The containment posture — Thessa would have started with the containment posture. Hands open, fingers spread, body low. The bowl serves as a focal point for directing the consumption, not as a receptacle.*

Maren remembered Thessa's test. She'd asked him to demonstrate a containment pass, then a residual clear, and he'd failed both so completely that she'd actually laughed — not cruelly, just the exhausted surprise of a professional watching someone demonstrate total ignorance of her field.

But he'd watched her hands while she laughed. The way she held them — loose, not rigid, the fingers not spread in the ceremonial way Aldric was describing but separated naturally, the way you'd hold your hands if you were about to pick up something fragile and you weren't sure how much it weighed.

*Lower,* Vess said. *Whatever you're doing with your back, stop. You look like you're praying. You're not praying.*

"Thanks."

*You're trying to eat something. Get lower. Your center of gravity needs to be beneath the contamination, not above it.*

This was the strange coalition. Aldric brought theory — the Order of the Veil had studied sin-eating mechanics the way architects studied load-bearing walls, from the outside, with measurements and nomenclature. Vess brought the body knowledge of someone who'd spent years learning how to move through spaces that fought back. And Thessa's remembered impatience filled the gaps with practical instruction stripped of both theory and grace.

Maren got lower. His knees pressed into the mud at the pool's edge, cold and gritty, small stones digging through his trousers. He placed the bowl in front of him, on the flat stone where the quest-light was thickest. The light moved around the bowl's chipped rim like water parting, then closing.

The hum was louder here. A low-frequency pressure behind his ears that he'd heard his whole life without anyone else acknowledging it. In Fenhold it had been background noise. Here, this close to the pooled residue, it had a quality he'd never noticed — not steady but pulsing, like something with a rhythm older than a heartbeat.

He opened his hands. Spread his fingers. Not the ceremonial spread but the fragile-object spread, the Thessa version.

And then he had to do the part he'd never done.

---

The fake ritual was performance. That was all it had ever been. He'd learned the postures from watching a real sin-eater work a field outside Fenhold when he was sixteen — an old woman whose name he'd never learned, bent over a pool not much larger than this one, her hands in the light for what seemed like an hour while the farmers waited behind her. He'd memorized the shapes. The shapes were easy.

The real thing required something underneath the shapes. Thessa hadn't named it because she didn't think it needed naming — it was obvious, to her, the way her own breathing was obvious. You opened yourself to the contamination. You let it in. Not metaphorically. The contamination was a physical presence with weight and texture and temperature, and the act of consumption was the act of taking that presence into your body and dissolving it there.

Which meant letting the thing in.

*The Order's theory,* Aldric said, and Maren could hear him choosing words carefully, the formal architecture of his speech reinforced against something underneath, *describes the initial phase as a voluntary permeability. The sin-eater's consciousness must become receptive to the contamination's signature without resisting it. Resistance prevents dissolution.*

"So stop resisting."

*Yes.*

"Helpful."

*I have never claimed to be a practitioner. I am providing the theoretical framework.*

Vess: *Ignore the theory. Think about when you get hit — your body tenses. Absorbs impact. This is the opposite. You need to not tense.*

That was closer. Not perfect — nothing about this was going to be perfect — but closer. Maren looked at the pool. The quest-light sat in it, faintly warm, that ozone-sweet smell thickening in his throat. He'd faked consumption so many times that his body knew the choreography by reflex: lean forward, murmur the incantation he'd cobbled together from half-remembered fragments of the old woman's words, place his hands on either side of the bowl, breathe in, breathe out, wait. In the fake version, nothing happened during the wait. He'd hold the pose until it felt like long enough, then stand up and tell the family the contamination was cleared.

Now he leaned forward, and instead of murmuring the fake incantation he said nothing. He put his hands into the quest-light.

It was warmer than he'd expected. Not hot — warm the way skin was warm, body temperature, alive-feeling. The texture was wrong for light. It resisted his fingers with the gentle friction of something between water and loose powder, a substance that hadn't quite decided on a state. The hum shifted pitch when he touched it, dropping lower, and the smell surged — ozone cracking in the back of his throat like biting tin.

*Contamination levels are responding,* Aldric said. His voice had shifted, the professional architecture thinning into something more attentive. *The interface registers your interaction as — it is difficult to translate. The System sees you touching the residue but cannot classify the interaction. The classification attempt is cycling. It is trying to assign you a role in the process and failing.*

"That's because I don't have one."

*Precisely. That absence is — I am observing something. Continue.*

Maren tried to not tense. He thought about what Vess had said — the opposite of absorbing impact. Getting hit, your muscles locked up before the blow landed, your body smarter than you were, preparing for damage before the conscious mind even registered the threat. The opposite was letting the blow arrive without preparation. Taking the damage open, without the reflex that protected you from it.

He let the quest-light in.

The first thing that happened was his stomach turned over. Not nausea yet — the precursor, the body's early warning that something was entering that didn't belong. The warmth from the pool crawled up his forearms and across his wrists, not on his skin but under it, a sensation like something threading through the muscle fibers of his arms. The ozone taste intensified until he could feel it in his sinuses, behind his eyes, a chemical sharpness that made his vision blur at the edges.

*The contamination signature is — hold. Something is —*

Aldric stopped.

The silence was immediate and total. Not the trailing-off of someone interrupted or distracted — the specific silence of a man who had seen something and could not continue speaking because the thing he'd seen had taken the words out of his mouth.

Maren knew this silence. The same quality as the creek where the fragments had collided and Aldric had gone somewhere inside himself that Maren couldn't reach. But this was different. Aldric was not retreating. His consciousness felt denser inside Maren's skull, heavier, like a weight shifting to one side — leaning toward whatever the consumption was showing him through the System's architecture, paying attention with an intensity Maren could feel pressing against the inside of his forehead.

*Keep going,* Vess said, and her voice was careful in a way Maren had never heard from her. She'd noticed the silence too.

He kept going.

The contamination was inside him now. Not much — a thread of it, maybe less, the leading edge of something larger — but enough to register. It moved through his chest like a cold wire, settling against his ribs, and the wrongness of it was profound, a foreign presence in territory that his body recognized as occupied. His lungs pushed back. His actual breathing hitched, his diaphragm spasming as if he'd swallowed water wrong, and for three horrible seconds he couldn't get air in.

Then something dissolved. Not all of it — a fraction, a wisp of the thread. A sensation like something breaking down in his chest, the cold wire losing coherence, fraying into strands, and then a warmth that was different from the pool's warmth, that came from inside rather than outside. The contamination — some of it, a little of it — had been consumed.

His body rejected the rest.

It wasn't nausea. It was worse than nausea. Every muscle from his throat to his pelvis seized simultaneously, a full-body clench that drove him forward onto his hands, the bowl rocking on the flat stone beside him, creek water splashing up between his fingers. His vision whited out for a count of two — not darkness, whiteness, as if the nerve channels for sight had been flooded with something too bright — and then it came back in pieces, the creek bed reassembling itself in fragments: mud, stone, the quest-light still pooling, his own hands flat in the shallow water with the fingers locked open, the tendons standing out like ropes.

He vomited. Creek water and the hard bread from that morning and something else, something that tasted like copper and ozone, that burned his throat coming up and left a film on his teeth. The retching came in waves, three of them, each pulling from deeper in his gut, and by the third there was nothing left to bring up and his body was just convulsing around emptiness, his back arching and his jaw locked open over the water.

When the retching stopped, the hum was quieter. Not by much — maybe a quarter of its previous volume — but the difference was obvious, the way you'd notice if someone turned down music you'd been listening to for hours. The pool had shrunk. The quest-light at its edges had receded, the pale shimmer pulling back from the banks by a handspan on each side, and the contamination smell had thinned from chemical sharpness to something stale and dissipating, like the ghost of the odor that had been there.

He'd done something. Not enough. Not well. But something real, for the second time in his life, and this time nobody had died.

*Maren,* Vess said. Then, after a pause that was long for her: *You need to roll onto your side.*

---

He didn't roll onto his side. He stayed on his hands and knees because his arms wouldn't unlock and his knees had found some arrangement with the creek-bed mud that was keeping him upright, and changing the arrangement seemed like the kind of decision that could go wrong in ways he was too wrecked to calculate.

The tremors started in his hands and moved inward. Not shaking — tremors, a different thing, a deep-muscle vibration that felt structural, as if the framework underneath his muscles had been knocked loose and the muscles were trying to find their attachments again. His fingers were still locked open, spread in Thessa's fragile-object position, and the tendons across the backs of his hands jumped and rippled in patterns that didn't correspond to any signal he was sending.

"That went great," he said. His voice was wrecked — thin and ragged, the sound of someone who'd been screaming, though he didn't remember screaming.

*You screamed,* Vess confirmed. *About midway through. Derry is standing approximately thirty feet away and has not moved since it started.*

Maren turned his head — slowly, because the creek bed was doing something unreliable with its distances, the stones shifting their relative positions in ways that made his inner ear rebel. Derry was at the tree line, exactly where he'd been when this started, his arms crossed over his chest. He could have been concerned. He could have been holding himself together. The distance between those two readings was the distance Derry had traveled from Maren in the past two days.

"I'm fine," Maren said. It was the most obviously false thing he'd said since the last time he'd told a family their contamination was cleared.

*Roll onto your side.* Vess again, and the patience in her voice still didn't sound like Vess. *If you pass out facedown over the creek, you'll inhale water. It's about four inches deep. That's enough.*

He rolled onto his side. The mud was cold and smelled like iron and crushed leaves and the fading contamination. He lay there with his cheek against the wet ground and let the tremors work through him, the deep-muscle vibration loosening its hold on his ribs and his forearms and the long muscles along his spine in slow, stuttering increments.

*You're shivering,* Vess said. *That's your body trying to regulate temperature after the consumption. It'll pass in a few minutes. Keep your knees drawn up — conserves heat and keeps your airway open if you vomit again.*

"How do you know this?"

A pause. Then: *I was trained to understand what bodies do when they've been pushed past capacity. The specifics are different. Recovery protocol's similar.*

He wanted to make some observation about the gap between assassination training and sin-eating recovery, but his throat was too raw and the thought came apart before it reached his mouth.

Aldric was still silent.

The quiet from that part of his skull had a weight to it. Not absence but a dense, watchful presence — someone who had seen a thing and was still turning it over, examining it from angles, testing it against what he already knew. Maren could feel Aldric's attention directed inward, toward something Maren couldn't see and Aldric wouldn't describe. Not yet. Maybe not at all.

"Aldric."

Nothing.

"What did you see?"

Nothing.

*He won't answer,* Vess said. There was no irritation in it. She was reporting a fact. *Whatever he saw during the consumption, he's still looking at it. Or he's afraid of what it means.*

"Is that a problem?"

*Depends on what he saw.*

Maren pressed his cheek harder into the mud. The cold was organizing — giving his senses something specific and local to hold onto instead of the diffuse wrongness the consumption had left behind, a residual unsteadiness in the center of his chest where the contamination had partially dissolved and partially been expelled. The place where the cold wire had frayed felt tender, raw, like the inside of a healing burn.

"How much did I clear?"

*Maybe a fifth of the pool. Hard to estimate without System data.* A beat. *Not bad for someone who's literally never done this before.*

"A fifth."

*Of a very small pool. A training pool. Not the kind of contamination that collects outside active dungeon mouths.*

"Right."

*But not nothing.*

He lay in the mud and let that sit. Not nothing. A fifth of a small pool, cleared through a combination of remembered instruction and borrowed instinct and whatever counted as sincerity in a man who'd spent his adult life perfecting the art of looking sincere, and the physical cost was — he inventoried it: hands still trembling, chest tender where the contamination had threaded through and been rejected, throat raw from screaming and vomiting, muscles in his back and sides aching with the deep soreness of recent seizure, a persistent wobble in his vision when he moved his eyes too fast. He'd be walking badly for the rest of the day. Maybe longer.

But a fifth of the pool was gone. The part of the creek bed where the quest-light had receded looked different — the stones paler, closer to their natural color, the water running over them without the sluggish quality the contamination gave everything it touched.

*Drink water,* Vess said. *Slowly. If it comes back up, wait and try again.*

His water skin was in his pack, on the flat stone where he'd left it, six feet away. Getting up to reach it seemed like the kind of ambitious project that required planning and maybe a committee.

Derry brought it to him.

He appeared at the edge of Maren's vision, crossing the distance from the tree line without comment. He crouched and set the water skin on the ground next to Maren's head without quite touching him, his hand steady, his face — from this angle, sideways, through mud-smeared vision — closed in a way Maren recognized from years of reading faces professionally. It was the expression of a man who was making decisions and hadn't finished yet. The old Derry would have been talking already. Would have been narrating the whole thing back to him in his looping, circular way, amending and qualifying, filling the aftermath with the noise that was Derry's particular form of care. This Derry set down the water skin and stood and went back to the tree line.

Maren drank. Slowly, the way Vess had said. The water tasted like leather and the copper residue that still coated the inside of his mouth, and his throat closed against it once before accepting. It stayed down.

"I can do this," he said. Not to Derry. Not entirely to Vess, though she was the one who answered.

*You can do this badly. Which is better than not doing it at all, and worse than doing it well. The gap between where you are and where a real sin-eater is could kill you.*

"Yeah."

*I'm not saying don't. I'm saying know what you're risking.*

He lay in the creek bed for a long time. The tremors eased. The mud dried on his cheek in a thin crust that cracked when he finally sat up, pulling himself upright against the flat stone where his pack sat. His ceramic bowl was still there, the quest-light residue drying on its surface in a faint, flaking film. He picked it up and turned it in his shaking hands. An ordinary bowl. Eleven years of prop work, of ritual theater, of kneeling over bodies and contamination pools and speaking his cobbled-together nonsense with the practiced conviction of a man whose entire livelihood depended on looking like he meant it.

For the first time, he'd meant it. His body was wrecked and a fifth of a small pool was cleared and Aldric had seen something that had stopped his mouth and Derry was standing at the tree line deciding whether to keep walking with a man who'd just screamed and vomited and shook in a creek bed for an hour. And the contamination smell that had been thick and chemical when Maren knelt down was thinner now. The air above the pool smelled more like mud and water and the first edge of autumn in the leaves overhead.

He wrapped the bowl in its cloth, his fingers fumbling the edges, the fine tremor still visible in his hands. He spent longer than necessary tucking it back into the pack because the alternative was looking at Derry, who had turned his face down the road toward the Crook — toward Caul's offered judgment, toward whatever version of this story ended with Derry going home to his daughter.

*When he's ready to talk,* Vess said — meaning Aldric, and something in her voice was different, a tone Maren hadn't heard from her, less flat, more careful, the carefulness of someone handling information that might break — *you should listen.*

"Why?"

She didn't answer. The silence was hers this time — not refusal but insufficiency. She didn't know enough to say.

Maren stood up. His legs held, barely, the muscles in his thighs protesting through a sustained wobble that he managed by leaning on the flat stone and then on his pack and then on nothing, standing free for a moment, swaying, before his balance decided to cooperate.

"Let's go," he said.

Derry was already walking. Twenty feet ahead, the distance maintained, the silence architectural.

Inside Maren's skull, the paladin held his own silence — the dense, attentive quiet of a man who had seen something he was not yet ready to name. Not because the evidence was uncertain. Because naming it would change everything, and Aldric was still deciding whether Maren was ready for a world that looked different from the one they'd been walking through.

The road got worse. The hum got louder. And the ceramic bowl, wrapped in its cloth and pressed against Maren's spine, had a faint warmth to it that hadn't been there before — residual heat from the consumption, or the contamination it had channeled, or something else that Maren didn't have a name for yet.

He walked. Badly, favoring his right side where the seizure had pulled something, his hands still trembling when he let them hang. But he walked.


# Chapter 12

The hum had changed.

Not louder — Maren had been hearing the System's hum since he was old enough to lie awake wondering what it was, and it had always sat at the same low pitch, ambient and ignorable, like wind or someone else's heartbeat through a shared wall. But since the consumption at the creek bed — the real one, the one that left him on his hands and knees with his mouth tasting like pennies and ozone while Vess talked him through the shaking — the pitch had dropped. Something underneath the sound he'd always known. A vibration that sat in his back teeth and the flat bones of his skull.

He'd tried to ignore it for two days. Two days of walking through scrub oak and exposed shale, deeper into dungeon territory than he'd ever been, with the road no longer pretending to be a road. Two days of Aldric's silence. Not his usual silences — the pointed ones, the annoyed ones, the ones that came loaded with moral judgment. This was the silence from the consumption. The one that started when Maren's eyes were rolling back and his hands were cramping into claws around the ceramic bowl. Aldric had seen something during the ritual and he'd stopped talking and he hadn't started again, not really.

Derry was asleep across the dead fire, curled on his side with his pack wedged under his head. He'd turned in an hour ago — said he was tired, which was accurate in the way that most of Derry's stated reasons were accurate without being the reason. Derry slept facing the road now. Had been doing that since Caul. His back to Maren, his breath even, his body aimed in the direction of home.

The first cool night of the season. Not cold yet, but the air carried an edge the summer air hadn't — a sharpness that cut through the contamination smell, which was itself different this deep. Less sweet. More mineral. The ozone undercurrent that usually signaled quest residue had thickened into something almost geological, like the air over limestone when it rained.

Maren sat against a birch trunk, the bark a pattern of rough and smooth against his shoulder blades, and watched the embers die. His body had mostly recovered from the consumption. Hands steady. Stomach settled around midday. But the hum sat in his molars and something about the night — the thin quality of the starlight, the way quest-light bled above the southern horizon like a stain on wet paper — made the ordinary act of sitting still feel precarious. As if the ground under him was not quite solid.

*You should rest.* Aldric. The words had the flat, procedural quality of a man checking a box — concern filed, duty noted.

"You've been quiet for two days," Maren said. Low, because of Derry. "Not your normal quiet. Not pissed-off quiet or disgusted-with-my-choices quiet. This is the quiet from the consumption, and I'm done waiting for you to come out of it on your own."

The hum in his teeth. The embers going grey.

"Aldric."

*I know.*

"Then talk to me. What did you see?"

*What I have to say will change how you understand everything. I want you to know that before I begin. Not as dramatic preface — as professional courtesy. I have been trying to organize this for two days and it doesn't organize well.*

"Professional courtesy from the dead man who lives in my skull. Fine. Noted. Talk."

Another silence. Then Aldric's voice, and something in it Maren had never heard — not the formality, the sarcasm, the controlled grief, or the careful architecture of a man who built every sentence like a report. Something underneath all of that. The sound a structure makes when it discovers its own load-bearing limits.

*When I first entered your consciousness, I assumed your System-blindness was a deficiency. An absence. The room was dark and you simply lacked a candle. I remember noting something — a quality to your consciousness that did not match my understanding of blindness. The way the System seemed to slide off you, rather than simply being invisible to you. I filed the observation and continued my assessment. I was wrong about the filing. I should have seen it then.*

"Seen what?"

*You are not System-blind, Maren. That term — blind — is wrong. Everyone uses it because from the inside it looks like absence. You don't see what others see, so the assumption is that something is missing. But from where I am now, having spent seventeen days observing the System's architecture from inside a consciousness it cannot touch — the term is wrong. You are not blind to the System. The System is blind to you.*

Maren's hands, which had been resting on his thighs, went flat. His fingers pressed the seam of his trousers. Each individual thread under his fingertips, the weave worn thin at the knee.

"That's not — what does that mean?"

*It means the interface that every person in this world sees from birth is not a window. It is a wall. And you exist outside it.*

Aldric paused. When he continued, his syntax had changed — shorter sentences, fewer subordinate clauses. The careful report-structure crumbling at the edges.

*I have been watching the System from inside your head for seventeen days. From inside a consciousness it cannot write on, cannot classify, cannot touch. Do you understand what that gives me? Perspective. Everyone else sees the interface from the inside. They see the classes, the levels, the quest prompts, the skill trees. They see the wallpaper. I am seeing the wall. The actual structure. The bones of the thing.*

*And the bones are containment.*

The word dropped into the space between them and sat there.

*The System is a containment structure. Not an organizational framework. Not a benevolent architecture for human potential. It has edges, Maren. Seams. I can see the places where the containment meets what it is containing. The interface everyone sees is the inner surface of a wall — a very old wall, built with a kind of engineering I don't have vocabulary for — and on the other side of that wall there is something. I can see the pressure of it. Where it pushes against the containment. Where the containment has worn thin.*

"What's on the other side?"

Aldric's answer came slower than anything Maren had heard from him. Not reluctance — the careful speech of a man describing something at the absolute limit of his ability to describe.

*I don't know. Not in any way I can classify, which is — that is the point. It predates classification. Whatever is behind the wall existed before the System was built, and the System was built specifically to contain it. Not to destroy it. Not to manage it. To hold it. It is not evil — I don't think it is anything that maps onto moral categories. It is wild. Unclassifiable. Older than anything the System's taxonomy can describe. And it is pushing.*

The hum pulsed. Maren's jaw ached. The vibration traveled through the shale under his boots, through the birch trunk at his back, into his ribs. The ground itself carrying the frequency of whatever was on the other side.

"And my — the blindness. What I am. You said I'm outside the wall."

*The System writes itself onto every human consciousness at birth. The interface, the classifications, the entire framework — it writes on you the way ink writes on paper. You are not paper.* Aldric's voice had dropped to something almost private, the register he never used voluntarily. *You are whatever ink does not adhere to. Glass, stone — I don't know the metaphor. The System has been trying to write on you since you were born and it cannot. It is not a deficiency. It is immunity.*

Immunity.

The word sat in him like the hum sat in his teeth. Twenty-eight years of a different word — blind, lack, missing, broken. Twenty-eight years of working around it, building a fraud on top of it, letting people look at him with that particular gentleness reserved for the damaged. His mother's face when he was small, the way she watched him move through a world she could see the scaffolding of and he couldn't. Not because he was broken. Because the scaffolding couldn't touch him.

Maren stared at the dead fire. The last ember pulsed once, orange, and went out.

"All those years," he said. His voice came out flat and stripped. "All those years I thought something was wrong with me."

*Something is wrong. But not the thing you thought.* Aldric, and his formality had entirely dissolved now, the architecture of his speech matching the architecture he was describing — cracking, load-bearing walls becoming visible. *You are a gap in the containment. Your consciousness exists in a place where the wall has thinned, or where the wall was never completed, or where the wall has begun to fail. System-blindness occurs at points of structural stress. You were not born broken. You were born in a crack.*

*Vess.* Not to Maren. An acknowledgment of presence.

*I'm here.* Flat, present. She had been listening the way she always listened — with the patience of someone who had spent years in dark rooms waiting for footsteps.

*You feel it. The collision between us — the entity it produced.*

*I feel something,* Vess said. *The pressure you're describing. It's been growing since the consumption. I don't have your language for it, but I can feel the shape.*

*The unclassified entity,* Aldric said. *What happened when a holy-order fragment collided with a shadow-guild fragment inside a consciousness the System cannot write on. From the System's architecture, from the structural perspective I now have — it looks like the crack you represent is widening. The entity is the widening. Each time it responds to the System — each time the System tries to classify it and fails — the gap grows.*

"The bounty," Maren said. The understanding assembling itself without effort, the way fraud-logic used to assemble — reading patterns, connecting details, seeing the shape of the con from the outside. Except this wasn't a con. This was worse. "The bounty isn't a punishment. It's maintenance. The System's trying to patch me."

*Yes. The bounty. The classification as unclassified entity. The correction Thessa warned you about. These are repair attempts. The System identified a structural failure — you — and it is sending its maintenance tools — heroes — to seal the breach. The last time it succeeded, sixty years ago, nothing remained of the host.*

"And when I did the consumption. At the creek bed. I felt — the pressure you're talking about. Something pushing back from the other side. I thought it was nausea."

*It was the wall. You opened yourself to the contamination — which is the wall's erosion made material — and for the first time, you interacted with the containment directly. You touched it. From the outside.* A pause. *That is what sin-eating is, Maren. What it has always been. Not waste disposal. Repair. A sin-eater opens themselves to the erosion and seals it. The ritual requires sincerity, openness, because you are not consuming contamination. You are filling a crack with yourself. You are patching the wall from the only side that matters.*

"Thessa," Maren said. "Thessa's been doing this for thirty years."

*From the inside. Every other sin-eater works from within the containment. They can see the wallpaper, and they can see the stains where the wall is leaking, and they can clean the stains. But they cannot reach the structure itself. You can. You are the only sin-eater I have observed who exists on the outside of the wall.*

The night bird had not called again. The scrub oak stood black against the stained horizon, each branch a fracture in the faint quest-light. Derry's breathing was even and unknowing, the breath of a man inside the wall where the wallpaper was still intact and the world still looked the way it was supposed to look.

Maren sat with it.

---

He sat with it for a long time.

Vess didn't speak. Her silence had a different quality from Aldric's — less pressurized, more professional. The silence of someone who processed through stillness rather than speech. Maren was grateful for it. The revelation sat in his chest like something swallowed too fast, the corners of it pressing outward.

He picked at the birch bark behind him. Pulled a strip free, thin and papery, and turned it between his fingers. The texture — rough on one side, smooth on the other, the tree's own containment — was specific and meaningless and he needed something specific and meaningless in his hands right now.

"The Order of the Veil," he said. Not a question. A door he was opening.

Aldric's silence shifted. Became the kind of quiet that happens when a man standing at the edge of a high place realizes someone has noticed him there.

"You said you reached for me. During the ritual. The dead farmer's body, the family watching, me going through the choreography for the eleven-thousandth time. You said it wasn't an accident."

*It was not an accident.*

"You were already dead by then. Fragment in the contamination, residual consciousness, whatever you are. You had choices about where to go. And you chose the guy standing over the corpse with a ceramic bowl and a practiced expression of spiritual gravitas."

*I chose you. Specifically.*

"Why?"

Aldric's voice, when it came, had the careful quality of testimony. Not his usual report-structure — something rawer. A man giving an account he knew would convict him.

*The Order of the Veil. We were assigned to investigate System anomalies. Formally designated System Anomaly Investigation, which made it sound more organized than twelve paladins with a shared interest in the places where the System behaved oddly. Misclassifications. Bounty errors. Zones where the interface glitched or the architecture showed seams it shouldn't have shown. We were supposed to document and report. I was — I was good at it. Fourteen years of training and a temperament that noticed patterns others dismissed.*

The birch bark tore in Maren's fingers. He let the pieces fall.

*Roughly two years before my death, I found something. A pattern in the System's behavior around specific individuals. The System-blind. You are rare — one in a hundred thousand, perhaps fewer. I had assumed, as everyone does, that System-blindness was a random deficiency. A failure of integration. But the pattern showed something different. The System does not fail to perceive you. It avoids you. Actively. The interface bends around a System-blind consciousness the way water parts around a stone in a river. I began investigating why.*

"And you found me."

*I found documentation first. In the Order's oldest archives — records predating the Order itself, transcribed and re-transcribed until the language was barely recognizable. References to the System-blind as structural features of the containment. Not accidents. Not deficiencies.* A pause, and the confession in it was audible. *Features. Designed features. Or emergent ones — I could not determine which. But System-blindness is not random. It occurs at points of structural stress in the containment wall. You were born in Fenhold. Fenhold sits twelve miles from an active dungeon mouth. The contamination density in your region has been above baseline for decades. You were not born blind because something went wrong with you, Maren. You were born immune because the wall needed a crack in that location. You are what grows in cracks.*

The shale was cold under Maren's legs. Each ridge and dip pressed through his trousers into his sit bones, into the backs of his thighs. Solid and ancient and part of the same structure Aldric was describing — the ground itself a surface of the containment, the whole world a wall built over something wilder.

"So you found out about me. Found out there was a System-blind man near a dungeon mouth with elevated contamination. And you went to investigate."

*I requested the assignment. A survey of the dungeon twelve miles from Fenhold. Specifically because of its proximity to a known System-blind individual. I told the Order it was a routine structural assessment. It was not routine.*

"And then you died."

The silence that followed had the texture of a man organizing the last thing he wanted to say.

*The dungeon was stable. It had been stable for years. Three independent structural assessments confirmed integrity. I entered with a full survey team — four others. Standard formation, standard equipment, standard risk profile. Four hours into the survey, the architecture shifted. Not a natural geological event. The System redesignated the internal structure in real time. Walls that had been classified as solid became permeable. Support columns were reclassified as obstacle-class objects and dissolved. The dungeon did not collapse. The dungeon was collapsed. Deliberately. By the System.*

"The System killed you."

*Two of my team made it to the entrance. I did not. The rubble field that buried me was — precise. Not random. The kind of precision that suggests targeting rather than catastrophe.* His formality had returned, the armor of official language closing over whatever he'd been exposed to for those few raw sentences. *I cannot prove intent. I can confirm that the structural changes were not geologically motivated. I can confirm they occurred during an investigation of the System's deepest architecture. I can confirm that no other dungeon in the region experienced similar anomalies that day.*

"You can confirm the System dropped a dungeon on you because you were asking the wrong questions."

*I can confirm correlation. I will not claim certainty about causation because I am a paladin and I was trained to be precise about what I know and what I suspect. But yes. The correlation is — it is what it is.*

"And then your fragment — whatever was left of you in the contamination from that collapse — you reached for me. During my ritual. You grabbed the nearest crack in the wall and climbed inside."

*I needed a consciousness the System could not interfere with. Could not rewrite. Could not reach. The investigation had to continue, and the only safe place to continue it was inside someone who existed outside the containment.* Aldric's voice had gone quiet. The quietest Maren had ever heard it — stripped of the formal architecture, the sarcasm, the grief, everything but the words and the man behind them. *I did not know who you were. I knew your location. I knew your condition. I assumed you were a victim of circumstances, navigating your System-blindness as best you could. I did not know about the fraud. I did not know about eleven years of villages left in contamination. I did not know you were a con man. I chose a stranger because he was the right shape for the job I needed done.*

"Without asking."

*I was dead. I could not ask.*

"You could have not reached. You could have stayed in the contamination and faded like every other fragment. You made a choice, Aldric. You chose to get inside my head without my permission and stay there. Everything that's happened to me since — the voices, the bounty, the killing, Caul's dead friends, Derry's face, the consumption that felt like it was going to pull me inside out — you set it in motion."

*Yes.*

The word landed between them. No subordinate clauses. No qualification. No report-structure to blunt the edges.

Maren stood. The birch bark scattered from his lap. He took three steps toward the dead fire and stopped because there was nowhere else to go — scrub oak and shale in every direction, the road a memory, the world a wall with him standing on the wrong side of it. The quest-light bled above the southern horizon, pale and wrong, the color of milk thinned with water.

"I didn't choose any of this."

*No.*

*He's sorry,* Vess said.

*I'm not saying that.*

*You're saying everything around it. I recognize the shape.* Vess, flat and exact. *You made a decision with incomplete information and the consequences were worse than you expected and you'd make the same decision again because the investigation matters more than one man's consent. That's not sorry. That's a field report with regret in the margins.*

Maren almost laughed. The sound came out through his nose, not quite voice.

"She's got you."

*The investigation —*

"Matters. Yeah. I heard." He sat back down. Not against the birch this time — on the bare shale, the stone biting cold through his trousers. He pulled his knees up and rested his forearms on them. His hands had a fine tremor, not like the post-consumption shaking. A subtler vibration, as if something inside him was recalibrating to a new frequency.

"So. I'm not blind, I'm immune. The System's a wall, not a window. Sin-eaters are maintenance crews. I'm a crack in the wall, and the thing in me is making the crack wider, and the System wants me dead because I'm a structural failure. And you dropped into my skull on purpose because you needed a place the System couldn't reach to finish your investigation." He paused. "And your death probably wasn't an accident. Which means the System kills people who look too closely at the wall."

*That is — broadly accurate. Yes.*

"And the Crook. The sin-eaters there. Thessa. They're all doing repair work on the inside of a wall they can't see the outside of. Patching erosion without knowing what's causing it."

*They know contamination. They know the practical work. They don't know the architecture.*

"Do you know what happens if the wall fails?"

*No.* Shorter than Maren expected. *I don't know what is behind it. I don't know what it would do if the containment collapsed. I only know the wall was built with a kind of engineering that suggests the builders were — were terrified of the alternative. And I know the maintenance has been insufficient for a long time. The contamination Thessa has been fighting is the wall's erosion made material. It has been eroding faster than the sin-eaters can repair. That is why her work is getting harder. That is why the contamination is growing denser across every region. The wall is failing. Slowly. And you are one of the cracks it is failing through.*

"And one of the only people who can actually fix it. Because I'm on the outside."

*Perhaps the only one. I don't know of another System-blind individual currently alive. There may be others. But you are the only one I found, and you are the only one carrying fragments that allow observation of the architecture from both sides simultaneously.*

Somewhere in the scrub oak, a branch cracked under its own weight and fell. The sound was sharp and ordinary and wrong in the quiet. Derry shifted in his sleep but didn't wake. His hand had come free of his blanket and lay palm-up on the shale, the fingers loosely curled. The hand of a man who planted things and pulled weeds and held his daughter while she counted to ten and missed seven every time. A hand that belonged inside the wall, where the wallpaper was enough, where you didn't need to know about the architecture because the architecture held.

Maren looked at the quest-light staining the southern sky. The Woundmouth was out there — the place Aldric had been looking for, the thinnest point in the containment, the place where whatever the System held back pressed hardest against its cage. The Crook was between here and there. Thessa. The sin-eaters. The examination he'd been given a day's head start to reach.

The ceramic bowl was in his pack. Eleven years of prop. One day of tool. Still the same bowl — the same ordinary ceramic he'd bought at the Fenhold market when he was seventeen and starting the longest lie of his life. It had no System properties. It had never had System properties. It was a bowl, and he'd used it to fill a crack in the wall of the world, and the crack had held, briefly, and the wall had hummed against his hands.

He pressed his palms flat on the shale. The hum traveled through his skin, through the small bones of his wrists, into the center of him where the fragments sat and the gap widened and the containment trembled with whatever it was trying to hold. Not a hero's inheritance. Not a chosen one's burden. The janitor-weight of being told the building was unsound and realizing you were the only one who could feel the cracks because you'd been standing in them your whole life.

"Well," he said. "Shit."

*Agreed,* Vess said.

Aldric said nothing, which was its own kind of answer — the silence of a man who had finally given his report and had nothing left to organize.

Maren sat on the wall and listened to it hum, and did not sleep, and waited for the morning to bring whatever a morning brought when the ground under you had changed its name.


# Chapter 13

The first cool morning came with mist. Not the stinging wet of a storm front but something thinner, like breath caught between the hills and held there. Maren woke with his back against a stone wall — the remains of a waymarker shelter, three walls and no roof — and found Derry already up, sitting with his pack on his knees, facing the road.

Not the fire. The road.

Maren catalogued that the way he'd catalogued a hundred tells across eleven years of fraud: the set of Derry's shoulders, the packed bag, the direction of his attention. The grammar of a man preparing to move. He filed it and said nothing.

"Morning," Derry said, without turning. "There's water in the skin. I didn't — I boiled some earlier, it should still be warm. Warmish."

"Thanks."

Maren drank. The water tasted like the iron pot Derry carried, which tasted like every meal they'd shared since Fenhold. He sat down across the dead fire and pulled his boots on, working the left one over the blister on his heel that had gone from raw to calloused over nineteen days.

*He packed before he built the fire,* Aldric observed. *The fire was afterthought. The pack was first.*

Maren didn't answer. The paladin had been quieter since his own revelation — the System as containment, the wall, the crack, the architecture visible only from inside a blind man's skull — and when he did speak it was with a kind of exhausted precision, as if each sentence cost him something he couldn't replenish. The constant sarcasm had thinned. What replaced it was worse: sincerity.

*I merely note the sequence,* Aldric said. *You've already drawn your own conclusion.*

"So," Maren said aloud, rubbing his hands together against the chill. The air smelled different this morning — less of the baked-road dust that had been their constant companion, more of something turning. Green things starting to let go. "How far do you figure we are from the Crook?"

Derry's shoulders shifted. "Three days, maybe? Four, if the road keeps going to shit. I asked at that farm stand yesterday — the woman with the, the bad teeth — she said the valley's about two days past the split, but I don't know which split she meant."

"The one by the creek crossing, probably."

"Right. Yeah. Probably."

Silence. Maren waited for Derry to fill it. Derry always filled silence. That was who he was — the chatter, the running commentary on weather and livestock and his daughter's latest developmental triumph, the nervous warm current that papered over whatever cold thing was underneath. Derry without talk was a fire without heat. You noticed the absence before you noticed the cause.

The silence held.

*Vess,* Maren thought, directing it inward. *You're quiet too.*

*Listening,* she said. Just the one word, and nothing after it.

They packed and walked. The road between Harrow and the Crook was a generous word for what they followed — a track beaten into the ground by sin-eater boots and contamination-survey teams, pocked with old scorch marks from hero patrols that came this way and left their residue burned into the dirt. The terrain rose and fell in long, rolling swells, treeline thickening as they moved deeper into dungeon territory. Quest-light pooled in the ditches like rainwater that glowed faintly from underneath. Maren could see it — had always been able to see the raw shimmer, just not the data overlaid on it that everyone else took for granted.

The hum was louder here. A low-frequency drone that sat behind his teeth and in the hinge of his jaw. System activity, Aldric had explained. The infrastructure maintaining itself. The sound of the wall holding.

Derry walked a half-step ahead. Not alongside. Ahead.

Maren noticed. Of course he noticed. He'd spent his adult life reading the geometry of human bodies — the distance between people, the angle of a turned shoulder, the way someone's stride changed when they wanted to be somewhere else. It was how he'd faked rituals for eleven years: not through knowledge of the System but through knowledge of people. How their faces moved when they were afraid, when they were grieving, when they believed something was happening even if it wasn't. Derry's body was saying something his mouth hadn't gotten to yet.

Around midday they stopped at a stream crossing where the water ran clear over flat stones. Derry pulled out bread and the end of a hard cheese he'd traded for at the farm stand. He handed Maren his portion without meeting his eyes, then sat on a rock facing downstream. Away from Maren.

"Cheese isn't bad," Maren said.

"Yeah, she said it was — I don't know, sheep? Goat? One of those." Derry bit into his bread. Chewed. Looked at the water. "Pell would like this stream.
