She's at that age where she wants to put her hands in everything. Lenna found her elbow-deep in the flour bin last — well, before we left. Covered in it. Looked like a little ghost."

The image landed in Maren's chest like a stone dropped into water. He could see it: the small hands white with flour, the round startled face, Lenna's exasperation that was really delight. Derry had told him about Pell learning to count back at the stream after the killing — the numbers that didn't go in order, the cow that was always "two cow" regardless of how many there were. That conversation had been warm. This one had a blade in it.

"She's probably counting past ten by now," Maren said.

"Yeah." Derry's voice caught, just barely. He covered it with another bite of bread. "Yeah, she might be. Lenna said — in her last letter, before — she said Pell counts the chickens every morning. Keeps getting a different number because they won't hold still."

"Sounds right."

"She only miscounts in one direction, though. Always more than there are. Lenna says it's optimism." Derry's mouth did something that was trying to be a smile. "I think she just can't tell the brown ones apart."

Maren laughed. It came out smaller than he meant it to, and the sound died in the space between them like something that had misjudged the distance.

*He's rehearsing,* Vess said. Her voice inside his skull was flat, toneless, the professional assessment of a woman who'd been trained to read people for different reasons than Maren had. *He's telling himself the reasons. Lining them up.*

*I know,* Maren thought.

*You're not going to say anything.*

*No.*

*That's either kind or cowardly. I can't tell which.*

Maren didn't answer her either.

They walked through the afternoon. The road deteriorated — ruts deepening, scorch marks older and more layered, the ground itself taking on the faint discoloration that came from years of quest-light exposure. A darker hue, like a bruise that had settled into the landscape's skin. The trees were starting to turn, just barely. A single branch of yellow in a canopy of green, like a warning flag someone had hung and forgotten.

Derry tried to make a joke about the smell — the ozone-sweet contamination stink that hung heavier in these lower valleys — and it didn't land. Not because it wasn't funny but because the delivery was wrong. Too bright, too much effort, the laughter offered up before the punchline had earned it. Maren recognized the mechanics: the forced cheer of a person who needed the performance to hold for just a little longer, who was building a bridge out of pleasantries so the collapse, when it came, could feel like a natural ending rather than a fall.

He'd used the same technique a hundred times. On grieving families, on suspicious village elders, on anyone who was getting too close to the shape of his lie. You kept things warm and light and moving forward so that no one had to stop and look at what was actually there.

*The road forks in roughly a mile,* Aldric said. *The left branch descends toward the valley she described. I can see increased System density in that direction — the interface thickens.*

"There's a fork coming up," Maren relayed. "Left takes us down toward the Crook."

"Right," Derry said. "Good."

He said it the way you'd say good to a doctor telling you the results were back.

They made camp before the fork, in a clearing where someone had built a fire ring out of flat stones. The ground was tramped down — other travelers, other sin-eaters maybe, had used this spot before. Derry gathered wood with more energy than the task required, moving through the treeline with the focused busyness of a man who needed to keep his hands occupied. Maren watched him from where he sat building the fire and tried to remember the last time Derry had started a conversation that wasn't about food, weather, or the road.

Three days ago. At least three days ago. Maybe four. Before Aldric's revelation — before Maren had sat in the dark with the paladin's description of the System's architecture ringing through him like a struck bell, containment structure, wall, crack, you are the gap — Derry had asked him something real. He'd asked whether Maren thought they'd ever go home. And Maren, still vibrating with everything Aldric had shown him, had said something honest. He couldn't remember the exact words. Something about not knowing if the person who left Fenhold could go back to it.

Derry hadn't asked a real question since.

The fire caught and they ate. Dried meat, the last of the cheese, water from the stream. Derry faced the road again. Maren faced the fire and didn't comment on the arrangement.

"I was thinking," Derry said.

Maren's hands went still around the water skin. Here it was.

"About — just, logistics. How far you said the Crook is, and what happens when you get there, with the sin-eaters and the — the examination, or whatever Thessa's going to — and I was thinking about what makes sense. For, you know. Going forward."

His voice was doing the thing it did when he was working toward something he couldn't say directly — circling, adding clauses, building a scaffolding of qualifications around the central admission so it could stand up without his having to hold it. Maren had heard this voice before. Derry used it with Lenna when he needed to explain why the money from the last ritual had gone to the tavern instead of the roof repair. He used it when the truth was a shape he needed to approach sideways because looking at it straight on would make him stop talking entirely.

"Derry."

"Because the thing is, and I've been — I know this isn't, I don't want you to think —"

"Derry."

Derry stopped. He was holding a stick he'd been using to push the fire around. His knuckles were white on it. In the firelight, Maren could see the sweat on his neck despite the cool air. The open, friendly face that had made him such a good partner in the fraud — that trustworthy farmer's face, the face that made marks relax — was trying to hold an expression it hadn't been built for.

"Just say it," Maren said. He kept his voice even. Warm, even. The con man's gift: giving people permission to do the thing they were going to do anyway, and making them feel like it was okay.

"I can't come to the Crook."

There it was. Maren's chest did something — not tightening, not the performative physical anguish he'd faked during rituals, but a slow, heavy settling. Like a door closing in a room he'd known was being emptied.

"Yeah," Maren said. "I know."

Derry's face crumpled. Not into tears — Derry wasn't a crier, at least not in front of people — but into a relief so naked it was worse than crying. His whole body sagged, the stick dropping from his hand, and he said, "Shit, Maren, I've been trying to — for days, I've been —"

"I know."

"Pell's going to be — Lenna's going to need — and the longer I'm out here the more —" He stopped. Started again. "She turned two while we were on the road. I wasn't there. I don't even know if Lenna did anything for it, because who's going to help her? Her mother's dead and my brother is — well, you know what Hest is like, he'd forget his own name if it wasn't on his mailbox, and Pell needs —"

"She needs her father," Maren said.

Derry looked at him. In the firelight his eyes were wet, but the tears stayed where they were. "I talked to Caul."

The settling in Maren's chest became something colder. Not surprise — he'd known, somewhere below the level of conscious thought, the way he always knew things about people before they told him. The geometry of Derry's behavior over the last three days had the specific shape of a man who'd already made his deal and was living in the aftermath of it.

"When."

"Two days ago. At the farm stand. When you were — the woman was showing you the road on her — she had that map. I went around back." Derry's voice was steady now, the way it got when the hard part was done and all that was left was the reporting. "He was there. Or, he found me, more like. He must've been tracking us. Following."

*Obviously,* Vess said, inside Maren's skull. *Caul is a ranger. Of course he's been tracking.*

*Be quiet,* Maren thought.

*I'm noting operational fact, not offering commentary.*

*I said be quiet.*

She went quiet.

"What did you tell him," Maren said. It wasn't a question. The flatness was involuntary — his voice losing its texture the way it did when the performance stopped and the real thing underneath couldn't figure out what shape to take.

"I didn't — I didn't sell you out. I didn't tell him where we were camped or how fast we're moving or anything like — I told him the direction. That we were heading for the Crook. That's all." Derry's hands were gripping his own knees now. "He already knew, Maren. He already knew where you were going. He just wanted confirmation."

"And he gave you safe passage."

"He said I could go home. That the bounty's on you, not on me, and if I walked south he'd make sure no hero patrol gave me trouble." Derry swallowed. "He said Pell deserved a father who was alive."

The specific cruelty of that — Caul using Derry's daughter's name, a name Derry must have given him because you don't use a child's name as leverage unless someone has handed it to you first — sat in Maren's mouth like a coin. Not anger. Something flatter and more permanent.

"I'm sorry," Derry said. "Maren, I'm — you're my best friend, you're my — we've been doing this since we were kids, and I wouldn't —"

"Don't."

"I just —"

"Don't apologize. It's the right call." Maren picked up a stone from the fire ring and turned it over in his hands. It was warm from the fire and rough with old char. "Pell needs you. Lenna needs you. You shouldn't have come in the first place."

"I came because you needed me."

"I know." The stone turned in his fingers. "And now I don't."

It was crueler than he meant it. He heard it leave his mouth and knew it was wrong — not the meaning but the angle of it, the sharp clean edge that made it sound like a dismissal instead of a release. Derry flinched.

"That's not — I meant you'll be fine. You've got the — " Maren waved vaguely at his own skull. "I've got company."

*A generous description of our arrangement,* Aldric said.

Derry tried to laugh. The sound that came out was wet and wrong. "Right. Your head people."

"My head people."

"Do they — can they hear this?"

"Yeah."

"Oh." Derry wiped his nose with the back of his hand. "Sorry. To them too, I guess. Or — Aldric, is it? The paladin. And the — Vess. Sorry."

*He does not need to apologize to me,* Aldric said. *He is a father. He is choosing correctly.*

*Tell him I said fine,* Vess said.

Maren relayed neither.

They sat with the fire between them. The mist was coming back, settling into the valleys below the treeline, and the quest-light in the ditches along the road glowed through it — pale, diffuse, like lanterns in a distant house. Maren could hear the hum louder than usual, a vibration that lived in the base of his skull and the roots of his teeth. The System maintaining itself. The wall holding. For now.

"When are you going," Maren said.

"In the morning. Before light, probably. If I head south from the fork I can make the main road in a day and a half, and from there it's — well, it's a long walk. But it's toward home." Derry pulled his knees up. He looked smaller than Maren remembered. Stocky, solid Derry with his farmer's hands and his open face — he looked like a man who'd been carrying something for weeks and was about to set it down, and the relief hadn't arrived yet because the guilt was still in the way.

"Take the cheese knife," Maren said. "And the extra water skin. And the — that fire-starting kit you made in Harrow."

"I'm not taking your —"

"I'll be at the Crook in three days. There'll be other sin-eaters. I'll manage."

Derry opened his mouth. Closed it. Opened it again. "Remember when the worst thing we did was fake Grenn's barn? And you couldn't — you had his widow right there, crying, and you were supposed to be solemn, and that stupid cow came around the corner and you just —" He stopped. His face was doing the failing-smile thing again. "We were nineteen."

"Seventeen. I was seventeen."

"Right. Seventeen." Derry looked at the fire. "The cow was named Patience."

"I remember."

"She'd gotten out of the fence. Again. She always — she was always getting out."

"I remember, Derry."

Something in Maren's tone made Derry stop talking. Not the words — the fracture underneath them, the place where the performance cracked and the real thing showed through. Maren's most honest moments were his least controlled, and right now his voice had a grain in it like wet wood splitting.

Derry stood up. He crossed the fire ring and sat down next to Maren — beside him, not across from him, the first time in days — and neither of them said anything for a while. The mist thickened. The quest-light pulsed once, slowly, like a heartbeat.

"You'll be all right," Derry said. Not a question. A thing he needed to say so he could carry it home with him.

"Sure."

"I mean it."

"I know you do."

*He means it as absolution,* Aldric said quietly. *He needs you to survive so that his leaving was a choice and not an abandonment.*

Maren felt something hot behind his eyes and blinked it back. He focused on the stone in his hand, its weight, its warmth, the specific texture of old char under his thumb. The world was made of things you could hold and things you couldn't, and friendship fell into the second category with a certainty that felt like something he should have understood a long time ago.

"Tell Pell her counting's going to be fine," Maren said. "And tell Lenna I'm sorry about the garden."

"The — what?"

"She planted tomatoes this year. You told me. Back at the stream. First time she's ever tried tomatoes."

Derry stared at him. "You remember that?"

"I remember everything people tell me about the things they love. It's a professional habit." Maren turned the stone over. "Bad professional, but. The habit stuck."

Derry's hand landed on Maren's shoulder. Heavy, rough, the hand of a man who knew soil and livestock and the specific weight of holding a child while she slept. It stayed there for exactly long enough, and then Derry stood up and went to his side of the fire and lay down with his back to Maren and pulled his blanket up and said nothing more.

Maren sat with the stone until the fire died to coals. The mist settled. The hum continued.

*You should sleep,* Aldric said.

*In a minute.*

*There is nothing productive about —*

*In a minute, Aldric.*

The paladin went quiet. Vess was already silent — had been since Maren told her to stop. Her silence wasn't absence; it was the specific withdrawal of a woman who'd been trained to vacate a space without leaving it. She was still there, listening, but she'd given him the room.

Maren set the stone back in the fire ring and lay down. The ground was cold through his blanket. The blister on his heel throbbed in time with his heartbeat, and the hum of the System sat under both rhythms like a third pulse.

In the morning, Derry was gone.

His side of the fire ring was neat — blanket folded, ground swept clean of crumbs. He'd left the iron pot, which he'd carried since Fenhold and which Maren had watched him polish with a rag every night like a man tending to something that mattered more than it should. The pot sat by the cold ashes with the extra water skin propped against it. He'd taken the cheese knife, and the fire-starting kit, and nothing else.

Maren stood in the grey pre-dawn and looked at the road forking below the clearing. The left branch descended into the valley toward the Crook. The right branch — he couldn't see it. It disappeared into the mist, heading south, heading toward the main road and Fenhold and a woman whose tomatoes might have come in by now and a girl who counted chickens wrong every morning with a faith in abundance that her father was walking home to protect.

He could feel the place where Derry had been like a gap in the air. Not dramatic. Not a wound. Just the specific absence of a particular person — a voice, a warmth, the sound of nervous chatter filling every silence because silence was the one thing Derry couldn't tolerate. Nineteen days of that chatter, and before that eleven years of it, and before that the two of them at seventeen in a dead man's barn with a cow named Patience and the beginning of a fraud that would feed both their families and ruin more than either of them could count.

*The iron pot,* Aldric said. *He left you the pot.*

"I can see that."

*In my experience — limited, admittedly, to memories of people who are now dead — a man does not leave behind something he polished nightly unless the leaving carries weight he cannot speak.*

"Thanks for the analysis."

*You are welcome. It was not intended as comfort, but if it functions as such, I will accept the secondary purpose.*

Maren picked up the pot. It was cold and heavier than it looked. He put it in his pack, on top of his ceramic eating bowl — the bowl he'd bought at seventeen to fake rituals with, the bowl that had become something real somewhere between the first botched consumption and the awful half-success by the creek bed when his body had tried to turn itself inside out and Vess had talked him through breathing like a woman coaching a stablehand through a broken leg.

Two things in his pack that used to be fake and weren't anymore. The bowl and whatever was left of the friendship, which had been built on a lie and had turned into something genuine and was now sitting at the bottom of his bag in the shape of an iron pot that a man polished every night because it was the thing he could care for when his daughter was two hundred miles away.

*The left fork,* Vess said. Speaking for the first time since he'd silenced her. *The Crook. Three days.*

"Yeah." Maren shouldered the pack. The weight had redistributed — one person's gear where two people's had been. Lighter in ways that didn't feel like relief. "Three days."

He took the left fork. The road descended into the valley, into the thicker mist, into the contamination-heavy air that smelled of ozone and overripe sweetness and the faint mineral undertone of System infrastructure running at capacity. The hum grew louder. The quest-light in the ditches glowed brighter through the mist. Behind him, the right fork was already invisible.

Somewhere south, Derry was walking home. And somewhere behind them both, Caul knew which direction Maren was heading, because a man who loved his daughter had told him, and that was the thing about betrayals that came from love — you couldn't even hate them. You just had to keep walking, and carry the pot, and not look back at the fork in the road where the last person who'd known you before any of this was choosing his child over your friendship, and being right to do it.

Maren walked. The road was bad and getting worse. The mist thinned as the sun climbed, burning off in patches that let the quest-light show raw and pale against the darker soil. Three days to the Crook. Three days alone with a dead paladin and a dead assassin and the knowledge that the System was a wall, and he was a crack in it, and somewhere behind the wall something older than classification was pressing against the architecture with the patient weight of a thing that had been waiting a very long time.

But that was tomorrow's problem. Today he had a road, a pack, an iron pot, and a bowl. And the specific, non-transferable loneliness of a man whose best friend had just done the right thing.

He kept walking.


# Chapter 14

The valley between the dungeon mouths tasted like scorched copper.

Maren had been walking through worsening contamination for two days, the smell thickening from background haze to something that lodged behind his sinuses and wouldn't clear, but the last quarter-mile dropped into a different register entirely. The ground under his boots was grey-dark and slick with decades of accumulated quest-light residue. The hum from three dungeon mouths — northeast, southeast, and the wide one dead ahead — layered into something that wasn't quite a sound anymore. It pressed against his chest, a low vibration that worked up through the soles of his boots and settled into the gap where the unclassified thing lived, and the thing hummed back.

*Six sin-eaters,* Aldric said. His voice had the edge it got when he was cataloguing rather than commenting — precision as habit, the ghost of field reports filed to an order that no longer existed. *All registered. Properly classified — maintenance tier, if the System still bothers to differentiate. The profiles are thin. I don't think anyone's updated them in years.*

"Six," Maren said. He'd been expecting more.

*The historical registry shows fourteen designations for this site. Most are marked inactive.*

"Dead?"

*Or quit. The classification doesn't distinguish.*

The valley opened below him in the last of the evening light, and the Crook was smaller than anything he'd prepared himself for.

He'd had a picture in his head — not grand, not that naive, but substantial. A camp with structure. Tents in patterns, a central fire, the organized bustle of people who did the same essential work gathering to share the load. Instead: five canvas shelters strung between boulders on the valley floor, smoke from two cook fires, a handful of figures moving with the careful efficiency of people who had stopped wasting motion years ago. One of the shelters had collapsed on its windward side and no one had fixed it. A line of washing hung between two stunted trees, the clothes stiff and faded from contamination exposure. The whole thing looked less like a professional gathering and more like a disaster camp that had forgotten to leave.

*Three approach angles from here,* Vess said flatly. *Two with cover. One visible from every point in the camp. You're on the visible one.*

"Good."

*That wasn't a recommendation.*

"I know." He kept walking down the exposed slope, hands open at his sides, no pack on his back because he'd lost it two days ago and hadn't replaced it. Nothing on him but his clothes, his bowl wrapped in cloth inside his shirt, and three coppers he'd found in the mud outside a collapsed waystation. Coming to the sin-eater gathering looking like exactly what he was: a man with nothing left to try.

The absence to his left was louder in the silence. No nervous chatter about the road or the weather or whether the bread had gone off. No second pair of feet on packed dirt, no Derry filling every quiet moment with stories about his daughter's counting or his wife's tomatoes or the cow that wouldn't stay fenced. Four days of walking alone, which meant four days of walking with only the voices in his head, and the voices in his head had the decency to be quiet about the person who should have been walking beside him.

The woman by the nearer cook fire saw him first. Short, square-shouldered, a grey bandage wrapped around her left hand that had gone stiff and dark at the edges. She stood and said something to the man sitting behind her — older, weathered, working a piece of dried root between his teeth with the concentrated patience of a long habit — and both of them turned to face the slope with expressions that had nothing to do with recognizing Maren's face and everything to do with what the System was telling them about the thing walking toward their camp.

Then Thessa came around the far shelter, a ceramic bowl in her stained hands, and stopped.

The distance between them was maybe forty feet. She looked the same as she had in Silt — tall, angular, grey-streaked hair cut short for practicality, hands dark to the wrist from thirty years of contamination exposure. Tired in a way that had gone structural, settled into the bones of her face. She looked at Maren the way she'd look at a contamination pool that had appeared where one shouldn't be.

"You came," she said.

"Wasn't anywhere else."

"No." Her eyes tracked across him, or past him — reading the interface, the profile, whatever it showed about the thing he was carrying. He'd never seen what they saw when they looked at him, but he'd gotten better at reading the reactions. The bandaged woman's reaction was fear. The older man's was professional assessment, the kind that measured before it responded. Thessa's was harder to parse. Something between fury and a recognition she didn't want.

"Your friend's not with you," she said.

"No."

She waited. He didn't elaborate. The silence held the shape of everything he wasn't going to say about Derry, about a man who'd chosen his daughter over a fight that wasn't his, about waking up on the road alone for the first time in weeks with the understanding that he had earned exactly this.

Thessa turned to the bandaged woman. "Petra. Get him water."

"I saw his profile," Petra said. "Thessa, that bounty—"

"I saw it too. Get him water."

Petra went. Maren reached the camp's edge and stopped because he'd learned not to walk into spaces where people were afraid of him. He stood at the border — contamination-darkened stone on both sides — and waited to be invited or turned away.

The older man finished chewing his root, spat the end into the fire, and stood. Taller than Maren had expected from above, with the kind of angular thinness that came from decades of physical work on insufficient food. His hands were stained like Thessa's — the dark residue that wouldn't wash out, the visible record of years of contamination handling. He looked at Maren with an expression of measured calm.

"Ruald," he said. "I work the western reaches."

"Maren."

"I know who you are. Your bounty's been visible for weeks — every hero in three provinces can see it." Ruald's voice was dry, unhurried, the voice of a man who'd spent enough years in contaminated silence that conversation was something he maintained rather than needed. "Thessa told us about Silt."

*He doesn't seem hostile,* Aldric said carefully. *His System profile shows contamination tolerance readings well below baseline. He's been absorbing more than he should be, for longer than is sustainable. All of them are.*

"Overworked," Maren murmured.

*Severely. Yes.*

Thessa was watching him from across the camp. Behind her, two more sin-eaters had emerged from the shelters — a young man barely past twenty who kept touching his own wrist as if checking for something he couldn't find, and a woman who stayed half-inside the canvas opening, arms crossed, watching with the fixed attention of someone deciding whether to run.

"Sit," Thessa told him. She pointed at a flat stone near the cook fire. "Eat if they'll feed you. Tomorrow we look at what you are."

"And then?"

"That depends on what we find."

He sat. Petra brought him water in a clay cup with a crack running down one side, mended with brown resin. She set it on the stone beside him rather than placing it in his hand — the distance of a woman who'd seen his readings and didn't want to be within arm's reach. Then she sat across the fire and unwound the bandage from her hand, working carefully, and the skin underneath was raw and mottled with a contamination pattern Maren recognized from his own failed attempts — the discoloration of flesh that had opened to something toxic and hadn't fully recovered.

"Bad consumption?" he asked.

She looked at him sharply. "You'd know what that looks like?"

"Had a few."

"Yeah. Source pool in the northeast mouth. Standard approach barely dented it and my hand was in the bowl when the backwash hit." She shook her head and started rewrapping. "Doesn't matter."

"She nearly lost the use of it," Ruald said, settling back onto his stone with a new piece of root. "The contamination is denser this season. The standard approaches work less than they did."

The fire was warm. The cool of early autumn was settling into the valley with the fading light, the first real chill Maren had felt since leaving Fenhold, and his body registered it gratefully after weeks of late-summer heat on dusty roads. His legs ached from four days of solo walking. The gap in his chest — the space he'd started thinking of as the crack, because Aldric had called it that and the word had stuck — pulsed heavier in the presence of concentrated contamination, as if it were responding to the density around it. A slow, dull throb in time with the triple hum.

*The young one is still watching you,* Vess said. *He's not deciding whether to trust you. He's deciding whether you're going to kill them in their sleep.*

Maren looked at the young sin-eater, who stood ten feet away with his hand still on his wrist, his face tight with an anxiety that didn't seem to belong exclusively to Maren's arrival — more like the residue of something that had been building for a while.

"I'm Maren," he said.

The young man said nothing. After a moment, he went back inside the shelter.

"Hale," Petra said. "He doesn't talk much lately. The work's been..." She trailed off, poked the fire with a stick.

The valley settled into its evening sounds — the low compound hum of three dungeon mouths, the crack and pop from the fires, the distant drip-echo of quest-light pooling somewhere below the surface. Maren ate what Ruald put in front of him: grain porridge with a heel of dense bread that tasted faintly of the contamination in the water they'd used to cook it. Everything here was contaminated. The food, the water, the air, the ground. These people lived in it, worked in it, ate it, breathed it, and went back to work the next morning.

He slept that night near the fire, wrapped in a blanket Ruald gave him that smelled of ritual residue and woodsmoke. The hum from the dungeon mouths didn't stop. Neither did the thing in his chest, which pulsed faintly in time with the contamination outside, as if it were breathing too.

---

The examination started at dawn, when the light was grey and the contamination smelled sharpest.

They didn't call it a trial. They didn't call it anything. Thessa told him to sit on the flat stone near the collapsed shelter where the contamination was densest, and the five other sin-eaters arranged themselves around him at varying distances — Thessa closest, Ruald three paces back, Petra and the woman from the shelter doorway (Denna, her name turned out to be, and she still hadn't spoken to Maren directly) at the midpoint, and Hale at the furthest remove, his hand on his wrist, his face white.

Thessa knelt in front of him. She held her bowl — stained dark, edges worn smooth, a tool that had seen thirty years of genuine use — and closed her eyes. Around her, the others did the same.

*They're scanning you,* Aldric said. *Through the contamination channels — using their sin-eating sensitivity to read your residue patterns. I don't have a clean term for it. The closest I can get is that they're listening to your contamination the way a physician listens to a heartbeat, but through the System's maintenance layer.*

Maren sat still. Something shifted in the contamination around him — not their attention exactly, but a change in the ambient density. The contamination pulled toward the sin-eaters' open channels the way water flows toward a drain. And inside him, the fragments stirred.

*They can feel us,* Vess said. Her voice was flat and controlled, which meant she was working to keep it that way. *Both of us. The scan is reaching the fragment layer.*

*I'm aware.*

*They shouldn't be able to reach that deep. That's not standard sin-eating.*

*No. But this close to three active dungeon mouths, with contamination this dense, their sensitivity range is significantly extended. They can see things that wouldn't be legible in a normal setting.*

Twenty minutes. Maren sat through it the way he'd sat through every uncomfortable examination of his life — a performance of stillness that had once been a fraud's best tool and was now the closest thing to honesty he could manage. He couldn't see what they were reading. He couldn't contribute to his own assessment. He sat on the stone and waited for the verdict while people with decades of real skill read the contamination in his chest and decided whether it was something they could work with or something they needed to destroy.

Thessa opened her eyes first. Her face was tight — the expression of someone who'd found something worse than expected and was still working out whether to be angry or afraid.

"How long have you been carrying the second fragment?" she asked.

"Since the road past Fenhold. Sixteen, seventeen days."

"The collision between them has gotten worse. The opposed classifications are generating friction at the interface boundary — two currents pushing against each other in a narrow channel. The turbulence is—" She stopped. Looked at Ruald. "You felt it?"

Ruald nodded. His jaw worked steadily on a fresh piece of root. "The fragments aren't seated properly. Neither of them. It's as if they're floating in the host rather than anchored, and the space between them—"

"The unclassified thing," Thessa said.

"Yes."

"It's grown since Silt."

"Considerably."

Maren's hands were flat on his knees. The gap pulsed once, dully, as if it knew it was being discussed.

*She's not wrong,* Aldric said quietly. *Whatever we are — whatever I am, inside you — it's less stable than it was two weeks ago. Whether that's the entity or the proximity to this level of contamination, I can't say. But something has shifted.*

"There's more," Thessa said. She stood and faced the circle. Her voice hardened into something instructional — the voice of the most experienced person in the room who was done being polite about it. "His contamination pattern is inverted. When he consumes — even the partial consumptions he's managed — the contamination doesn't clear. It redistributes. He's moving it, not dissolving it."

Silence.

Denna spoke for the first time. Her voice was rougher than Maren expected, scratched by years of contamination exposure or maybe just a voice that had never been smooth. "Where does it go?"

"Outward. Wider dispersal, lower density. It reads as reduced locally, but the total volume—" Thessa stopped. The next words cost her something. "The total volume isn't decreasing. He's spreading contamination across a wider area and making it look like clearance."

The silence this time landed differently. Maren could read it in the way Petra's bandaged hand clenched around nothing, in the way Ruald's jaw stopped working, in the way Hale took a step backward — the horror of contamination workers discovering that a contamination source had walked into their camp.

"Every village you touched," Thessa said, turning back to him. "I told you this in Silt. The contamination is still there. But it's worse than I thought. You haven't just been leaving it in place — you've been redistributing it. Thinning it, spreading it. Making it harder for real sin-eaters to find and clear because it's no longer concentrated enough to locate through standard channels."

"I didn't know," Maren said.

"No. You didn't. Because you were faking."

He had nothing for that.

*She's right,* Aldric said. *I've been watching the pattern since your consumption attempt at the creek. The contamination enters you, and when you can't fully dissolve it — which you can't, not yet — it exits through the gap. The entity acts as a diffusion engine. It scatters what you take in across a wider field.*

"Consume him," Thessa said.

It took Maren a moment to understand she wasn't talking to him. She was talking to the circle.

"Break down the fragments. Dissolve the unclassified structure. It's the same process we use on contamination that can't be cleared through standard consumption — we eat it. One of us takes him, and we end this."

"Thessa—" Ruald began.

"He's carrying two hostile fragments in an unstable configuration, an entity the System itself can't classify, and a contamination pattern that poisons every area he passes through. He's not a sin-eater. He's a contamination event with legs. If a pool showed these readings, we'd consume it without discussion."

"A pool doesn't speak," Ruald said mildly.

"Neither does the contamination in the villages he's been spreading for eleven years. The children growing up in it don't get to speak either."

Maren took it. He sat on his stone and looked at Thessa's stained hands and understood, with a clarity that bypassed every defense he'd ever built, that she was right. From every angle that mattered to these people — the only people who did the work that kept the world from drowning in its own waste — he was the problem, not the solution.

"Who takes him?" Petra asked. The question was practical, not moral, and the practicality was worse. "I can't. My hand's barely functional. Ruald?"

Ruald shook his head slowly. "His readings are too high. The unclassified structure alone exceeds my consumption capacity. If any of us attempted a full consumption and failed, the backwash would be catastrophic. We'd be redistributing him instead of dissolving him."

"Then we — what? Call someone who can?"

"There's no one to call," Thessa said. The anger in her voice cracked, and underneath it Maren heard something he recognized from the inside — the exhaustion of someone who'd been carrying a weight alone for so long that the weight had become invisible to everyone but her. "There are six of us. There should be fourteen. The contamination is denser every season and there are fewer of us every year and the standard approaches don't work the way they did a decade ago, and I—"

She caught herself. The control came back down over her face like a door closing. The professional. The one who did the work.

"I don't have a clean answer," she said, and the words sounded like they'd been pulled out of her against her will. "But leaving him as he is means leaving the problem to grow."

*She almost said it,* Vess said. *That was as close as she's ever gotten to admitting the work isn't holding.*

Maren looked at Thessa's face and saw it: the private doubt she'd been keeping sealed. Not just about him. About all of it. The contamination getting worse. The methods losing ground. The numbers dwindling. The work that the System classified as maintenance-tier, that no one funded or praised or even noticed until it wasn't done, that was somehow supposed to be performed by six exhausted people in a poisoned valley while heroes left scorch marks on clean roads and collected bounties worth more than a sin-eater would see in a lifetime.

"Test me," Maren said.

Thessa's eyes snapped to him.

"You can't consume me safely. You said it yourself — the readings are too high, the risk is too great. So test me instead. Make me do a consumption under your supervision. If I can't manage it, if I'm nothing but a contamination event with legs—" He used her words deliberately, watched them register. "Then you work out something else. But you've got nothing to lose from watching me try."

"Except more redistributed contamination across our workspace," Denna said from her position in the circle.

"If it goes wrong, the contamination's already here. This valley's baseline is worse than anything I could add."

*That's not entirely true,* Aldric said. *But it's close enough to be tactically useful.*

Ruald spat the end of his root into the dirt. "Let him try."

Thessa looked at Ruald. Looked at Maren. Looked at the valley around them, the three dungeon mouths breathing their contamination into the low ground, the grey-dark stone, the faint shimmer of quest-light pooling in the morning air — the raw glow without data, without labels, without the overlay the others navigated by.

"Fine," she said. "Supervised. Every step. And if what I see looks like redistribution instead of clearance, I stop you. Hard."

"Fine."

"Get up. We do it now."

---

They moved to the nearest dungeon mouth within the hour, the morning mist still thinning around them. Vapour rose from the contaminated ground in thin tendrils, catching the pale light, and the hum from the opening was a physical pressure that Maren could feel in his teeth. The stone around the mouth was nearly black from years of quest-light saturation, and the contamination smell was dense enough to coat the inside of his throat.

Maren knelt ten paces from the mouth with his ceramic bowl unwrapped and in his hands. The bowl — the ordinary ceramic he'd bought in a Fenhold market at seventeen, the prop of eleven years of fake rituals — sat on the ground in front of him, and in this place, surrounded by real sin-eaters, it looked exactly as fraudulent as it was. No staining. No ritual wear. Clean, cheap, useless.

Thessa stood behind him and to his right. Ruald, Petra, Denna, and Hale were arrayed in a loose half-circle at twenty paces — close enough to observe, far enough to absorb the backwash if the consumption went wrong. Hale had both hands at his sides now, fingers curled, watching with the rigid attention of a young man who didn't want to be the first to flinch.

"Kneeling posture," Thessa said. "Bowl in front of you, open. Elbows off your knees — you need your chest open for the intake."

He adjusted. The stone was cold and damp through his trousers.

"Breathe first. Not the performance breathing you've been doing — the actual pattern. In through the nose until you feel the contamination enter your sinus cavity. Hold it. When the ozone taste reaches the back of your throat, open."

*She's describing the primary intake sequence,* Aldric said. *It matches what the Order's texts describe, but more embodied. Less theoretical. She's teaching from practice, not doctrine.*

Maren breathed in. The contamination entered through his nose — not a metaphor, a physical sensation, the dense copper-sweet mixture that was the valley's baseline air — and he held it. The taste crept along the roof of his mouth, down the back of his throat. The gap in his chest stirred.

"Open," Thessa said.

He opened. Not his mouth — this wasn't about the mouth, despite the name. Opening was the thing he'd learned on his own near the creek bed, the thing that had nearly broken him: a deliberate surrender of the boundary between himself and the contamination around him. Like unclenching a fist he hadn't known was clenched, somewhere behind his sternum. The inside of his chest peeled back, and the contamination rushed in.

*Intake volume is—* Aldric's voice sharpened. *That's significantly more than your solo attempt. The density here is — Maren, slow down. You're pulling from more than the immediate pool. You're drawing from—*

"Wider," Thessa said, and her voice had changed. The instructional flatness was gone. "He's pulling wider. Ruald — do you see the radius?"

"I see it."

The contamination poured through the gap and the gap was not a hole but a channel, a crack, and the crack widened as the contamination flowed, and the flow was not something Maren was controlling. His hands were locked on the cold stone beside his knees. His bowl sat empty because the bowl was for collection and there was nothing to collect — the contamination was going through him, not into him, passing through the space where the unclassified entity lived and coming out the other side as something that read, to everyone watching, like clearance. His vision blurred. The hum from the dungeon mouth rose in pitch and the mist around the opening churned and his body wanted to double over and vomit but Thessa's hand was on his shoulder, holding him upright, her grip hard and professional.

"Don't collapse. Keep the channel open. If you close it with contamination still in transit—"

He kept it open. His muscles were shaking. Aldric had gone quiet — the quiet that meant he was seeing something in the System's architecture that outpaced language — and Vess was counting.

*Fifteen seconds. Your body starts losing structural coherence around thirty. Close soon.*

"I can't — I don't know how to close it from—"

"Close the same way you opened," Thessa said. Her voice was tight but held. "Clench. The fist behind your sternum. Find it and close it."

He found it. He closed it. The contamination flow cut off like a valve shutting and the shock — the abrupt cessation of something that had been flowing through the core of him — knocked him sideways off his knees. He caught himself on his palms. His bowl tipped and rang against the stone, a clear ceramic note that sounded absurd in the contaminated dawn. His arms shook. His stomach heaved once, dry, and he spat on the ground and the spit was dark with residue.

"Stay down," Thessa said. She was standing over him, reading the interface. Around her, the other sin-eaters were reading too.

He was on his hands and knees on contaminated stone, shaking, spitting dark, and the gap in his chest felt wider than it had before the consumption, as if something had been stretched that wouldn't go back. His bowl lay on its side next to his hand, empty. A prop. A fake thing in a real place.

*Maren.* Aldric's voice came back, rough, stripped of its usual register. *The ambient contamination readings across the valley have decreased. Not just around the dungeon mouth. The entire valley. Every reading point I can access has dropped, by a margin that would normally require days of individual consumption by multiple sin-eaters.*

The silence in the circle was different now.

Maren got himself back to kneeling. His hands were smeared with the dark residue of contaminated stone, his forearms trembling. He picked up his bowl and set it flat in front of him and looked at the clean white interior — his eleven-year prop, his fake tool — and then he looked at Thessa.

She was staring at the interface readouts with an expression he had never seen on her face before. Not anger. Not disgust. Something more complicated than either — the look of a woman who'd been doing essential work alone for decades watching someone accomplish in twenty seconds what she couldn't do in a full day, and trying to reconcile that fact with the knowledge that the someone was a fraud, a contamination hazard, and an unclassified threat to everything she'd built her professional life around.

"The redistribution pattern," Ruald said. He was chewing a fresh root, but his voice had changed — something careful in it, something wondering. "It's different from what you described. The contamination didn't scatter. It decreased. Genuinely. The total volume in the valley dropped."

"I can see that," Thessa said.

"How?" Petra asked. She was flexing her bandaged hand, staring at whatever the interface was showing her. "That's not — sin-eating doesn't work like that. We consume and dissolve. We don't just reduce ambient levels across an entire valley in one intake."

*The unclassified entity,* Aldric said. *It's acting as an amplifier. Or a wider aperture. The standard sin-eating process works through a narrow channel — one pool, one consumption, one dissolution. But the gap in Maren's chest isn't narrow. It's connected to the broader contamination structure in a way that individual sin-eaters aren't. When he opens, he opens wider than any of them can.*

Thessa was looking at Maren. The other sin-eaters were looking at Thessa.

"The last person who could do this," Ruald said slowly. "Sixty years ago. You told us about them."

"They were consumed by the System," Thessa said. "Corrected. Nothing left."

"But before that—"

"Before that, the records say contamination levels in their region were the lowest in a century."

The mist was thinning in the growing light. The hum from the three dungeon mouths continued, as it always did, as it would continue regardless of what six exhausted sin-eaters and one fraudulent newcomer decided to do about it. The contamination was still there — thick, structural, getting worse every season. The valley still needed more people than it had. Petra's hand was still raw under the bandage. Hale was still standing at the far edge of the half-circle, though he'd moved three steps closer at some point and his face had shifted from fear to something closer to wary attention.

Thessa looked at Maren for a long time. He knelt on the cold stone with his dirty hands and his clean bowl and his trembling arms and the gap in his chest that was wider than it had been an hour ago, and he waited.

"You're sloppy," she said. "Your intake form is wrong. Your closing is dangerous — you're shutting the valve too hard and you'll give yourself internal contamination rebound within a month. You have no control over the width of your aperture, which means any consumption could pull more than your body can structurally handle. You're a hazard to yourself and anyone standing near you."

"Yeah."

"And you did more in twenty seconds than I've done in a day."

He didn't know what to say to that. He'd never known what to say when someone told him the truth about himself — he'd spent eleven years making sure no one had the chance.

*Don't say anything,* Vess said. *She's working through something. Let her get there.*

Thessa looked at her own hands. The stained, weathered hands of a woman who'd done the real work for thirty years, who'd cleared contamination from farmland and villages and the spaces where children played, who'd done it alone and underfunded and unrecognized while the System classified her labor as maintenance-tier and heroes collected bounties worth more than she'd earn in a decade. She looked at her hands and she looked at the valley and she looked at the man kneeling on the contaminated stone with his fake bowl and his real problem.

"Not entirely useless," she said.

Maren breathed out.

"We start with the intake form. Your posture's wrong, your breathing's wrong, your closing technique will kill you within a month if you keep doing it that way. You don't eat, sleep, or breathe without my say-so until I'm satisfied you won't blow us all apart." She paused. "And the first time you fake anything — one gesture, one posture, one breath that's performance instead of practice — I stop teaching and we go back to the consumption discussion. Clear?"

"Clear."

"Get off the ground. You look like you're praying."

He got off the ground. His legs barely held. Aldric was quiet in the way that meant he was still processing, and Vess was quiet in the way that meant she'd already processed and was running scenarios for what happened next. The morning light reached the bottom of the valley and the quest-light pooling near the dungeon mouths caught it — a faint shimmer, like water reflecting something Maren couldn't quite see, beautiful in the way things were beautiful when you didn't have the data to know what you were looking at.

Thessa walked toward the cook fire. After a moment, Petra followed. Then Ruald, chewing his root. Denna went back to her shelter without comment. Hale stood where he was, still watching — but he nodded at Maren once before turning away, and the nod carried the weight of something being permitted that hadn't been before.

Maren picked up his bowl. The ceramic was cool in his hands, the same cheap clay it had always been, no heavier and no lighter for what had just passed through it. He wrapped it in its cloth and held it against his chest where the gap pulsed faintly in time with the contamination in the air, and walked toward the fire where six sin-eaters were already beginning their day in the valley between three dungeon mouths, doing the thankless essential work that kept the world from choking on itself.

He had not been invited. He had not been welcomed. He had been judged dangerous, nearly consumed, and grudgingly spared because his particular kind of danger happened to do something useful. It was the worst version of belonging he could imagine, and it was the first real one he'd ever had.

*Well,* Aldric said, and underneath the paladin's usual register there was something that might have been warmth, or at least the ghost of where warmth would live if he'd permit it. *Not entirely useless.*


# Chapter 15

The valley smelled different in the early morning. Cooler air pushed the contamination stink below the threshold of constant notice, thinned it enough that Maren could almost forget the ozone-sweet undertow rising from the discolored ground. Almost. The hum from the three dungeon mouths was lower at dawn too, the way a furnace is quieter when the fire banks down but the metal stays hot. He'd slept badly on the thin bedroll Kael had lent him, the entity pressing against the walls of his consciousness in slow, rhythmic surges like breathing, and when he'd given up on sleep and crawled out of the lean-to in the grey pre-dawn, the valley had looked peaceful in the way that only contaminated places can look peaceful — the quest-light pooling in the low spots with a faint shimmer he could barely see, pale as moonlight on standing water.

He was on his knees now, east of the main camp, working through the second containment pass Thessa had drilled into him the afternoon before. The ceramic bowl sat in his lap. His hands moved through the postures — or tried to. His left wrist kept dropping at the wrong angle, and each time it did, the thin thread of contamination he was trying to draw from the nearby residue pool snapped back like a fishing line cut.

Behind him, the camp was waking up. Errin had the fire going — Maren could hear the crackle and the particular scrape of her clay jars as she sorted contamination samples on the wooden table. She did this every morning, Kael had told him. Thirty years of collecting and cataloguing residue, and Errin could identify the age and source of contamination by smell alone. She hummed while she worked, something tuneless and repetitive, the way someone hums who has forgotten they're doing it. She walked with a bad left hip that made her gait lopsided, and her fingers were stained the same dark brown as Thessa's — the permanent record of decades spent handling what other people wouldn't touch.

Paran was sitting on his upturned bucket near the eastern lean-to, rewrapping the cloth strips he used for his own version of the containment pass. His method was different from Thessa's — regional, she'd said with the particular displeasure she reserved for techniques that weren't hers. The strips were grey-brown from repeated contamination exposure and smelled faintly of burnt sugar, a smell that followed Paran around the camp like a second body. He had a wife somewhere south of Silt, and he'd mentioned her once in Maren's hearing — not her name, but the way she folded his strips for him when he came home, matching the edges precisely, a kindness so small and specific that Maren had looked away.

Goss — thin, quiet, ate with his left hand and wrote with his right — was somewhere behind the second lean-to. Maren had barely spoken to him. He knew Goss had arrived at the Crook three days before Maren, and that Goss kept a tally of the contamination pools he'd cleared on a strip of leather tied to his belt, notching it with a knife after each job. Kael had said he was up to two hundred and nine.

*Four hostiles approaching from the northwest ridge.*

Aldric's voice came fast and flat, the subordinate clauses stripped away, the formal syntax compressed under something that wasn't quite urgency — it was worse. It was recognition.

*Full combat loadout. Hero-ranger lead, two melee fighters, one support caster. Moving in formation. The ranger is Caul.*

Maren set the bowl down in the dirt. The containment thread dissolved. "How far?"

*Quarter mile. Descending the ridge. The caster is already generating a targeting overlay — they have visual on the camp.*

The bowl's rim bit into his palm where he'd been gripping it too hard. He'd known Caul would come. Derry had given him the direction, and Caul was a tracker by training, patient the way men are patient when patience is the only thing between them and the grief underneath. But Maren had thought he'd have days. He'd thought the Crook's contamination would slow them, make the approach harder, buy time that could become teaching that could become competence that could become — something. Something other than this.

He stood and his knees cracked from the cold ground. His balance shifted wrong, the entity's weight redistributing inside him like water in a barrel, and he caught himself on the trunk of a dead birch. The bark came apart under his palm, dry and soft as old bread.

He crossed the camp at a run.

"Heroes," he said. He said it to all of them and none of them. "Four. Northwest ridge, quarter mile out. They've got a targeting overlay on the camp."

Errin's humming stopped. The clay jar in her hand went still.

Paran looked up from his cloth strips. His face did something complicated — not panic, exactly, but the rapid calculation of a man who understood immediately what this meant and was already measuring the distance to every exit he could see.

Thessa came out of the largest lean-to. Her grey-streaked hair was flat on one side from sleep and her stained hands were empty, which meant she'd heard him coming and had already set down whatever she'd been holding. She took one look at his face.

"How many?"

"Four. Ranger, two melee, one caster. The ranger's Caul."

Something moved behind Thessa's eyes — not fear, but the sharp recalculation of a woman who'd been managing contamination crises for thirty years and had just had the crisis revised upward. She turned to the camp. "Leave the lean-tos. Take what you can carry. We're moving south toward the third mouth."

"We can't outrun a hero party," Paran said. He'd stood up. The cloth strips hung from one hand, grey-brown and limp. "Thessa, we're not — none of us are combat-classified, we don't have—"

"I'm not suggesting we fight. The contamination pools around the third mouth are deep enough to degrade their targeting. Hero interface loses precision in heavy-contamination zones." She looked at Maren. The look was not warm. "You're the reason they're here."

"Yeah."

"Then you stay and give us time."

It wasn't a question. Thessa turned and began herding the other sin-eaters south, issuing instructions in the clipped imperatives that meant she was working the problem rather than discussing it. Errin gathered her clay jars against her chest, clutching them the way someone clutches something precious, and her bad hip made her gait worse than usual as she moved. Paran stuffed his cloth strips into a pack and fumbled the buckle — once, twice, his hands doing the thing hands do when the body knows something the mind hasn't caught up to. Kael was already moving, her pack on her back. Goss emerged from behind the second lean-to with his notched leather strip swinging from his belt.

*They won't make it.* Vess's voice, colorless and level. *The targeting overlay covers the full valley. The caster will track them.*

*Confirmed. The overlay shows every heat signature in the camp. The sin-eaters are flagged as non-combatant civilians, but—* Aldric paused. The pause was worse than what followed. *Collateral authorization has been granted. The bounty's threat level exceeds the containment threshold.*

"What does that mean in actual words?"

*It means the heroes are allowed to kill anyone between them and you.*

The first arrow hit the support pole of the eastern lean-to with a flat crack, and the canvas slumped sideways like a tent with its spine broken. Maren hadn't heard the bow. He heard the impact, the crack of the wood, and then a second arrow buried itself in the dirt three feet from where Paran was still wrestling with his pack buckle.

"Move!" Maren shouted. "Paran, move, don't—"

The two melee heroes came over the northern lip of the camp at a run. Maren saw them as shapes — one tall, carrying something long, one shorter and fast. His body recognized what his eyes couldn't parse. Aldric's combat training flooding into his muscles, Vess's killing instinct prickling along his nerves, and his feet were moving before he'd decided anything, shifting his weight, dropping his center, putting himself between the descending fighters and the retreating sin-eaters.

The tall one reached him first. Polearm — he felt the displaced air before he saw the sweep, a low strike aimed at his legs, and his body wasn't there. It had sidestepped, half-turned, and his hand was on the shaft, redirecting the arc past his hip. The weapon was heavier than his wrists expected and the shock of it traveled up his arms to his shoulders. He could smell the fighter — leather oil, road sweat, something herbal in the armor's padding.

*She's third-tier melee. Polearm specialization. Weakness: inside range. Get past the—*

He was already past it. His body closed the distance while his mind was still processing Vess's tactical feed, and his elbow connected with the side of the woman's head, just above the ear guard. She staggered. Blood ran from somewhere he couldn't see.

The second fighter came from the right. Dual blades. Young — Maren caught that much in the fragment of attention his body spared him. A face that still had the smooth finish of someone who hadn't been doing this long, mouth open with effort. The blades came in fast and crossing, and Maren's body did things he couldn't track — a block that used his forearm against the flat of one blade, a pivot that took him outside the arc of the other, something with his knee that buckled the young fighter's stance.

He was fighting and he was not fighting. The fragments operated his body while Maren occupied it, present but not in command, experiencing the combat through sensation without data. No damage numbers. No threat calculations. No targeting overlay telling him where to hit and how hard. Just the jar of impact traveling through his bones, the smell of disturbed contamination rising from the ground under their feet, the specific sound of metal on leather and leather on dirt.

Errin screamed.

Not a combat sound. A high, ragged sound — the sound a sixty-year-old woman makes when something hits her body that her body cannot survive. Maren's head turned. His body was still engaged with the dual-blade fighter, feet working patterns that belonged to a dead paladin, and he saw Errin on the ground near the third lean-to. Her clay jars were smashed around her, dark contamination samples soaking into the discolored earth. The arrow was in her back, below the left shoulder blade, and she was trying to crawl. Her bad hip dragged behind her. The humming had stopped.

*Caul's position. Northwest ridge. Sixty-yard range. He's cycling—*

The second arrow took Paran in the throat.

He'd turned back. That was the thing — he'd turned back when Errin screamed, the way you turn back for someone you've worked beside for years in a job nobody thanks you for, and the arrow found the gap between his chin and his chest. The broadhead went in clean and Paran sat down hard, as if someone had removed a stool from under him, and his hands went to his neck. The cloth strips — grey-brown, smelling of burnt sugar, folded by a wife whose name Maren would never know — spilled from his open pack onto the contaminated ground.

*Maren.* Vess, still level, still precise, the tone of someone recording data during a disaster. *Third melee engagement on your left. The polearm fighter is back up.*

His body spun. The polearm came in overhead this time, a chopping strike, and Maren ducked under it and drove his fist into the woman's diaphragm. She doubled and his body did something terrible to her knee that made a sound like a branch snapping and she went down.

The dual-blade fighter was backing off. Maren could hear the young hero's breathing — ragged, panicked, the breathing of someone whose combat-class training hadn't prepared him for an enemy his interface couldn't classify. The targeting overlay showed nothing where Maren stood. A void. A gap in the System's classification where a person should be.

"Goss is down." Kael's voice, from somewhere south. Flat with shock. "He's — there was a System pulse, something from the caster, and he just—"

Three sin-eaters. Three people who'd come to the Crook to do the work that kept the world's contamination from poisoning farmland and children. Dead because Maren had brought his bounty to their door.

*The caster is repositioning to the east. She's trying to establish a new targeting lock on you.*

Maren looked south. Thessa was fifty yards out, moving fast, and Kael was with her. The other sin-eater — Maren hadn't caught a name — was running for the southern ridge. Behind Maren, the polearm fighter was on the ground holding her ruined knee, and the dual-blade boy was retreating upslope toward Caul's position, and the caster was somewhere Maren couldn't see because he couldn't see anything the System generated.

*Maren.* Aldric's voice had changed. Not the compressed urgency of tactical feed — something rawer, closer to the grief that lived underneath his formality. *The caster's next pulse will establish a targeting lock that allows precision strikes at range. If she completes the scan—*

"How long?"

*Thirty seconds. Perhaps less.*

Maren looked at the residue pool. Not the small practice pool where he'd been doing containment passes. The big one — the wide, shallow depression between the first and second dungeon mouths where contamination had collected for years, deep enough and old enough that even Maren could see it clearly: a steady, cold glow spread across the low ground like a frozen lake. The smell coming off it was dense and layered — fresh ozone on top, the sweetness of accumulated quest residue underneath, and below that the sour-metal stink of contamination so old it had begun to change the chemistry of the soil.

*No.* Aldric understood before Maren moved. *That is a nexus-grade contamination pool. You consumed a fraction of a minor pool under supervision and it nearly broke you. If you open yourself to that—*

"If I don't, the caster gets a lock and everyone south of here dies."

*You cannot control—*

"I'm not trying to control it." Maren was already running toward the pool, his boots slipping on the discolored ground, the entity pressing outward inside him as if it could sense what he was about to do and was already reaching for it. "I'm trying to break their targeting."

Vess said nothing. Her silence, as always, was its own assessment.

---

He dropped to his knees at the edge of the pool. The contamination hummed against his shins through his trousers — not the diffuse background hum of the valley but a focused vibration, as if the pool had its own frequency, its own voice. The glow was cold and steady and he could feel it on the skin of his hands, a faint pressure like being watched.

He placed the ceramic bowl at the pool's edge. The bowl that had been a prop for eleven years of fraud, then a beginner's tool for three days of real practice. His hands were steadier than they should have been. The steadiness of someone who had gone past the point where shaking helped.

He used the second opening — the one Thessa had called reckless, the one she'd said was only for emergencies, the one that required the least preparation and the most willingness to let the contamination in. The willingness was the part that mattered. Not technique. Not knowledge. The simple, skin-crawling act of opening himself to the world's accumulated wrongness and letting it pour into the space a person leaves when they stop defending against what's outside.

The contamination came.

Not the thin trickle of his practice sessions. This was a torrent — cold, vast, heavier than anything he'd felt, pouring into him through whatever channel sin-eating opened between the world and the willing. His chest filled with something that was not air and not liquid but had the mass and pressure of deep water. The taste hit the back of his throat — ozone and copper and the ghost of sweetness, like fruit rotting in a cellar. His teeth ached. The roots of them. His eye sockets ached, the bones behind his eyes pressing inward under a weight that had nothing to do with physics.

*The targeting overlay is degrading.* Aldric's voice came from somewhere distant, as if he were speaking from the bottom of the pool Maren was swallowing. *The heroes' interface — it is flickering. The local System architecture is destabilizing around the consumption point.*

More. He pulled more. The contamination wanted to be consumed — that was the thing nobody told you, the thing Thessa's instructions hadn't covered, the thing that made real sin-eating different from every other form of work Maren had ever done. The contamination rushed toward the opening he'd made in himself the way water rushes toward a drain, eager, almost grateful, and the pool's glow dimmed as the residue flowed into him.

Inside his skull, the unclassified entity — the thing born from the collision of a dead paladin and a dead assassin, the nameless presence the System couldn't categorize — responded to the flood of contamination like a fire responds to fuel.

It didn't stir. It surged.

The sensation was unlike anything in Maren's body's vocabulary. His bones vibrated at a frequency that didn't belong to his skeleton, as if his marrow had begun resonating with something that lived in the contamination, something that recognized the entity and answered it. The muscles of his back seized — not a cramp but a recruitment, every fiber contracting at once as if his body were trying to become a fist. The bowl cracked under his knee. He heard it — a sharp, ceramic pop — and felt a shard bite into his kneecap through his trousers, but the pain was a small thing, a detail, a single note inside a sound that was becoming deafening.

*Maren, stop.* Vess, sharp and immediate, the professional flatness fracturing. *The entity is expanding beyond your containment threshold. If it—*

He couldn't stop. The consumption had its own momentum. The pool was feeding into him faster than he could regulate, and the entity was drinking from the same channel, unfolding, growing, pressing against the inside of his consciousness like something trying to stand up inside a room with a ceiling too low to permit it.

*The System sees it.* Aldric's voice had changed entirely. Not formal. Not even frightened. Awed, in the specific way a man is awed by the thing he has always suspected and had spent his career hoping he was wrong about. *The entity is registering on the System interface. It is — Maren, it is not reading as a threat classification. It is reading as a void. A gap in the architecture itself. The System is—*

The pool went dark. Not empty — dark. As if something had consumed the light from inside the contamination rather than from the surface, eating the glow inward the way a body absorbs a bruise. The last of the residue rushed into Maren and the channel snapped shut and he was on his hands and knees in dead soil where a nexus-grade contamination pool had been, retching, and nothing came up because the contamination was not in his stomach. It was everywhere else.

The hum from the dungeon mouths spiked.

Not a gradual increase. A step-change — from the low furnace-bass that Maren had been hearing for two days to something that scraped the inside of his sinuses and set his back teeth ringing. The frequency was wrong. Too high for the dungeon mouths, too structured, as if the hum had been replaced by something deliberately generated.

The air temperature dropped. All at once, five or six degrees in a single breath, the warmth of the early-autumn morning yanked away and replaced by the damp chill of a root cellar. Maren's exhale fogged.

*Local reclassification in progress.* Aldric was reading from the System interface now, his voice stripped to the formal, clipped cadence that meant he was too frightened for anything else — retreating into the language of reports and briefings because the alternative was silence. *The Crook Valley is being redesignated. Threat classification: Critical. Contamination index: Extreme. Authorized response: full containment protocol.*

"What does that—"

*It means the System is treating this valley as a dungeon breach. Every entity within the designated perimeter is reclassified as a potential threat target.* A pause that lasted too long. *Every entity. Including Caul's party.*

---

The quest-light came first.

Not the slow pooling of normal residue — the patient accumulation that Maren had watched settle into low ground over the past two days like luminous groundwater. This erupted. It came up through the soil in threads and runners, bright and cold and hard-edged, crawling across the discolored earth with the purposeful speed of roots finding water. In seconds the valley floor was laced with it — a web of pale light so dense that even Maren, who had never seen the System's interface overlay, could see individual filaments branching and connecting, building a visible architecture across the ground.

The contamination followed. It spiked from the dungeon mouths in a wave — Maren smelled it before he felt it, the ozone-sweet-rot smell going acrid and caustic, the kind of contamination density that Thessa had told him could blister exposed skin in minutes. The air turned thick with it. Breathing went from automatic to effortful, each inhale carrying a metallic weight that sat in his chest like a coin placed on a sleeping man's sternum.

And then the dungeon mouths opened.

Not literally — they were already open, had always been open, the dark holes in the valley's hillsides that breathed contamination and spawned quests. What happened was worse. The nearest mouth, forty yards north, began producing shapes. They emerged from the darkness the way condensation appears on glass — gradual, then suddenly present, then impossible to ignore. Maren couldn't see their System data. He saw their bodies, and their bodies were wrong. Too many joints. Limbs that bent at angles no living skeleton supported, moving with the jerky, over-precise gait of something that had been designed by a system that understood anatomy as a structural diagram rather than a living process. They were the size of large dogs and they glowed faintly from within, the same cold light as the quest-filaments crawling across the ground.

Threat creatures. System-generated. The antibodies of an immune response, spawned to neutralize everything in the reclassified zone.

The first one moved toward Maren. Then stopped. Its malformed head — if it was a head — turned toward the northwest ridge, where heat signatures that the System could actually read were broadcasting their positions to anything with targeting access.

*They are not prioritizing you.* Aldric, quiet. Almost wondering. *The entity — you are not registering as a classified target. The creatures are targeting everything they can read. You are a blank.*

"And Caul's people?"

*They are the loudest signatures in the valley.*

From the ridge, Maren heard Caul's voice. Not the measured field-commander tone of earlier. Louder, rawer, the voice of a man whose professional composure had just been punctured by something he could see on his interface and could not reconcile with everything he had believed. "Fall back! Fall back to the ridge — all units, disengage the target, we have—" A sound that might have been a weapon striking something that wasn't flesh and wasn't stone. "What is this? This isn't standard. These aren't — the System is—"

The polearm woman screamed. Not a combat shout — the raw, involuntary sound of someone being hit by something she hadn't seen coming, something her System-enhanced reflexes should have warned her about but hadn't because the reclassification had rewritten the threat parameters and her own interface was telling her the thing killing her was authorized.

*She's down.* Vess, steady and distant. *The creatures reached her before she cleared the combat zone. The polearm — they are dissolving the polearm. The material is—* A pause. *System-forged equipment is not resistant to System-generated threat creatures. There is a logic to that.*

A logic. Maren got to his feet. His body was wrong — too heavy, the consumed contamination distributed through him in a way that shifted his center of gravity and made each step feel like walking through knee-deep water. The cracked ceramic bowl lay in the dead soil at his feet. The crack ran from rim to base, a clean split through the glaze. He picked it up. His fingers were numb, thick, contamination-clumsy, and the shard that had bitten into his kneecap had left a smear of blood on his trouser leg that he noticed without caring about.

More creatures were emerging. From the nearest mouth, from the second mouth to the south, and from the faint glow of the third mouth farther along the valley where Thessa and Kael had fled. The spawning was accelerating. Ten. Twenty. The valley floor crawled with their wrong-jointed movement, each one trailing faint quest-light like a snail trails slime.

From the ridge, the young dual-blade fighter was making a sound that Maren recognized. He'd made the same sound weeks ago, the first time his body killed a man without his permission — the raw, unprocessed noise a person makes when the framework they trusted to protect them reveals that it never had. The boy was crying. Actual crying, heaving sobs between the clang of his blades against things that shouldn't exist, fighting creatures sent by the very System his training had taught him to serve.

Caul's voice cut through. Not orders this time. A single word, repeated twice, directed at something he could see on his interface that Maren never would.

"No."

And then, louder: "*No.*"

*He's reading the reclassification order.* Aldric said it quietly. The formality was gone, stripped away by what he was watching through Maren's ears and the System data flooding his inherited interface. His voice sounded the way it had when he'd first described the System's architecture — the containment wall, the edges — the voice of a man confronting a truth he'd spent his career suspecting and had organized his entire identity around not confirming. *He can see that the threat creatures were generated in response to the consumption event. He can see that the targeting parameters include his own party. He can see the System weighed the cost of four loyal heroes against the cost of allowing the unclassified entity to persist, and—*

*He can see that the System chose.* Vess finished it. Flat. Final.

Caul stopped fighting Maren's direction. On the ridge, Maren could hear him — the sound of his sword against the creatures' strange bodies, the grunt of effort, the boy's sobbing — fighting the things the System had sent to maintain the integrity of a classification that could not accommodate what Maren was.

Thessa appeared at his side. Maren hadn't heard her approach. She moved through contaminated terrain with the silence of someone who had spent three decades learning where every bad patch and unstable pocket lay, and even in a valley that had just been remade by the System's immune response, her feet found the solid ground. Her face was grey in the harsh quest-light, the deep-set lines around her mouth carved deeper by the shadows. Her stained hands held a clay jar — one of Errin's jars, Maren realized, retrieved from the dead woman's scattered collection.

She uncapped it and poured the contents across the ground between them and the nearest threat creature. The dark, thick residue of years of careful collection spread across the contaminated soil and the creature's jointed legs found no traction on it.
