The thing skidded, stumbled forward onto too many elbows, and Thessa kicked it sideways with the flat of her boot — a practiced motion, not a combat move, the motion of a woman who'd spent decades moving contamination from where it was dangerous to where it was useful — and the creature slid into a puddle of concentrated residue where its body began dissolving, the quest-light inside it guttering like a candle in water.

"I have three jars left." Thessa's voice was steady. The steadiness of someone who had been dealing with impossible work for thirty years and who understood that panic was a luxury the profession didn't offer. "That'll buy us four minutes, maybe five. The third mouth is spawning too, but the contamination there is older — older residue dissolves the creatures faster."

"How many got out?"

"Kael. Maybe Renna — I didn't see." She said the next part without changing her tone, the way she'd say the next contamination site was twelve miles east. "Errin's dead. Paran's dead. Goss is dead. The caster got Goss with a System pulse before the reclassification. The other two were Caul's arrows."

Three sin-eaters. Two hundred and nine notches on Goss's leather strip, lying in the dirt somewhere behind them. Errin's thirty years of sample collection, shattered in clay shards. Paran's wife who folded his cloth strips with matching edges, who did not know yet and would not know for days.

Maren did not say *I'm sorry*. The words existed in his mouth and he swallowed them because they were insufficient and Thessa would know they were insufficient and saying them would cost time they didn't have.

"Can we get past the third mouth?"

"Through the heavy contamination on the south slope. The older residue will cover our heat signatures — the creatures track by System-readable data, and the old contamination generates enough noise to mask us." She was already moving, pouring residue from a second jar as she walked, laying a path of concentrated contamination that the creatures had to route around. "Stay behind me. Step where I step. Don't touch the quest-light filaments — they're mapping the valley's architecture and they'll tag anything biological that contacts them."

Maren followed. His legs felt like they belonged to someone else — heavy, unresponsive, the consumed contamination making his movements thick and approximate. The entity pressed outward inside him with each step, pulsing against the boundaries of his consciousness, and he held it through something that wasn't skill or training but the brute stubbornness of a man who had spent eleven years maintaining a lie and had at least that much practice in keeping the inside from showing on the outside.

Behind them, the valley was unrecognizable. Quest-light covered the ground like luminous frost. Threat creatures moved through it with their wrong-jointed gait, converging on the ridge where Caul and his surviving party member fought. The contamination smell was so thick Maren's eyes streamed. The hum was a physical thing now, a pressure against his eardrums that made his balance worse and turned every step into a negotiation between his body and the ground.

*Caul has disengaged from pursuit.* Vess reported it the way she reported everything — without inflection, without judgment, as data. *He is defending the surviving hero. The caster is incapacitated. He is — he is not retreating. He is holding position on the ridge.*

Maren stopped. He was at the base of the south slope, where the contaminated valley floor began to rise toward the third dungeon mouth. Thessa was five yards ahead, pouring the third jar. Below and behind him, the valley seethed with cold light and the chittering movement of things that shouldn't exist.

He could hear Caul's sword. The steady rhythm of it — a man trained in long-endurance combat, efficient with his strikes, conserving energy. Fighting something his System told him was authorized. Fighting it anyway, because the alternative was letting it kill the crying boy behind him.

*He could leave.* Aldric said it softly. The tone was not admiration or contempt. It was the tone of a man watching someone else reach the conclusion he had reached months ago and finding no satisfaction in the confirmation. *His interface is showing him the evacuation route. The reclassification zone has a perimeter. He could reach it in three minutes if he ran.*

"He won't leave the boy."

*No.* A pause that was almost, but not quite, a formal silence. *He will not.*

Thessa had turned back. She stood on the slope with the empty jar in one stained hand, looking down at Maren with an expression he couldn't read in the grey light. The quest-light filaments cast strange shadows on her angular face, making her look older, or maybe she just looked her age for the first time since he'd met her — fifty-five years of thankless work in a valley full of the System's waste.

"Move," she said. "There's nothing you can do for him."

She was right. Maren knew she was right. He carried no combat class, no hero designation, no System tools that could help a man fighting on a ridge. He carried a cracked ceramic bowl and an entity that the System treated as an infection site and the borrowed muscle memory of two dead people who could not help Caul any more than Maren could.

He climbed. The south slope was steep and the contamination underfoot was old — Thessa had been right about that too. The residue here was decades-deep, structural, woven into the soil itself, and it hummed at a lower, older frequency than the fresh contamination the System was pumping into the valley floor. Maren's boots slipped on the contaminated ground and the smell changed as they climbed — from the sharp ozone-metal of the reclassification to something earthier, deeper, the smell of contamination that had been part of the landscape for so long it had begun to smell like dirt.

Thessa led without looking back. Her long stride ate the slope and her footing never faltered. She knew this terrain the way Maren knew the social architecture of a village — every pocket and pressure point mapped through decades of repetition. Even now, even with the valley remade beneath them, her body navigated the contamination by memory and instinct.

They crested the ridge. Below them on the far side, the slope dropped into scrub and thin forest, the trees stunted by contamination but alive, their leaves beginning to turn with the early autumn. The air was cooler here, cleaner, the System's reclassification perimeter ending somewhere on the ridgeline behind them.

Maren turned back. From the ridge he could see the whole valley — the Crook, or what had been the Crook. The camp was gone, buried under quest-light filaments. The lean-tos were shapes in the glow. The bodies of the dead sin-eaters were somewhere down there, covered in the same cold light that covered everything.

On the northwest ridge, a single figure was still fighting. Maren couldn't see the details — his eyes were wrong, watering from contamination, and the distance was too far. But he could see the movement, the trained economy of a man who knew how to hold a position, and he could see the smaller shape behind the man, crouched, no longer fighting.

*The boy is alive.* Aldric. Quiet. *Caul is holding the ridge. The creatures are — they are slowing. The reclassification appears to be concentrating on the valley floor rather than the high ground. If Caul can maintain his position for another few minutes, the spawning rate should—*

"Should what?"

*Should normalize. The System does not sustain maximum response indefinitely. It — it corrects, and then it settles. Like a fever.*

The entity pulsed inside Maren. Enormous, restless, fed by what he'd consumed, pressing against his ribs and his skull and the spaces between his thoughts. He held it. He didn't know how. He didn't know how long.

Thessa stood beside him on the ridge, looking down at the valley with an expression Maren now recognized. He'd seen it in the mirror, years ago, the first time he'd understood that the villages he'd been pretending to serve were living in contamination he'd left behind. The expression of someone watching the cost of what they are.

"Three of them," he said. "Because of me."

"Yes." Thessa didn't soften it. She didn't qualify, didn't contextualize, didn't offer the kind of structural absolution that would let him slide the weight sideways. She said *yes*, because it was true, and then she said nothing else, because nothing else was required.

Below them, the valley hummed. The quest-light pulsed in slow waves, mapping and remapping the contaminated ground. The threat creatures circled the camp's remains with their wrong-jointed gait, looking for heat signatures to read. Caul's sword caught the early-autumn light on the far ridge — a small, bright movement in a landscape that the System had remade in its own image.

The last thing Maren heard before they descended the far slope was Caul's voice, distant and wrecked and clear, speaking not to his surviving party member or to Maren or to the creatures but to the interface itself, the System that had been the architecture of his grief and his duty and his faith.

"You did this." Quiet enough that Maren shouldn't have been able to hear it, but the valley's acoustics carried sound the way its soil carried contamination — faithfully, indifferently, without regard for who was meant to receive it. "You sent us here and then you — you did this."

The hum swallowed the rest. Maren turned and followed Thessa down the far side of the ridge, into the thin trees where the contamination was old and quiet and the autumn light came through the stunted canopy in long, low shafts that didn't warm anything they touched.


# Chapter 16

They found a sheep pen half a mile from where the valley started to taste wrong.

Not a farmstead — just a low stone wall in a rough circle, open to the sky, with a gate that had rotted off its hinges years ago. The kind of place shepherds used for overnight holding during drives, back when anyone still drove sheep through dungeon-adjacent territory. Nobody had used it in a long time. Grass had come up through the gaps between stones and died there, thin brown stalks that crumbled when Maren brushed against them.

He sat with his back against the wall and tried to catalogue what still worked. His hands were shaking, but that was ordinary — they'd been shaking since the consumption at the Crook, since the quest had been too large and the unclassified entity had surged and the System had turned the valley into a threat zone. Two days of shaking wasn't ideal, but it wasn't new. The headache behind his left eye was new. The way his stomach kept clenching around nothing, as if his body were still trying to reject something that had already been absorbed — that was new too.

The air out here smelled like autumn coming in under contamination: decaying leaves and the acrid sweetness of old quest residue, layered over each other like stains on a floor nobody mopped. The hum was louder than it should have been this far from the dungeon mouths, as if the System's reclassification had pushed the noise boundary outward. Everything within a day's walk of the Crook now sounded like standing next to an active quest.

*The instability is increasing.*

Vess. She hadn't spoken in hours — not since they'd cleared the valley and the running had stopped and there was nothing left to be tactical about. Her voice was the same as it always was: flat, economical, stating things.

Maren didn't answer. He was concentrating on not throwing up again, which required most of his attention.

*Maren. The instability is increasing and it's not going to stop.*

"I know." He said it out loud because talking to the voices inside his skull silently still felt like a thing insane people did, which was funny, because talking to them out loud in a sheep pen probably wasn't the picture of mental health either. "I can feel it."

He could. The unclassified entity — the thing born from the collision of Aldric's paladin classification and Vess's shadow-guild classification, the thing the System couldn't name and therefore wanted to destroy — was doing something in his chest. Not pain, exactly. More like a vibration at a frequency his body couldn't quite resolve, an internal hum that didn't match the external one. It had been getting worse since the overloaded consumption. Before the Crook, it had been background noise. Now it was the loudest thing in any room he walked into, including rooms without walls.

*It's me.*

"What?"

*I'm the variable. Two opposing classifications in a System-blind host produce an entity the System can't categorize. Remove one classification and the entity simplifies. Stops surging. The System can still track it, but it doesn't—* A brief pause, recalibrating. *The interaction between us is what creates the instability. Between what he was and what I was. Take me out and the math changes.*

"The math," Maren said.

*The math.*

He pressed the heel of his hand against his left eye where the headache lived. The stone wall was cold through his shirt. Early autumn had crept in while they'd been running — the nights were longer now and the warmth went out of things faster. He missed the late-summer heat of Fenhold, which was an absurd thing to miss when he could never go back there.

"Aldric," he said.

Nothing.

"Aldric, she's—"

*I heard her.* The paladin's voice was clipped. Formal. The register he retreated to when the framework was failing. *I am considering her assessment.*

*Don't consider it. It's accurate.* Vess didn't wait for silence the way she usually did. She spoke into Aldric's pause as if she'd been listening for exactly that gap. *The entity surged because I'm here. It'll surge again. Worse each time. The next time the System responds, it won't reclassify the area. It'll reclassify him.*

Maren's stomach clenched again. He swallowed and tasted contamination — that ozone-sweet undertone that clung to the back of his throat after consumption, fainter than it had been yesterday but still present, like something spoiled he couldn't spit out.

"So what are you saying?" He knew what she was saying. He wanted her to say it anyway, because some part of him was still the con man who needed to hear the version of events someone else committed to before he committed to his own.

*I need to leave.*

The wind came through the gap where the gate had been. It brought the contamination smell with it — stronger from the east, where the valley lay reclassified and the quest-light had flooded in so thick that even Maren could see the glow from the ridge. The dead grass crumbled against the stones.

"Leave," he said. "As in—"

*Separation. You've learned enough from the old woman to perform a consumption. The reverse should be possible. Expulsion instead of absorption. Push me out instead of pulling me in.*

*That is not—* Aldric stopped. Started again. *The ritual framework Thessa taught him was not designed for expulsion. There is no precedent for—*

*He ate a quest mid-combat two days ago. We're past the precedents.*

*You would become an unhosted fragment.* Aldric's voice had gone quieter. The formal register was holding but something underneath it wasn't. *In the open. Without a consciousness to anchor to. The dissipation rate for an unhosted fragment in contaminated territory is—*

*I know the rate.*

Aldric went silent.

Maren sat with his back against the stone wall in a sheep pen that smelled like autumn and old quest residue and listened to the wind. The hum was steady from the east. His hands shook. The wrong-frequency vibration in his chest ground against his ribs, and he thought about Vess's voice the first time he'd heard it — furious, professional, livid about the paladin and the incompetent host and the fact of being dead — and he thought about the way she'd talked him through the physical recovery after his first real consumption at the creek bed, the unexpected steadiness of it, how she'd narrated his breathing the way she must have narrated approach routes in the life she'd had before. In, hold, out. Professional. Something close to kind. Alive, even dead.

"How long?" he asked. "After. How long before you—"

*I don't know.* The first time she'd admitted not knowing something since he'd carried her, and it wasn't really a correction — it was a gap in her information, a hole where the answer should have been, and the specific distress of it was quiet and precise. *Theory says hours. Maybe longer in contaminated air — fragments persist where the residue is dense. But the theory also says a System-blind host can't carry fragments at all, so.*

So the theory was shit. Maren almost laughed but the headache pressed down harder and the laugh died somewhere in his sinuses.

"There's got to be—"

*There isn't.* She said it once. No repetition. No emphasis. The words occupied their space and that was all. *Two fragments from opposed classifications are destroying your body. One fragment from a single classification is stable. The choice is straightforward.*

"For you, maybe."

*For everyone. That's the point.*

Maren closed his eyes. The darkness behind his eyelids was the same darkness he'd seen his whole life when the System's interface should have been there — a comfortable nothing, warm and unwritten. Twenty-eight years of that nothing. Twenty-three days of it being crowded with voices, and the thought of one of those voices going quiet made the nothing feel different. Larger. Colder.

"What about Blackhall?" he said.

The silence that followed was different from Aldric's silences. Aldric's silences were retreats — he went somewhere inside himself and closed the door. Vess's silence was presence without speech. She was there, fully, and the words had stopped.

*What about him.*

"Your contract. The massacre. The thing you were — the reason you were in that quest in the first place. You were hired to—"

*I know what I was hired to do.*

"And you never finished it. Torren Blackhall is still alive. Still—"

*I know.*

Maren opened his eyes. The sheep pen. The dead grass. The glow from the east where the reclassified valley bled quest-light into the sky. His hands, shaking.

"You've been carrying that since before you died. You told me his schedule. Tuesdays the lower market. Thursdays the holding pens. Fridays he meets the reeve and drinks too much and walks home through the garden alone, and you know the garden, and you know where the hedgerow blocks the sight lines, and now you're just going to—"

*Yes.*

The word sat in the air between them. Inside him, technically — all of this was inside him, the voices and the argument and the silence — but it occupied space the way a physical thing did.

"Vess."

*I thought it would matter more.* Her voice was the same — flat, professional. But the sentence was longer than anything she'd said about herself before, and that alone was a fracture in the surface. *The contract. Blackhall. Justice, if that's what it was. I thought when the time came it would be the thing I couldn't let go of. The hook that kept me here.*

*And it isn't?*

Aldric. Quiet. Unadorned. None of the formality, none of the classification language. Just a question from someone who needed the answer.

*No.* Vess. Simply. *It matters. He killed those people and used the System's own infrastructure to hide it and he's still alive and it matters. But it doesn't matter more than this.*

She didn't specify what "this" was. She didn't need to. Maren could feel the unclassified entity shuddering against his ribs, the wrong-frequency hum that was getting worse with every hour, and he understood that "this" was him staying alive long enough to do whatever came next. "This" was the work. "This" was what remained when you let the last piece of your old self go.

"When?" he asked.

*Now. Before I find a reason to stay.*

---

Maren took the bowl out of his pack.

It was ceramic, glazed once and badly — the color somewhere between mud and dried blood, an indeterminate brown that had never been attractive. One side was chipped from eleven years of careless packing and road travel; the chip exposed the pale clay underneath, a half-moon of lighter color near the rim. He'd bought it from a woman at the Fenhold market when he was seventeen and needed a prop for his first fake ritual. Three coppers. The woman had tried to sell him a nicer one for five, and he'd said no, because he was seventeen and two coppers was dinner and the dead didn't care about pottery.

He turned it in his hands. The glaze was worn smooth in the places where his fingers always sat — two spots on either side where he'd held it during every performance for eleven years. He could feel the depressions his thumbs had made, so shallow they were invisible, worn there by nothing more than repetition and time and the hands of a man who had been lying about everything else but who had always held the bowl the same way.

It had never done anything. Not once. Every ritual where he'd placed it before a body and murmured words he'd invented and watched the family's faces for cues about when to nod, when to be solemn, when to close his eyes as if something were happening — the bowl had sat there being ceramic. Three coppers' worth of clay doing the work of nothing.

"I don't know if this'll work," he said.

*It'll work.* Vess.

*You cannot know that.* Aldric.

*He consumed a quest the size of a village two days ago with nothing but bad training and stubbornness. He can manage a single fragment.*

*That is not the same as certainty.*

*Nothing is. Start.*

Maren set the bowl on the ground between his knees. The grass was cold and damp through his trousers. The sheep pen's stone wall circled them — waist-high, roofless, open to the sky where the early autumn stars were coming out in patches between thin clouds. The hum from the east was steady. Quest-light reflected off the cloud cover in a pale wash that Maren could see only as ambient brightening, a sky that refused to go fully dark.

He put his hands on either side of the bowl, thumbs settling into the depressions, and tried to remember what Thessa had taught him.

The proper consumption sequence was: preparation, opening, acceptance, dissolution. He'd managed it twice now — badly at the creek bed, barely at the Crook. Both times the process had felt like pulling his own skin back, an exposure of something underneath himself that had no business being exposed. Like finding a door in a wall you'd thought was solid and being told to stand in the doorway while something came through.

For this he needed the opposite. Not a door opening inward. A door opening out.

He closed his eyes. The bowl was cold under his hands. The grass was cold under his knees. The contamination smell was sharp in the air — that ozone-sweet decay, stronger toward the valley. He could hear his own breathing, and underneath it, the voices: Aldric's formal silence, Vess's professional waiting.

"Vess."

*I'm here.*

"I know." His thumbs pressed deeper into the worn depressions on the bowl, and for a moment the clay felt thin enough to crack. "I just — yeah. I know."

He began.

The opening was the same whether you were consuming or expelling — Thessa had been clear about that much. The sin-eater's work started with making yourself available. Permeable. Maren had spent twenty-eight years being impermeable to the System and eleven years performing a pantomime of permeability, and the real thing was still like learning a language his body didn't want to speak. He breathed and tried to mean it. Not perform it — mean it. The difference between those two things had been the entire distance of his life, and it was narrowing, and that should have been a comfort but mostly it just hurt.

Something shifted. The same something that had shifted at the creek bed, at the Crook. A loosening in his chest, as if a fist he'd been making since birth had opened one finger at a time. The unclassified entity responded — surging toward the opening, confused and eager, the way it always did when he made himself available to the work.

*Careful.* Aldric. Not a command. Something closer to worry, though he'd never use that word.

Maren pushed.

The consumption was about pulling — drawing the contamination in, dissolving it, accepting the weight. The expulsion was about the opposite, and the opposite turned out to be harder, because you had to push something out of yourself that had made itself part of you. Vess's presence in his consciousness had been specific and constant for weeks — her tactical awareness at the edges of his perception, the way she noticed exits and approach angles and the exact distance between himself and any potential threat. She'd been the cold voice that assessed while Aldric moralized, the one who counted moves instead of sins. She was in his reflexes now, in the way he'd started checking sight lines when he entered a room, in the automatic inventory of objects within reach that could be weapons.

He pushed, and she began to move, and it hurt.

Not the dramatic pain of the consumption — no convulsions, no retching. A subtler thing. Like removing a splinter that had been in his hand so long the skin had grown around it. His body had accommodated her the way a tree accommodates a fence post, growing against and around until the foreign object was structural, and now he was pulling it out and the gaps it left were shaped exactly like what was missing.

The bowl warmed under his hands.

That was new. In eleven years of fake rituals, the bowl had never been anything but room temperature or cold — whatever the air was, the bowl was. Now it heated from the inside, not hot, not painful, but warm the way a thing being used was warm. The glaze under his thumbs changed from dead ceramic to something that conducted, that accepted, that did what it was made to do even though it had never been made for anything. Three coppers. A potter who couldn't center a handle. And now, for the first time in its dull, chipped, unremarkable existence, the bowl was working.

*There's—* Vess started, and for the first time since Maren had known her, she didn't finish. A gap. A fracture in the controlled voice, as if she'd hit one of her own memory gaps mid-sentence and the sentence had fallen into it.

*I can feel it,* she said, and this sentence was complete but it cost her something. *The bowl. It's doing the real thing.*

The fake tool. The market bowl. The prop. Something was passing through it — through Maren, out of him, into the ceramic — and the bowl was receiving it with the patience of an object that had waited eleven years to be useful, though Maren knew that was his own sentimentality talking and the bowl was just clay and heat and function and didn't care.

He kept his hands on it and pushed, and Vess moved, and the gaps she left filled with quiet.

*Maren.*

"Yeah."

*The contract is released. If you ever get close enough—* She stopped. *No. It's released. That's all.*

He held the bowl and said nothing, because there was nothing to say to a woman letting go of the last thing that had kept her shape, and anything he tried would be wrong.

*The old woman's teaching will hold. Aldric's theory is sound, even when it's pompous.* Quieter now. Not softer — Vess didn't do soft. But the volume had dropped, as if the space between them was widening even before the separation was complete. *You're bad at this. But you're real at it. That's the part that counts.*

He pushed. The bowl heated. The thing in his chest — the unclassified entity, the collision, the impossible classification — shuddered and simplified. He could feel it happening: not a loss of power but a loss of complexity. Like a chord resolving to a single note. Still a sound. Just less.

*My sister's name,* Vess said. *There should be a name here. There always should have been a name here.*

And then she left.

It was exactly like a sound leaving a room. Not a door closing, not a dramatic exit — just the moment when you realized the noise that had been there a second ago wasn't there anymore. Had it stopped just now? Or a moment ago, and you were only noticing? The boundary between presence and absence was blurred, and Maren sat with his hands on the warm bowl and his knees in the cold grass and waited for the difference to become legible.

The difference was enormous.

Her tactical awareness went first — the constant peripheral assessment of angles and distances and threats that had become so automatic he'd stopped noticing it was hers. Then the coldness, the professional temperature of competence that she'd maintained even inside someone else's skull. Then the small things: the way she'd noticed what people ate, how rooms smelled, the domestic inventory she kept alongside the lethal one. Gone. All of it. Gone the way a sound left a room, which was to say: completely, suddenly, and with nothing remaining except the quality of the silence it left behind.

The bowl cooled under his hands. Whatever had passed through it — whatever Vess had been, whatever the ritual had carried out of him and into the ceramic and then into the contaminated air — was already dissipating. The bowl was ceramic again. Three coppers' worth. But the wear on the glaze looked different to his fingers, or maybe it didn't — maybe it was the same bowl it had always been, and the difference was in the hands that held it.

Maren sat in the sheep pen with the empty bowl between his knees and listened to the quiet inside his own skull.

It was so quiet.

For weeks his head had been a room with three people in it — himself, the paladin, and the assassin. There had always been someone talking, someone objecting, someone assessing a threat or cataloguing a moral failure or describing the System interface in tones of sarcasm or professionalism or barely contained fear. The noise had been maddening and constant and alive, and now a third of it was gone, and the space it left was the emptiest thing he'd ever experienced — emptier than his twenty-eight years of System-blindness, because at least the blindness had been a familiar emptiness. This one had a shape. The shape was hers.

"Aldric."

Nothing.

"Aldric."

Nothing. Not the hostile nothing of his early silences, when he'd withheld speech as punishment for Maren's moral failures. Not the frightened nothing of his System-observations, when he'd seen something in the architecture he couldn't describe. A different nothing. A nothing that sat where an argument should have been.

Maren picked up the bowl. The chip near the rim. The indeterminate brown. The thumb-depressions worn smooth by eleven years of lies and three weeks of something else. He put it back in his pack and the weight of it against his spine through the canvas was so familiar it hurt, and he pulled his knees up to his chest and wrapped his arms around them.

The stars came out over the contaminated valley. The quest-light painted the clouds from below in the pale wash that was the closest thing Maren ever saw to what everyone else navigated by. The hum from the east was steady. The grass rustled against the stones. Somewhere — close, closer than it should have been — a night bird called once and didn't call again.

Inside his skull, the paladin who had argued with the assassin every day for three weeks, who had talked over her without apology, who had corrected her moral positioning and questioned her tactical assessments and interrupted her field reports and been interrupted in turn with a patience more unsettling than any interruption — that paladin sat in the silence where her voice had been.

The silence lasted a long time.

When Aldric finally spoke, his voice was different. Not warmer. Not kinder. Different the way a room was different after you removed a piece of furniture that had always annoyed you — the same walls, the same dimensions, but the space was rearranged and you kept looking at the place where the thing used to be and discovering that annoyance was a kind of company.

*The entity has stabilized.* Formal. Clipped. A report delivered to an order that no longer existed. *The opposition is resolved. Single classification within a System-blind host. Stable, as far as I can determine.*

"Good," Maren said. His voice sounded wrong in the quiet. Too loud. Too present.

*Consumption capability should remain largely intact. Thessa's training addressed the foundational techniques. What you have lost is...* He stopped. *Tactical assessment. Threat analysis. Her specific observations about—*

He stopped again. Maren waited. The night bird didn't call.

*She was wrong frequently.* Aldric said it with the cadence of a man reaching for a familiar position and finding that it no longer fit his hand. *About matters of moral consequence. Her classification training prioritized expedience over—*

He stopped a third time.

*She was not wrong about this.*

Maren put his chin on his knees. The stone wall pressed cold against his back. His hands had stopped shaking at some point during the ritual — the first time in two days — and the stillness of his own fingers against his shins was strange, as if the tremor had been keeping time and now the music had stopped. The headache behind his left eye was gone too. The wrong-frequency vibration in his chest had resolved into something simpler, steadier — still the unclassified entity, still the thing the System couldn't name, but quieter now. One note instead of a chord.

He missed the chord.

"She didn't say goodbye," he said. "Not exactly. She said the contract was released and she said something about her sister and then she was just — not here."

*She said things once.* Aldric. Quiet. *You knew this about her.*

"Yeah."

*Then you know that was the goodbye.*

Maren sat in the sheep pen. The bowl in his pack pressed against his spine through the canvas. The air smelled like autumn and contamination and the fading trace of something that might have been a person, or might have been nothing more than quest residue thinning in the night wind. He couldn't tell the difference. That was the worst part, or maybe the most fitting part — that Vess, who had always been precise about what she could and couldn't perceive, would leave behind an absence that smelled like everything else that was lost and dissipating in this world, and that he was too blind to sort her from the rest of it.

The skull was quieter.

Maren stayed in the sheep pen until the stars moved. Aldric didn't speak again. The hum from the east went on, the System doing its work, the wall maintained, the containment holding against whatever lived behind it. Somewhere in the dark, in the contaminated air between the dungeon mouths, what was left of Vess was thinning into nothing — becoming part of the residue, the background, the particular smell of a world that consumed its own.

In the morning he would get up and keep walking toward the Woundmouth. He would carry the bowl and the single voice and the simplified thing in his chest, and he would be less than he had been, and the world would be exactly as indifferent to that as it was to everything else.

But that was the morning. For now there was the night, and the quiet, and the place where a sound had been.


# Chapter 17

Three days past the wreckage of the Crook, the roads gave out entirely.

Not all at once. The deterioration had been gradual — ruts softening into packed earth, packed earth dissolving into root-cracked mud, until what remained was just a suggestion of direction between rockfall and scrub. The autumn air had an edge to it now, mornings cold enough that Maren's breath fogged, though by midday the sun still warmed the stone. Quest-light didn't pool here the way it had near Silt or Harrow. It seeped. Thin runnels of pale luminescence bled through cracks in the rock, trickling downhill like water after rain, except it moved too slowly and sometimes in the wrong direction — uphill, sideways along a cliff face, following paths that had nothing to do with gravity.

The hum was constant now. Not the background drone he'd grown up filtering — this was pressure behind his teeth, a vibration in the bones of his face that made his sinuses ache. It had started as a nuisance two days ago and thickened until he couldn't ignore it, until he was walking through it the way a person walks through heavy wind.

*Left at the split rock. Then follow the bleeding.*

Aldric's guidance had simplified. In the early days of the journey, his directions came formatted like military dispatches — compass bearing, estimated distance, landmark descriptions that assumed Maren cared about northeast versus north-northeast. Now the paladin pointed and named. Left. Down. That gap. The change was partly economy, partly something else. Aldric was speaking less, and when he spoke, his voice carried a quality Maren couldn't name. Not quite fear. Closer to the sound a man makes when he's standing on a high ledge and being very careful about where he looks.

"You alright in there?" Maren said, out loud. There was no one to hear him. The last person he'd seen was three days gone — a contamination surveyor heading the other direction, walking fast, who hadn't even nodded.

*I am observing.*

"That's not what I asked."

Silence for a dozen steps. The rock path narrowed between two shoulders of dark stone, and Maren's pack scraped the wall on his left. Quest-light ran in a slow stripe at ankle height, warm against his boot when he grazed it.

*The interface here is — I can see the structure more clearly. The architecture. It's becoming more legible the closer we get, but the legibility is not—* A pause. Aldric did not pause. Or he hadn't, for the whole length of their shared existence, through running commentary and moral objection and the kind of constant narration that made Maren want to scream on good days. When the paladin stopped mid-sentence, something had changed the shape of his thinking. *The edges are physical here. I do not mean merely visible. I mean physical. The containment has thickness.*

"So when you say physical—"

*The way a load-bearing wall is physical. You cannot see what it holds unless it fails, but the wall itself has mass, joins, stress points. And this place—* Another pause. *The wall is thin here. I have never seen it this thin. There are — fractures is not the right word. Places where the containment material has been stressed beyond its design tolerance and the joining compound has degraded.*

"You're describing a building."

*I am describing what I see. The metaphor is imprecise but not inappropriate. The System is constructed. It was built. Whatever built it intended it to hold.*

Harrow had looked like infrastructure. Maren remembered that now — the quest boards and gear shops and scorch marks layered on every road, the whole town built around a System that organized everything. He'd thought of it as bureaucracy at the time, the filing system of heroism, but Aldric's description from weeks ago had changed that. Not a filing system. A wall. And all the infrastructure — the classes, the levels, the quests, the bounties — was the wall's public face, the side people could see and navigate by, while the actual structure went deep and held something on the other side.

He stepped over a narrow channel where quest-light ran like a creek, pale and faintly warm against his shin through his trouser leg. He could see it — he'd always been able to see quest-light, the raw glow without the data overlay everyone else perceived. But here the glow was different. Sick-looking. The light stuttered and pulsed with no discernible rhythm, like a heartbeat that couldn't find its pace.

The skull was quiet around Aldric's voice. Maren noticed the silence the way he noticed a missing tooth — the tongue kept going to the gap. Vess would have had something to say about this terrain. An assessment. *Defensible here. Kill zone there. If anyone's waiting, you'd want elevation.* The flat, practical narration of a woman who saw the world as a series of tactical problems, and who, underneath the tactical language, noticed the color of the light and the smell of the rock and the exact weight of the autumn air but would never have said so. He missed her. Not the way he missed Derry, which was the specific ache of losing a person who knew him from before. This was the miss of losing a perspective. The world was less precisely observed with only two of them in here.

The terrain tilted downward. The path — the idea of a path — led between two rock faces that leaned toward each other, not quite meeting, leaving a stripe of grey autumn sky above. Quest-light bled from both walls in slow runs that pooled at the bottom of the gap and drained somewhere ahead, downhill, toward whatever the Woundmouth actually was.

Maren's bowl clinked against his hip where he'd tied it. The ceramic was cold through his shirt. He'd been carrying it outside his pack since the separation — since the bowl had done real work for the first time, dissolving the boundary between Vess and himself and sending her into the formless quiet of dissipation. Every few minutes the weight of it bumped against him and the bump was a reminder that he carried tools now, not props. The same cheap market-stall bowl. Different man holding it.

*Stop.*

Maren stopped. His boots were ankle-deep in pooled quest-light, warm and thick, and the hum jumped — a physical pressure against his eardrums, like altitude change or the moment before a thunderclap.

*Look down.*

He looked down, though looking meant nothing beyond the raw glow and the dark stained rock. The quest-light ran ahead of him into a depression in the ground that widened and deepened, and he felt it before he understood what he was seeing. The ground opened. Not a cave mouth or a cliff edge — a wound. The rock itself looked torn, the edges ragged and discolored, quest-light pouring from the opening like blood from a cut that had been left too long. The contamination smell was overwhelming: not just the familiar ozone-sweet but something underneath, something that smelled the way old wood smells when you split it and find the rot has gone all the way through.

The Woundmouth.

It was smaller than he'd imagined. He'd expected spectacle — a cavern, a vast opening, the kind of thing that earned its name through drama. Instead it was maybe eight feet across, an irregular tear in the stone with quest-light hemorrhaging from every edge, pooling in the depression around it in a warm, pulsing lake that reached Maren's calves. The hum came from it. Not from around it — from the opening itself, a vibration that resonated in his ribs and made his vision swim at the edges.

*This is where the architecture is thinnest. This is where the wall—* Aldric stopped. Started again with the careful precision of a man choosing each word because the wrong one might make the situation real. *The containment here has failed to the degree that what it holds is nearly visible to me through the interface. I can see — I am seeing things that should not be visible from this side.*

"Describe them."

*No.*

"Aldric."

*Not yet. You will see for yourself, I think. When you consume. If—* He caught the word. Corrected it. *When you consume. The ritual. You need to set up.*

The *if* had been there, though, sitting in the paladin's voice, and the correction was its own kind of honesty.

---

Maren knelt at the edge of the Woundmouth.

The quest-light was warm against his legs, warmer than any pool he'd worked before — body temperature, or close to it, as though the light itself were alive and fevered. He untied his bowl from his hip. The ceramic was white and cracked, glazed once but worn to matte by eleven years of handling, and when he set it at the lip of the wound the quest-light ran into it immediately, filled it, spilled over the rim in a slow overflow. The bowl was too small for what was being offered.

He had learned the real sequence. Thessa had taught him at the Crook, grudgingly, with corrections that landed like slaps — the proper order of gestures, the breathing, the way to open himself to contamination rather than performing openness. The difference between the fake version and the real one was the difference between miming the act of eating and actually putting food in your mouth. One was theatre. The other was consumption: taking the thing inside yourself, letting it change you, doing the work of dissolution that the System could not do on its own.

His hands were steady. That surprised him. They hadn't been steady for the practice consumption at the creek, hadn't been steady at the Crook when Thessa stood over him correcting his form in front of the other sin-eaters, hadn't been steady during the combat consumption when the valley was collapsing around them. But now, kneeling at the edge of something that could kill him, they were calm. He didn't trust it. Calm before a consumption usually meant his body hadn't figured out how bad it was going to get yet.

*Maren.*

"Yeah."

*I want you to know that I chose correctly.*

"That's a hell of a thing to say right before I—"

*I chose to reach for you because you were the only kind of host who could do this. Not because you were convenient or because you were nearby. Because you were necessary. I want you to know that while you can still hear me say it with composure.* A beat. *I may not have composure shortly.*

"You're really selling the optimism here."

*Begin.*

He began.

The first gesture was the opening — hands flat against the stone beside the wound, palms down, letting the contamination read his skin. Thessa called it the greeting: you showed the contamination what you were, and it showed you what it was, and from that mutual recognition the work could start. In the fake version, he'd always mimed this part with his eyes closed and a solemn expression, holding the posture long enough for the family to feel the ritual's weight. In the real version, his eyes were open and there was nothing solemn about it. The contamination crawled up through his palms like heat from a stove, except it kept going — into his wrists, up his forearms, a tingling pressure that pushed into his chest and settled behind his sternum. The ozone smell filled his mouth and his sinuses.

The second gesture was the opening of the channel. Not his physical mouth — the interior one, the passage Thessa described as the space between what you are and what the world is full of. He'd struggled with this at the creek, struggling to distinguish metaphor from instruction, until Vess had said, bluntly, *Stop trying to understand it. Just do what your body already knows how to do.* And she'd been right. His body knew. Eleven years of faking had built the postures into his muscles even though the intent behind them had been hollow, and when he let the postures carry him without resistance, the channel opened like a door swinging on hinges that had been waiting.

The contamination poured in.

Not the thin trickle of a residue pool. Not the manageable stream of a quest abandoned by a roadside. This was the accumulated contamination of something that had been bleeding since before anyone alive could remember — structural, woven into the rock and the light and the hum, as old as the wound itself. It hit him like cold water from a height. His lungs seized. His vision went white.

And then, for the first time in twenty-eight years of blindness, he saw the System.

Not as Aldric described it. Not mediated through the paladin's narration, filtered through his vocabulary and his moral framework and his careful formal cadence. Maren saw it himself. His own eyes. The interface that every other person in the world had been born into, the overlay that sat on reality the way ink sits on paper, had appeared.

There was light everywhere. It was layered over reality like a second skin — every rock annotated, every crack in the stone carrying data, classifications and numbers and categories scrolling in columns he couldn't read because he'd never learned the visual grammar. The taxonomy of everything. Every insect near the Woundmouth had a classification hovering beside it. The rock itself was labeled. The quest-light pooling around his knees carried strings of data he could see but not parse, like looking at text in a language you don't speak. The world, organized. The world, named.

It was beautiful. He hadn't expected that. The light was intricate and precise, a vast careful system of annotation that turned the raw mess of reality into something navigable, something that could be understood at a glance by anyone who'd grown up reading it. He understood, suddenly, viscerally, why Aldric had sounded like a man reading a grocery list at a state funeral — because that's what the interface was. A list. An enormously detailed, constantly updating list of what everything was, what it could do, and how dangerous it was. People lived inside this list. They were born into it and they died inside it and they never thought to wonder what it was attached to.

He saw his own classification: UNCLASSIFIED ENTITY. The words hung in the air in front of him, bordered in pulsing red, wrong against the organized architecture of everything around them. A warning to everything that could read it. And below it, the bounty figure — a number so large it looked like a printing error.

*Maren. Don't stop. Keep going. You have to go past it.*

Aldric's voice was distant, not because the paladin had moved but because Maren was moving — past the interface, through the interface, into the machinery behind the labels. The light thinned. The classifications stretched, distorted, the clean annotations fraying at their edges as if the writing had run out of surface to adhere to. The data degraded. He was going somewhere the System hadn't been designed to let anyone go, and the System's architecture was responding by failing in his presence the way it always had — not violently, not with alarm, but with the quiet structural inability to account for what he was.

Behind the interface was the wall.

He could feel it before he could see it — a pressure, a density, a thing with mass and age that his body registered the way his body registered gravity. The wall was not a metaphor. Aldric had described it in architectural terms weeks ago, sitting in camp with the smoke going sideways, and Maren had understood it intellectually then. He understood it in his bones now. The containment was constructed from something that wasn't rock or light or data but was all three compressed into a single barrier, and it extended in every direction — up, down, sideways, deeper than his spatial perception could follow. It had been built with enormous care and maintained through constant repair, patched and repatched in layers he could feel the way you feel the layers of paint on an old wall when you run your hand over it.

And it was failing.

Not dramatically. Not all at once. The way a dam fails: one crack at a time, the cracks slowly connecting, the seepage getting worse by degrees that any single observer would call manageable but that the structure itself knew was terminal. The Woundmouth was one crack. The worst one, maybe, or just the one he'd reached first, but through it he could feel the others — dozens, scattered across the containment's surface, hairline fractures in a structure that was older than any recorded history.

He was at the crack. From this side, the Woundmouth was a fissure in the wall that went deeper than the physical wound in the rock above, and through the fissure he could feel what was on the other side.

It was not evil. The word arrived and he rejected it instantly because it was wrong. Not malevolent. Not hungry, not angry, not anything that came with moral weight or narrative shape. What was on the other side of the wall was wild. Unclassified — not the way Maren was unclassified, which was a bureaucratic failure, a missing label — but unclassified in the way the world had been before the classification existed. Before the taxonomy. Before the System. Before the labels and the levels and the neat organizational architecture that made the world legible and safe. What was behind the wall was the world that had preceded all of that, and it was vast and complicated and indifferent to categories and it pressed against the barrier with the patient weight of an ocean against a seawall. Not trying to break through. Just being what it was, and what it was happened to be larger than what held it.

The System was a dam. The interface was the dam's public face. And the dam was leaking.

Sin-eaters were supposed to fix the leaks. That was the job — the real one, the one underneath the ritual and the bowl and the clearing of contamination from farmland. Contamination was the wall's material bleeding from its own wounds, and when sin-eaters consumed it properly, they cauterized the damage. Resealed the cracks. Patched the wall from the inside. The work Thessa had done for decades — the thankless, essential, underpaid labor of clearing residue from villages and farmland — was maintenance. She was a structural engineer who thought she was a janitor, and even thinking she was a janitor, she'd done the work without complaint.

And Maren could do what she couldn't. Because the System couldn't write on him, he existed in the gap between the wall and what it held. He could touch both sides. A System-sighted sin-eater who tried this would be written into the wall's own architecture — absorbed, classified, made part of the structure rather than a worker on it. Maren's blindness wasn't immunity from the interface. It was immunity from the wall. He was the only kind of person who could reach the structural damage without becoming part of it.

He was both the crack — because every place the System's writing failed was a place the wall thinned — and the sealant.

The understanding was clear and cold and it arrived with a cost, because understanding was not free here. The consumption had been running while he processed, and his body had continued doing the work of dissolution without his conscious direction, and the work was too much. The contamination from the Woundmouth was exponentially denser than anything he'd consumed before. It poured through the channel he'd opened and his capacity was not sufficient. He'd been kneeling in the quest-light for — how long? Minutes? Longer? His sense of time had dissolved along with the contamination.

His hands had locked against the stone, fingers white, the tendons standing in rigid cords. The ceramic bowl beside him was overflowing, quest-light running over his knees and soaking his trousers to the skin. The hum had become a sound he could feel in his teeth, in his jaw joints, in the plates of his skull. His heartbeat was wrong — too fast, then too slow, then skipping beats entirely, his chest doing something that didn't feel sustainable. His breath came in hitches. Something in his throat was closing, not from panic but from the contamination — the muscles of his esophagus clenching against the volume of what was filling him.

*Maren. Stay with me. Maren. Stay.*

He couldn't answer. His mouth was full of the taste of contamination — ozone and copper and something older, something that tasted the way deep groundwater tastes when it's been in the rock for centuries, mineral and cold and impossibly heavy. His vision strobed between the raw world and the interface overlay, flipping back and forth like a lamp with a failing filament. He could see the data, the labels, the classifications — and then he couldn't, and then he could, and his brain was fighting both because it had spent twenty-eight years learning to see without the overlay and now the overlay was being forced through channels that had never carried it.

*Your heart is arrhythmic. I don't — I am not a healer, I don't know the—* Aldric's voice cracked. Not the voice of a paladin, not the voice of a man giving reports. The voice of someone watching a person die and having no hands to stop it. *Keep breathing. Just keep breathing. I can — I can try to — I don't know what I can do. But I am here and I am not leaving and you will keep breathing.*

The paladin's formality was gone. Not strategically dropped but torn off by the situation, the way weather tears a sign from a post. No subordinate clauses. No careful attribution. No reports filed to an order that no longer existed. Just a dead man refusing to let another man die, holding on through nothing but the sheer stubbornness of someone who had already lost everything he valued and would not lose this too.

Maren could feel Aldric's presence — not as a voice but as a pressure, a weight of attention bearing down on him from inside his own skull. The paladin was doing something. Not prayer. Aldric had stopped praying weeks ago, quietly, without announcement, the way a man stops writing letters to an address that never replies. What Aldric was doing was simpler and harder than prayer: he was paying attention. He was watching Maren's heartbeat and his breathing and his consciousness with the focus of a man who believed that witnessing was itself an act that could hold a thing together.

Maren's left hand spasmed off the stone. The contamination in his chest surged. His vision went white again and then dark and then settled into something that was neither — a grey, pressurized awareness of his own body failing, his consciousness flickering at the edges like a candle in wind.

*You are not done. You are not finished. Stay.*

---

Maren couldn't feel his legs.

He was on his side — when had he fallen? — lying in the pooled quest-light, the warm sick glow soaking through his clothes, his hair, the side of his face pressed against stone that smelled like ozone and age. The bowl had tipped. It lay near his right hand, on its side, empty, the quest-light that had filled it absorbed or drained back toward the wound. The hum was quieter. Not gone — reduced, as if the wound had exhaled and the pressure behind it had eased, slightly, enough to notice.

*You sealed it. Some of it. Not all — the fracture is too deep for one pass. But the edges are narrower. You cauterized the worst of the seepage. I can see the wall reforming along the—*

"Shut up." His voice was a croak. His throat was raw, contamination-burned, and every word scraped going out. "Give me a minute."

*You don't—*

"Give me a fucking minute, Aldric."

The paladin went quiet. Not the frightened silence of earlier, not the observant silence of the approach. The obedient silence of someone who recognized that the request was not negotiable.

Maren lay there. His whole body shook with a fine tremor that made his teeth click against each other. He could taste blood — copper and salt — and when he spat sideways onto the stone, it was pink. He'd bitten the inside of his cheek at some point during the consumption, or maybe the contamination had burned something on its way through. His chest ached with a deep, bruised soreness, the feeling of muscles that had been clenched for too long without oxygen. His heartbeat was present again — not regular, not calm, but present, one after another, which was the minimum standard.

He watched the quest-light seep along the ground near his face. It moved differently now — slower, less fevered, the stuttering pulse smoothed into something closer to a steady flow. The Woundmouth was still there. You didn't close a wound this old with one consumption. But the hemorrhaging had slowed. The edges of the tear looked less ragged, the quest-light bleeding from them thinner, weaker.

He'd sealed a crack. Part of one. With his face in the dirt and blood in his mouth and his heart stuttering like a broken clock.

*The interface — your ability to perceive it — has faded. I expected that. The consumption provided temporary access, but the access was a byproduct of proximity to the wall's interior, not a permanent change. You are still System-blind. You will be System-blind for the rest of your life. The immunity is structural.*

"Good." He meant it. The minutes he'd spent seeing the interface — the light, the labels, the relentless classification of everything — had been useful and specific and he didn't want them back. The world was legible enough without annotations. More legible, actually. The interface was a layer of explanation laid over a reality that didn't require explaining, just maintaining.

He rolled onto his back. The strip of autumn sky between the rock walls had gone from grey to the pale gold that came before evening. The air was cooler. The contamination smell had eased from overwhelming to merely strong — the background stink of old, structural decay rather than the active hemorrhage of before.

*You could have broken it. You were at the fracture and you could have pushed instead of sealing. You know that.*

"Yeah."

*The containment would have failed locally. The wild space behind the wall would have entered through the breach and the taxonomy — the classes, the quests, the bounties, the entire architecture — would have become incoherent in the affected area. The System would have had to restructure or collapse. I cannot predict which.*

"Yeah."

*Why didn't you?*

Maren turned his head and spat again. Less pink this time. The bowl was within reach — he stretched his hand and picked it up, felt the weight of it, the familiar ceramic cool against his shaking fingers. A cheap bowl from a market stall in Fenhold. Eleven years of fake rituals. A few days of real ones. The crack in the glaze ran from the rim to the base like a scar.

"Because someone has to do the maintenance."

*That is — yes. But you had the option to end it. To dismantle the containment structure entirely and—*

"And let everything behind it flood through?" He sat up. It cost him — his abs screamed, his ribs ached, even the bones of his face ached from the hum — but he sat up because lying in a puddle of quest-light while having the most important conversation of his life felt wrong, even by his standards. "I felt it, Aldric. What's behind the wall. It's not evil. I know it's not. But it's — everything. Everything the System sorted and labeled and organized into categories, all of it, but without the sorting. You want that?"

He waited. The paladin didn't answer.

"What's behind that wall isn't a monster. It's the world without the filing system. And the filing system is shit — I know it is, you know it is. It sent heroes to kill me. It killed sin-eaters at the Crook to keep its categories intact. It ate its own people. But the alternative isn't—"

He stopped. Ran his hand over his face. His palm came away damp with quest-light residue and something else, salt-water, though he didn't remember crying and didn't particularly want to examine when it had started.

"The alternative isn't nothing. It's everything at once, without any way to manage it. And people can't — people need the categories. Even wrong ones. Even categories maintained by a system that's desperate and failing and sometimes cruel. They need to see threats and quests and bounties because without that they're just—" He gestured at himself. At his soaked clothes, his shaking hands, his blood-tinged spit on the stone. "They're just walking around blind. Trying to figure it out from the ground level."

*Like you have been doing.*

"Like I've been doing for twenty-eight years. And I'm qualified to say it's not something you want for everyone." He picked up the bowl again. Set it upright on the stone beside him. Quest-light immediately began seeping into it, a thin trickle, patient and slow. "Someone has to do this. The wall's failing. It's going to keep failing. There are cracks everywhere — you said you can see them. Dozens. And the System's regular sin-eaters can't reach the structural ones because they're written into the wall themselves. They can patch the surface but they can't get to where it's actually breaking. I can. Because the System can't write on me. Because I'm—"

He didn't finish the sentence. He didn't need to. The understanding sat between them, in the space that used to hold three voices and now held two.

"I'm not choosing this because it's heroic," he said. "I'm choosing it because it's the job. The real one. Not the fake one I did for eleven years, not the heroic version you'd pick if you still had a body and a sword. The actual job, which is keeping a wall together that holds the world in one piece, and which nobody's going to see or thank me for, and which has to be done by someone who can't see the wall because seeing it makes you part of it."

He was aware that he was explaining this out loud, to the inside of his own skull, sitting in a pool of sick quest-light in a valley that smelled like old rust, with blood on his teeth and no audience. He was aware that this was not how the stories framed these moments. In the quests the System generated — the hero narratives and the legendary-grade completions and the damage rankings that scrolled across tavern walls in Harrow — choices like this happened in throne rooms or on battlefields, with witnesses, with fanfare. Not in a hole in the ground with your trousers soaked and your body wrecked.

But Maren had never had the fanfare. He'd had the dirt, and the bowl, and the work.

*You are choosing to be a maintenance worker.*

"I'm choosing to be a maintenance worker who knows what he's maintaining. First one, apparently."

A long silence from Aldric. Not scared. Not obedient. A different kind — something that, if the paladin had still been alive and still had a face, might have looked like the moment a person revises a judgment they've held for a very long time and feels the framework shift under them.

*There are other cracks. I can see them from here — from inside the interface, the fracture points are visible when I know what to look for. Dozens. Some are minor. The kind that a trained sin-eater could manage if they understood what they were actually maintaining. But some are structural. Deep. The kind that only someone like you could reach.*

"How many of the bad ones?"

*More than one person can address in a lifetime. But you would be the first to try while knowing what the work actually is.*

Maren leaned back against the rock wall. The stone was cold against his spine, contamination-stained dark, and above him the strip of sky was turning gold at the western edge. He could hear the Woundmouth behind him — a low, wet sound, like breathing through a damaged chest. He could smell the old contamination, the layered decay, and underneath it the clean cold of autumn air pushing in from above. His body was a catalogue of damage: raw throat, bruised ribs, the ache in his hands where they'd locked against the stone, the deep fatigue of muscles that had been used beyond their capacity.

He was alive. Barely, with the kind of margin that didn't inspire confidence, but alive.

"Alright," he said.

*Alright.*

"You tell me where the cracks are. I go seal them. You make your objections along the way." He paused. "You're going to have objections."

*I expect so. My objections have not historically been well received.*

"They've been received fine. I just don't always do what you want."

*That has been — yes. That has been evident.*

He picked up his bowl. The ceramic was warm from the quest-light that had seeped into it, the cracked glaze catching the last of the daylight. He held it in both hands, the way he'd held it a thousand times before — the same bowl, the same posture, the same man kneeling at the edge of something he was supposed to clean up. But the weight of it sat differently in his hands now. Not heavier. Truer.

"So. Which way?"

*Southwest. Three days' walk. There is a fracture near a settlement — the name is not fully legible on the interface. The area has been flagged for contamination clearance for six months and no sin-eater has responded.*

"Course not. Nobody wants this job."

*You do, apparently.*

Maren stood up. His legs held. There was a wobble — a bad one, the kind he'd deny later if anyone ever asked — but they held. He tucked the bowl against his hip, tied it with the leather strip, felt it settle into its familiar weight. Quest-light dripped from his clothes and hair, pooling at his feet, running back toward the Woundmouth as if the wound wanted it back.

The hum was still there. Quieter, steadier, but present. It would always be present. The wall would always be leaking, and the work would never be finished, and nobody would build a quest around it or put his name on a board in a tavern or assign a class to what he did.

He turned southwest and started walking.


# Chapter 18

The hum was quieter on this side of the valley.

Not gone — the hum was never gone, not since Fenhold, not since the first morning he could remember hearing it — but stretched thin, like water across too much stone. Three days past the Woundmouth and Maren's body still hadn't forgiven him. His knees ground when he bent them. The muscles along his ribs ached in a deep, wrung-out way, as if someone had reached inside his trunk and twisted everything a quarter turn to the left. But his hands were steady. That was new.

The road — what passed for a road this deep, where dungeon territory gave way to farmland and the path widened from packed dirt to actual ruts — had scorch marks in the earth. Old ones, layered dark over darker, from heroes who'd passed through seasons ago. Nobody cleaned them. Nobody ever cleaned them. Early autumn had crept in while he wasn't paying attention, and the light hit those burn marks differently now, cooler, less shimmer and more of a pale, flat quality that made the road look tired.

*The contamination index has dropped significantly since your... intervention,* Aldric said. He paused on "intervention" the way he used to pause on "incompetent" — with a precision that suggested he'd considered and rejected several worse options. *The residual levels in this stretch are within normal seasonal parameters.*

"So it worked."

*I believe I just said that.*

"You said 'contamination index' and 'seasonal parameters.' I said 'it worked.'"

A pause. Then, quieter: *Yes. It worked.*

That was the new thing. The pauses. Aldric had always been careful with language — fourteen years of paladin training had given him the syntax of reports and prayers, every sentence a finished architecture — but the pauses were different now. Before the Woundmouth, his silences had been dramatic. The silence of a man who had seen something terrible and was deciding whether to describe it. Now they were just pauses. The kind where someone was sorting through what they actually wanted to say, rather than what they should say.

Maren missed the dramatic silences, honestly. They'd been easier to ignore.

The early-autumn air sat in the back of his throat with a crispness that wasn't unpleasant — just present, like a taste he couldn't quite place. The fields on either side of the road had been harvested recently. Cut stubble scratched at his ankles where the road narrowed, and the smell was turned earth and drying grain and, underneath it, the faint sweet-ozone note of contamination residue that had been sitting too long in the soil.

*There's quest-light pooling in the drainage channel to your right,* Aldric said. *Several weeks of accumulation. No active quest attached — just residue that hasn't been cleared.*

Maren hadn't asked. A month ago, Aldric wouldn't have offered. The interface readings had been transactional — Maren requesting, Aldric delivering with whatever degree of moral disdain the question warranted. Now the descriptions came unprompted. Not constantly. Not like the frantic narration of those first days in Fenhold, when everything had been new and the paladin had been processing his own horror by cataloguing the world around them. This was different. Someone pointing out a bird because they'd both noticed the tree.

It was harder to be annoyed by it when it wasn't transactional. Maren was working on that.

The road crested a low hill and the valley opened up — farmland, a scatter of buildings in the middle distance, the dark mouth of a dungeon visible on the far ridgeline like a bruise against the tree line. Quest-light pooled around it, brighter than the residual traces in the ditches, and even from this distance the hum thickened. Someone would need to clear it eventually.

Maybe him.

The thought still had a quality of absurdity to it. Like wearing shoes that fit but belonged to someone else.

*Your stride has improved,* Aldric noted. *Less compensatory tilting. The muscular damage from the consumption appears to be resolving.*

"You could just say I'm walking better."

*I could. I won't, but I could.*

That was a joke. A gentle one. Before the Woundmouth, Aldric's humor had been a blade — precise cuts aimed at Maren's competence, his hygiene, his moral architecture. The sarcasm of a man who'd been stripped of everything except his ability to judge. Now the edge had been filed down to something that didn't draw blood every time.

Maren sat on a flat stone at the roadside to eat. The bread was stale — two days old, bought from a farming woman who'd sold it to him without looking at his face, which was either generosity or indifference, and Maren had been grateful for either. His jaw worked at the crust. Crumbs fell on his shirt and he brushed them into the dirt.

Derry would have had better bread. Derry always had better bread — baked by Lenna, or bought fresh because Derry couldn't walk past a baker's stall without stopping, the same way he couldn't pass a cow without commenting on its quality. A small, specific failure of impulse control that Maren had found endlessly funny for eleven years.

The thought arrived and sat there, like the ache in his ribs. Not sharp anymore. Not even unpleasant, exactly. Just present.

He'd worked out the shape of it during the three days since the Woundmouth, the way you work out a bruise — pressing on it, testing the edges, seeing how much weight it could take before the pain changed quality. Derry had told Caul their direction. Not their location, not their camp, not anything that would have gotten Maren killed immediately — just the direction. Toward the Crook. Enough for Caul to narrow the search. Enough for people to die.

But also: Derry had a daughter who was learning to count, and a wife who'd planted tomatoes for the first time, and a cow that kept pushing through the fence. He'd talked about all of it, across campfires and long roads, in the specific voice of a man trying to hold the world steady by describing its ordinary parts. And then he'd done the math that Maren couldn't argue with, the one where his daughter needed a father more than Maren needed a friend.

The math was right. Maren could see it clearly — he'd always been good at seeing what other people were feeling, it was the foundation skill of his entire fraudulent career — and the math was right.

He still couldn't forgive it. Maybe later. Maybe not.

He finished the bread, brushed his hands on his trousers, and stood up. His knees complained. The road went on.

* * *

Thessa was sitting on a low wall at the edge of a settlement Maren hadn't been able to see from the road. The village was small — a handful of stone buildings clustered around a well, a livestock pen, the low walls that marked property in this part of the country. She was eating an apple. Her hands, stained dark from thirty years of ritual contamination exposure, made the fruit look very white.

"You're alive," she said. Not a question. An assessment.

"Apparently."

She looked at him the way she always looked at him — with the specific evaluative attention of someone checking a structure for cracks. Her eyes tracked from his face to his hands to his stance.

"Your feet are wrong."

"My feet."

"You're favoring your left side. The Woundmouth consumption pulled everything right — the asymmetry's contamination-patterned. Correct it or you'll be limping inside a week."

"Thanks."

"It wasn't a compliment." She took another bite of her apple. The crunch was very loud in the morning quiet. "There's a farmstead twelve miles east. Settlement called Barren Rill. Contamination pool behind the main house — it's been building three seasons because their sin-eater died and nobody replaced her. They've got children."

She said it the way she said everything — like a report, like information with an obvious response, like the next item on a list that had always been too long for one person.

"Twelve miles," Maren said.

"Twelve miles." She finished the apple and tossed the core over the wall into someone's garden. "Contamination's mid-grade. Standard clearing. Within your capability, assuming you don't botch the approach sequence."

"I might botch the approach sequence."

"Then you'll do it again." She stood up, wiping her hands on her trousers. The apple juice left a streak on the dark fabric. "There are three other sites on the line between here and Silt. I'll take the two nearest. You take Barren Rill and the one past it." She paused, calculating. "Meet me in Silt in ten days. If you're not dead."

*She's not the most encouraging teacher I've encountered,* Aldric said. *The Order of the Veil had a man who once told me my prayer posture made him question whether I had a spine. He was gentler.*

Maren didn't answer him. He was looking toward Thessa — or looking in her direction, which was the best he could manage, orienting by the sound of her breathing and the particular scrape of her worn leather bag against her hip.

"So that's it," he said.

"What were you expecting? A ceremony?" She slung the bag over her shoulder. "You're a sin-eater. There's contamination. Go eat it."

She left the way she did everything — without looking back, without checking whether the instruction had landed, with the absolute confidence of a woman who had been doing this work alone for longer than Maren had been alive and had stopped waiting for anyone to catch up.

*That was an acknowledgment,* Aldric said.

"That was a job assignment."

*From her, those are the same thing.*

He was right. Maren stood on the road with the low autumn sun hitting the side of his face and the hum threading through the air and his knees aching and stale bread sitting heavy in his gut, and he understood that Thessa had just treated him as a colleague. Not a student. Not a threat. Not a fraud who'd collected fees for eleven years while contamination built up in every village he'd visited. A colleague — someone she could split the work with, someone she trusted to take a site and clear it and show up in Silt in ten days.

It wasn't warm. It wasn't generous. It was better than both of those things. It was practical.

"Twelve miles east," he said to nobody in particular.

*Twelve miles east,* Aldric confirmed. *I'll have the route for you.*

He started walking.

* * *

Barren Rill was exactly what it sounded like — a thin settlement strung along a creek bed that had mostly dried to mud, fed by runoff from the hills where a dungeon mouth sat, old and leaking quest-light into the groundwater. The contamination pool behind the farmstead's main house was visible even to Maren: a faint shimmer in the packed earth, like heat haze that had gotten lost and settled down to stay.

The farmer's name was Reth. Stocky man, weather-beaten, with the cautious courtesy of someone who'd been told a sin-eater was coming and wasn't sure whether to be grateful or afraid. He had calloused hands and a habit of rubbing the back of his neck when he talked, as if the words had to be physically pushed out.
